The previous section established that the three generations arise from the common family Hilbert space
\[
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{fam}}
=
H^0(\mathbb{CP}^2,\mathcal O(1))
\cong \mathbb C^3,
\]
and that the mass hierarchy is generated by BCC-induced anisotropy lifting together with exponentially small overlap amplitudes.

The next task is to explain flavor mixing, namely:

\[
V_{\mathrm{CKM}}
=
U_u^\dagger U_d,
\qquad
U_{\mathrm{PMNS}}
=
U_e^\dagger U_\nu.
\]

We shall show that mixing arises whenever different fermion sectors are governed by different effective mass operators on the same family Hilbert space.

\subsection{Common family space and sector-dependent mass operators}

All chiral matter fields carry the same generation space
\[
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{fam}}\cong \mathbb C^3.
\]
However, there is no reason that the effective mass operators for the different sectors coincide.

For each sector
\[
f\in\{u,d,e,\nu\},
\]
let the effective Hermitian mass seed operator acting on \(\mathcal H_{\mathrm{fam}}\) be
\[
\boxed{
H_f
=
m_{0,f}\,\mathbf 1_3
+
\lambda_f\,T^{(1)}(V_f)
+
\Delta_f^{\mathrm{np}}.
}
\]
Here:

\begin{itemize}
\item \(m_{0,f}\mathbf 1_3\) is the sector-dependent degenerate seed,
\item \(T^{(1)}(V_f)\) is the Berezin--Toeplitz quantization of a real anisotropy potential \(V_f\) on \(\mathbb{CP}^2\),
\item \(\Delta_f^{\mathrm{np}}\) is the nonperturbative overlap/tunneling correction.
\end{itemize}

Because the background couplings to the defect sector need not be identical for all fermion species, the operators \(H_u,H_d,H_e,H_\nu\) are generically distinct.

\subsection{Mass basis versus generation basis}

Fix a reference orthonormal basis of the common family space:
\[
\mathcal B_{\mathrm{gen}}=\{g_1,g_2,g_3\}.
\]
This is the natural generation basis obtained from the geometric quantization of the \(\mathbb{CP}^2\) defect moduli.

For each fermion sector \(f\), let
\[
U_f^\dagger H_f U_f = \operatorname{diag}(m_{f1},m_{f2},m_{f3}),
\]
with
\[
U_f\in U(3).
\]
Then the columns of \(U_f\) define the mass eigenbasis for that sector.

Thus, even though all sectors live in the same generation Hilbert space, the physical mass eigenstates are sector-dependent rotated bases.

The mismatch between two such bases produces flavor mixing.

\subsection{Derivation of the CKM matrix}

The charged-current weak interaction couples left-handed up-type and down-type quarks in the common weak doublet structure.
Let
\[
u_L^{(\mathrm{gen})},\quad d_L^{(\mathrm{gen})}
\]
denote the quark fields in the generation basis.
The weak current is
\[
J^\mu_{W,q}
=
\overline{u}_L^{(\mathrm{gen})}\gamma^\mu d_L^{(\mathrm{gen})}.
\]

Now define the mass eigenfields by
\[
u_L^{(\mathrm{gen})}=U_u\,u_L^{(\mathrm{mass})},
\qquad
d_L^{(\mathrm{gen})}=U_d\,d_L^{(\mathrm{mass})}.
\]
Then
\[
J^\mu_{W,q}
=
\overline{u}_L^{(\mathrm{mass})}
U_u^\dagger \gamma^\mu U_d
d_L^{(\mathrm{mass})}.
\]
Since the \(U_f\) act only in generation space, they commute with \(\gamma^\mu\), hence
\[
J^\mu_{W,q}
=
\overline{u}_L^{(\mathrm{mass})}\gamma^\mu
\left(U_u^\dagger U_d\right)
d_L^{(\mathrm{mass})}.
\]

Therefore the Cabibbo--Kobayashi--Maskawa matrix is
\[
\boxed{
V_{\mathrm{CKM}} = U_u^\dagger U_d.
}
\]

\subsection{Derivation of the PMNS matrix}

Similarly, for leptons the charged-current weak interaction couples left-handed charged leptons and neutrinos.
Let
\[
e_L^{(\mathrm{gen})}=U_e\,e_L^{(\mathrm{mass})},
\qquad
\nu_L^{(\mathrm{gen})}=U_\nu\,\nu_L^{(\mathrm{mass})}.
\]
Then the lepton weak current becomes
\[
J^\mu_{W,\ell}
=
\overline{e}_L^{(\mathrm{mass})}\gamma^\mu
\left(U_e^\dagger U_\nu\right)
\nu_L^{(\mathrm{mass})}.
\]
Thus
\[
\boxed{
U_{\mathrm{PMNS}} = U_e^\dagger U_\nu.
}
\]

Hence both quark and lepton mixing arise from the same structural principle:
mixing is the relative unitary rotation between the mass eigenbases of two sectors that share the same weak interaction basis.

\subsection{Off-diagonal overlaps as the microscopic origin}

We now connect the unitary matrices \(U_f\) to the internal defect geometry.

Let
\[
\{\phi_1,\phi_2,\phi_3\}
\]
be a semiclassical localized basis of family states in \(\mathcal H_{\mathrm{fam}}\), each concentrated near one anisotropy-selected region of \(\mathbb{CP}^2\).
The effective mass matrix for sector \(f\) is
\[
(M_f)_{ij}
=
\langle \phi_i, \mathcal Y_f \phi_j\rangle,
\]
where \(\mathcal Y_f\) is the effective internal Yukawa operator of that sector.

In the adiabatic regime,
\[
(M_f)_{ii}\sim A_{f,i}e^{-S_{f,i}},
\]
while the off-diagonal entries are overlap amplitudes
\[
(M_f)_{ij}\sim \epsilon^{(f)}_{ij},
\qquad
i\neq j.
\]
Therefore a canonical form is
\[
\boxed{
M_f
\approx
\begin{pmatrix}
A_{f,1}e^{-S_{f,1}} & \epsilon^{(f)}_{12} & \epsilon^{(f)}_{13}\\
\epsilon^{(f)}_{12} & A_{f,2}e^{-S_{f,2}} & \epsilon^{(f)}_{23}\\
\epsilon^{(f)}_{13} & \epsilon^{(f)}_{23} & A_{f,3}e^{-S_{f,3}}
\end{pmatrix}.
}
\]
Diagonalizing this matrix gives the sector-specific unitary \(U_f\).

Thus the microscopic origin of CKM/PMNS mixing is the fact that the overlap matrices \(\epsilon^{(f)}_{ij}\) are not simultaneously diagonal across all fermion sectors.

\subsection{Perturbative mixing angles}

Assume for simplicity that the hierarchy is strong:
\[
m_{f1}\ll m_{f2}\ll m_{f3},
\]
and that off-diagonal terms are small compared to level splittings.
Then first-order perturbation theory gives the mixing angles approximately as
\[
\theta^{(f)}_{ij}
\sim
\frac{\epsilon^{(f)}_{ij}}{m_{fj}-m_{fi}},
\qquad i<j.
\]
Therefore the observable quark mixing angles are controlled by differences
\[
\theta^{\mathrm{CKM}}_{ij}
\sim
\theta^{(d)}_{ij}-\theta^{(u)}_{ij},
\]
while the lepton mixing angles are
\[
\theta^{\mathrm{PMNS}}_{ij}
\sim
\theta^{(\nu)}_{ij}-\theta^{(e)}_{ij}.
\]

Hence small or large observable mixing depends not only on the size of a single overlap, but on the relative structure of the corresponding sectoral mass matrices.

\subsection{Why CKM is naturally small}

The quark sector can naturally yield small mixing if the up-type and down-type anisotropy operators are almost aligned.
Mathematically, this means
\[
[H_u,H_d]\approx 0,
\]
or equivalently that the corresponding eigenbases are nearly the same.

In that case
\[
U_u \approx U_d,
\]
and therefore
\[
V_{\mathrm{CKM}} = U_u^\dagger U_d \approx \mathbf 1_3.
\]

More quantitatively, if the quark overlap operators are both controlled by the same BCC-selected minima and differ only weakly in their Higgs-coupling weights, then
\[
|\epsilon^{(u)}_{ij}-\epsilon^{(d)}_{ij}| \ll |m_{fj}-m_{fi}|,
\]
leading to small mixing angles.

Thus a nearly aligned anisotropy structure explains the smallness of CKM mixing.

\subsection{Why PMNS can be large}

The lepton sector can naturally yield large mixing if the neutrino mass operator is generated differently from the charged-lepton operator.

For instance, charged leptons may inherit the same strongly localized hierarchy mechanism as the quarks, with nearly diagonal mass matrix,
while neutrinos may arise from a more delocalized overlap mechanism or a Majorana-type seesaw operator with a different internal kernel.

Then
\[
[H_e,H_\nu]
\]
need not be small, and the corresponding eigenbases can be strongly misaligned:
\[
U_e \not\approx U_\nu.
\]
Therefore
\[
U_{\mathrm{PMNS}} = U_e^\dagger U_\nu
\]
can contain order-one angles.

This explains how the same underlying family space can simultaneously produce:

\[
\boxed{
|V_{\mathrm{CKM}}-\mathbf 1|\ll 1,
\qquad
|U_{\mathrm{PMNS}}|\ \text{contains large angles}.
}
\]

\subsection{Inclusion of CP phases}

If the overlap amplitudes \(\epsilon^{(f)}_{ij}\) are complex, then the mass matrices are Hermitian or, more generally, complex matrices after left-right coupling.
In that case the diagonalizing transformations contain physical phases.

For quarks:
\[
V_{\mathrm{CKM}} = U_u^\dagger U_d
\]
contains one physical Dirac CP phase after rephasing.

For leptons:
\[
U_{\mathrm{PMNS}} = U_e^\dagger U_\nu
\]
contains one Dirac phase and, if neutrinos are Majorana, two additional Majorana phases.

In the present framework, these phases originate from complex Berry/WZW overlap amplitudes and complex tunneling paths in the defect moduli geometry.

\subsection{A unified effective flavor Lagrangian}

Projecting all fermions onto the family Hilbert space yields the effective flavor Lagrangian
\[
\mathcal L_{\mathrm{flavor}}
=
\overline{u}_L M_u u_R
+
\overline{d}_L M_d d_R
+
\overline{e}_L M_e e_R
+
\overline{\nu}_L M_\nu \nu_R
+\mathrm{h.c.},
\]
or, for Majorana neutrinos,
\[
\mathcal L_{\nu}
=
\frac12\,\nu_L^T C M_\nu \nu_L+\mathrm{h.c.}
\]

The matrices \(M_f\) all act on the same three-dimensional family space,
but are generated by different sectoral defect couplings.
Their relative misalignment is the complete microscopic source of flavor mixing.

\subsection{Structural theorem}

We may now summarize the mixing mechanism.

\paragraph{Theorem.}
Let the common family Hilbert space of the minimally quantized TECT defect be
\[
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{fam}} = H^0(\mathbb{CP}^2,\mathcal O(1))\cong \mathbb C^3.
\]
Assume that each fermion sector
\[
f\in\{u,d,e,\nu\}
\]
is governed by an effective mass operator
\[
H_f = m_{0,f}\mathbf 1_3 + \lambda_f T^{(1)}(V_f)+\Delta_f^{\mathrm{np}},
\]
with distinct anisotropy/overlap data.
Let
\[
U_f^\dagger H_f U_f = \operatorname{diag}(m_{f1},m_{f2},m_{f3}).
\]
Then:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the quark mixing matrix is
\[
\boxed{
V_{\mathrm{CKM}} = U_u^\dagger U_d,
}
\]
\item the lepton mixing matrix is
\[
\boxed{
U_{\mathrm{PMNS}} = U_e^\dagger U_\nu.
}
\]
\end{enumerate}

If the up-type and down-type operators are nearly aligned, then \(V_{\mathrm{CKM}}\) is close to the identity.
If the charged-lepton and neutrino operators are strongly misaligned, then \(U_{\mathrm{PMNS}}\) can contain large angles.

Thus flavor mixing is the observable remnant of sector-dependent lifting of the same quantized \(\mathbb{CP}^2\) family space.

\subsection{What is proved and what remains open}

What is established:

\begin{enumerate}
\item CKM and PMNS arise automatically once different fermion sectors have different mass operators on the same family Hilbert space,
\item off-diagonal overlaps provide the microscopic origin of the mixing matrices,
\item the simultaneous coexistence of small CKM and large PMNS is naturally explained by near-alignment in the quark sector and strong misalignment in the lepton sector.
\end{enumerate}

What remains open:

\begin{enumerate}
\item deriving the exact microscopic sectoral operators \(V_f\) and \(\Delta_f^{\mathrm{np}}\) from the TECT defect PDE,
\item computing the observed numerical values of the CKM and PMNS angles,
\item deciding whether the neutrino sector is Dirac or Majorana from the defect bundle geometry.
\end{enumerate}

\section{Final summary}

All fermion sectors share the same generation Hilbert space
\[
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{fam}}
=
H^0(\mathbb{CP}^2,\mathcal O(1))
\cong \mathbb C^3.
\]
However, each sector
\[
f\in\{u,d,e,\nu\}
\]
has its own effective mass operator
\[
H_f
=
m_{0,f}\mathbf 1_3
+
\lambda_f T^{(1)}(V_f)
+
\Delta_f^{\mathrm{np}}.
\]
Let
\[
U_f^\dagger H_f U_f
=
\operatorname{diag}(m_{f1},m_{f2},m_{f3}).
\]
Then the observable flavor-mixing matrices are
\[
\boxed{
V_{\mathrm{CKM}}=U_u^\dagger U_d,
\qquad
U_{\mathrm{PMNS}}=U_e^\dagger U_\nu.
}
\]

Microscopically, the entries of the mass matrices are overlap amplitudes of localized family states:
\[
(M_f)_{ii}\sim A_{f,i}e^{-S_{f,i}},
\qquad
(M_f)_{ij}\sim \epsilon^{(f)}_{ij}\quad (i\neq j).
\]
Hence mixing is produced by off-diagonal overlaps and by the fact that the different sectoral mass operators are not simultaneously diagonalizable.

Small CKM mixing corresponds to
\[
U_u\approx U_d,
\]
that is, near alignment of the up and down mass operators.
Large PMNS mixing corresponds to
\[
U_e\not\approx U_\nu,
\]
that is, strong misalignment between the charged-lepton and neutrino sectors.

Therefore the flavor structure is:

\[
\text{common } \mathbb{CP}^2 \text{ family space}
\;\Longrightarrow\;
\text{sector-dependent mass operators}
\;\Longrightarrow\;
\text{basis misalignment}
\;\Longrightarrow\;
\text{CKM/PMNS mixing}.
\]

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Dirac versus Majorana Neutrinos from Defect Bundle Geometry in TECT}

\subsection{Goal}

We now determine whether the neutrino sector emerging from the TECT defect geometry is naturally Dirac or Majorana.

The question is structural:

\begin{quote}
Given the chiral defect bundle
\[
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{tot}}
\cong
H^0(\mathbb{CP}^2,\mathcal O(1))
\otimes
\left(\overline V\oplus \Lambda^2V\right),
\]
what type of neutrino mass term is geometrically allowed?
\end{quote}

The answer depends on whether the defect spectrum contains only the minimal Standard-Model-type chiral family bundle, or whether it also contains a neutral sterile mode together with a lepton-number-violating pairing channel.

\subsection{The left-handed neutrino in the family bundle}

From the previously derived family bundle
\[
\mathcal F
=
(\mathbf 3,\mathbf 2)_{1/6}
\oplus
(\mathbf 1,\mathbf 2)_{-1/2}
\oplus
(\overline{\mathbf 3},\mathbf 1)_{-2/3}
\oplus
(\overline{\mathbf 3},\mathbf 1)_{1/3}
\oplus
(\mathbf 1,\mathbf 1)_{1},
\]
the lepton doublet is
\[
L = (\mathbf 1,\mathbf 2)_{-1/2}.
\]
Its upper component is the left-handed neutrino
\[
\nu_L \in (\mathbf 1,\mathbf 1)_0
\]
after electroweak symmetry breaking.

Thus the existence of \(\nu_L\) is automatic in the minimal defect family.

\subsection{Dirac mass: required structure}

A Dirac mass term pairs a left-handed neutrino with an independent right-handed mode:
\[
\mathcal L_D = - y_\nu \,\overline{L}\,\widetilde H\,\nu_R + \mathrm{h.c.},
\]
where
\[
\widetilde H = i\sigma_2 H^*,
\qquad
\nu_R \sim (\mathbf 1,\mathbf 1)_0.
\]

This term is gauge invariant because
\[
(\mathbf 1,\mathbf 2)_{-1/2}
\otimes
(\mathbf 1,\mathbf 2)_{-1/2}
\supset
(\mathbf 1,\mathbf 1)_{-1},
\]
and with the standard conjugation convention for \(\overline L\widetilde H\) the total hypercharge vanishes when multiplied by \(\nu_R\).

Therefore Dirac neutrino masses are possible if the defect spectrum contains an additional sterile zero mode
\[
\nu_R \in (\mathbf 1,\mathbf 1)_0.
\]

\subsection{Majorana mass: required structure}

A Majorana mass term identifies a neutral fermion with its own charge conjugate.

There are two possibilities:

\paragraph{(i) Left-handed Majorana (Weinberg operator).}
At dimension five one has
\[
\mathcal L_W
=
- \frac{c_{ij}}{\Lambda}
(L_i H)(L_j H) + \mathrm{h.c.}
\]
This induces, after symmetry breaking,
\[
m_\nu^{(L)} \sim \frac{v^2}{\Lambda}.
\]

\paragraph{(ii) Right-handed Majorana.}
If a sterile mode \(\nu_R\sim (\mathbf 1,\mathbf 1)_0\) exists, then a gauge-invariant bare Majorana term is allowed:
\[
\mathcal L_R
=
-\frac12 M_R \,\nu_R^T C \nu_R + \mathrm{h.c.}
\]

This term is possible because \(\nu_R\) is a gauge singlet.

Hence Majorana masses require not merely a chiral family bundle, but either:

\begin{enumerate}
\item an effective \(\Delta L = 2\) operator in the low-energy theory, or
\item a gauge-singlet sterile mode with a self-pairing channel.
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{Bundle-theoretic criterion}

The geometric distinction may be formulated as follows.

A Dirac mass requires a pairing between two independent bundles:
\[
\nu_L \in \Gamma(\mathcal E_L),
\qquad
\nu_R \in \Gamma(\mathcal E_R),
\]
with a nondegenerate bilinear map
\[
\mathcal E_L \otimes \mathcal E_R \to \mathcal O.
\]

A Majorana mass requires instead a symmetric or antisymmetric self-pairing of a neutral bundle:
\[
\mathcal E \otimes \mathcal E \to \mathcal O.
\]

Thus:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Dirac} \Longleftrightarrow
\text{pairing between distinct left/right neutral bundles}
}
\]

\[
\boxed{
\text{Majorana} \Longleftrightarrow
\text{self-pairing of a neutral bundle is allowed}
}
\]

\subsection{What the minimal TECT family provides}

The minimal family bundle
\[
\overline V \oplus \Lambda^2V
\]
contains the left-handed lepton doublet \(L\), but it does \emph{not} automatically contain an independent neutral singlet \((\mathbf 1,\mathbf 1)_0\).

Therefore the minimal family bundle alone gives:

\begin{enumerate}
\item a left-handed neutrino \(\nu_L\),
\item no automatic right-handed sterile partner,
\item no automatic renormalizable Majorana self-pairing channel.
\end{enumerate}

Hence, at the level of the minimal defect construction, neutrino masses are \emph{not yet forced}.

However, once one adds the mildest neutral extension --- a sterile defect mode
\[
N \sim (\mathbf 1,\mathbf 1)_0,
\]
two possibilities open:

\begin{itemize}
\item Dirac neutrino if only the Yukawa pairing \(\overline L \widetilde H N\) is allowed,
\item Majorana/seesaw neutrino if in addition \(N^T C N\) is allowed.
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Defect interpretation of the sterile mode}

Within the TECT picture, the sterile mode is naturally interpreted as a defect excitation that:

\begin{enumerate}
\item carries generation index,
\item is neutral under the \(S(U(3)\times U(2))\) gauge splitting,
\item lives in an auxiliary singlet sector of the defect kernel rather than in the minimal family bundle.
\end{enumerate}

Thus one may write the full neutral sector as
\[
\mathcal H_{\nu}
=
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{fam}}\otimes
\left(
\mathcal E_{\nu_L}\oplus \mathcal E_N
\right),
\]
where
\[
\mathcal E_{\nu_L}\sim (\mathbf 1,\mathbf 2)_{-1/2},
\qquad
\mathcal E_N\sim (\mathbf 1,\mathbf 1)_0.
\]

Then a Dirac coupling is always possible, whereas a Majorana term depends on whether the singlet bundle \(\mathcal E_N\) admits a self-conjugate pairing compatible with the defect selection rules.

\subsection{Lepton number as an emergent or accidental symmetry}

The decisive physical distinction is whether lepton number \(L\) is exact, accidental, or violated by topology.

\paragraph{Case A: \(L\) exact or effectively exact.}
If the defect sector preserves a global \(U(1)_L\) to all relevant orders, then terms with
\[
\Delta L = 2
\]
are forbidden.
In that case Majorana masses are forbidden, and the neutrino mass must be Dirac:
\[
\boxed{
U(1)_L \text{ preserved } \Longrightarrow \text{Dirac neutrino}.
}
\]

\paragraph{Case B: \(L\) accidental only.}
If \(U(1)_L\) is not fundamental but only an emergent low-energy selection rule, then nonperturbative defect processes may violate it:
\[
\Delta L = 2.
\]
Then the Weinberg operator or sterile Majorana mass becomes allowed:
\[
\boxed{
\Delta L = 2 \text{ allowed } \Longrightarrow \text{Majorana neutrino possible}.
}
\]

In TECT language, this means that Majorana neutrinos are associated with defect pairings that do not conserve the topological lepton-number assignment exactly.

\subsection{Seesaw structure in the defect framework}

Suppose a sterile neutral mode \(N\sim(\mathbf 1,\mathbf 1)_0\) exists.
Then the most general neutral mass Lagrangian is
\[
\mathcal L_{\nu}
=
- \overline{\nu_L} m_D N
-\frac12 N^T C M_R N
+\mathrm{h.c.}
\]
In the basis \((\nu_L, N^c)\), the mass matrix is
\[
\mathcal M_\nu
=
\begin{pmatrix}
0 & m_D\\
m_D^T & M_R
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

If
\[
\|M_R\|\gg \|m_D\|,
\]
then the light neutrino mass matrix is
\[
\boxed{
m_\nu^{\mathrm{light}}
\approx
- m_D M_R^{-1} m_D^T.
}
\]

Thus the smallness of neutrino masses is explained if the sterile defect singlet has a large self-pairing scale.

\subsection{Geometric interpretation of the seesaw scales}

In the TECT defect picture, the two scales have distinct origins:

\begin{itemize}
\item \(m_D\): overlap/Yukawa coupling between the left-handed lepton doublet defect mode and the sterile neutral defect mode,
\item \(M_R\): self-pairing scale of the sterile singlet sector, controlled by a short-distance topological or nonperturbative defect process.
\end{itemize}

Hence
\[
m_D \sim v\,e^{-S_D},
\qquad
M_R \sim M_*\,e^{-S_R}
\]
or, in the simplest case, \(M_R\sim M_*\) if the singlet self-pairing is unsuppressed.

Then
\[
m_\nu^{\mathrm{light}}
\sim
\frac{v^2}{M_*}\,e^{-2S_D+S_R},
\]
which naturally yields much smaller neutrino masses than those of charged leptons and quarks.

\subsection{Why Majorana is not automatic}

Even though a sterile singlet can support a Majorana mass, this is \emph{not} automatic from the minimal chiral family construction.

Indeed:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the minimal family bundle \(\overline V\oplus \Lambda^2V\) does not itself contain \((\mathbf 1,\mathbf 1)_0\),
\item the existence of a sterile mode requires an additional singlet defect kernel,
\item the Majorana self-pairing requires \(\Delta L=2\) to be allowed by the defect selection rules.
\end{enumerate}

Therefore:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Majorana is an additional geometric/topological option, not the minimal default.}
}
\]

\subsection{Why Dirac is the minimal default}

By contrast, if the TECT defect spectrum is extended only minimally so as to include a neutral right-handed partner but preserves an exact or approximate \(U(1)_L\), then the neutrino is naturally Dirac.

This is the least-structured possibility because it needs only:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the standard left-handed lepton doublet already present in the family bundle,
\item one sterile neutral partner \(N\sim(\mathbf 1,\mathbf 1)_0\),
\item no lepton-number-violating self-pairing.
\end{enumerate}

Hence the minimal extension principle favors Dirac neutrinos.

\subsection{Structural theorem}

We may summarize the result as follows.

\paragraph{Theorem.}
Let the minimal TECT defect family bundle be
\[
\mathcal F
=
(\mathbf 3,\mathbf 2)_{1/6}
\oplus
(\mathbf 1,\mathbf 2)_{-1/2}
\oplus
(\overline{\mathbf 3},\mathbf 1)_{-2/3}
\oplus
(\overline{\mathbf 3},\mathbf 1)_{1/3}
\oplus
(\mathbf 1,\mathbf 1)_{1}.
\]
Then:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the left-handed neutrino \(\nu_L\) is present automatically inside \((\mathbf 1,\mathbf 2)_{-1/2}\),
\item no right-handed neutral singlet is forced by the minimal family bundle,
\item no renormalizable Majorana self-pairing is forced by the minimal family geometry.
\end{enumerate}

If the defect spectrum is extended by a neutral singlet mode
\[
N\sim (\mathbf 1,\mathbf 1)_0,
\]
then:

\begin{enumerate}
\item if \(\Delta L=2\) is forbidden, neutrinos are Dirac;
\item if \(\Delta L=2\) is allowed, Majorana masses and the seesaw mechanism become possible.
\end{enumerate}

Therefore
\[
\boxed{
\text{Dirac neutrino is the minimal/default TECT option,}
}
\]
while
\[
\boxed{
\text{Majorana neutrino is a natural extended option once a sterile singlet and } \Delta L=2 \text{ pairing are admitted.}
}
\]

\subsection{Phenomenological consequence}

The framework predicts two qualitatively distinct branches:

\paragraph{Dirac branch.}
\[
M_\nu = m_D,
\qquad
U_{\mathrm{PMNS}} = U_e^\dagger U_\nu,
\]
with no neutrinoless double beta decay.

\paragraph{Majorana/seesaw branch.}
\[
M_\nu^{\mathrm{light}}
\approx
- m_D M_R^{-1} m_D^T,
\]
with possible neutrinoless double beta decay and additional Majorana phases in \(U_{\mathrm{PMNS}}\).

Thus the nature of neutrinos becomes an experimentally testable discriminator of which defect branch is realized.

\subsection{What is proved and what remains open}

What is established:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the minimal TECT family geometry does not by itself force Majorana neutrinos,
\item Dirac neutrinos are the minimal/default possibility,
\item Majorana neutrinos require a sterile neutral defect mode and a \(\Delta L=2\) pairing channel,
\item once these exist, a standard seesaw structure follows naturally.
\end{enumerate}

What remains open:

\begin{enumerate}
\item deriving the sterile singlet mode directly from the defect PDE spectrum,
\item determining whether \(U(1)_L\) is exact, accidental, or topologically violated in the full TECT theory,
\item computing \(m_D\) and \(M_R\) explicitly from the microscopic defect overlap and pairing amplitudes.
\end{enumerate}

\section{Final summary}

In the minimal TECT defect family bundle, the left-handed neutrino is present automatically inside
\[
(\mathbf 1,\mathbf 2)_{-1/2},
\]
but an independent neutral singlet
\[
(\mathbf 1,\mathbf 1)_0
\]
is not forced.

Therefore:

\[
\boxed{
\text{minimal family geometry alone does not force a Majorana mass.}
}
\]

If a sterile neutral defect mode
\[
N\sim (\mathbf 1,\mathbf 1)_0
\]
is added, then a Dirac Yukawa coupling
\[
\overline L \widetilde H N
\]
is possible immediately.

A Majorana mass requires, in addition, a self-pairing channel
\[
N^T C N
\]
or an effective \(\Delta L=2\) Weinberg operator
\[
(LH)(LH)/\Lambda.
\]

Hence the structural alternatives are:

\[
\boxed{
U(1)_L \text{ preserved } \Longrightarrow \text{Dirac neutrino}
}
\]

\[
\boxed{
\Delta L=2 \text{ allowed and sterile singlet exists } \Longrightarrow \text{Majorana/seesaw neutrino}
}
\]

In particular, Dirac neutrinos are the minimal/default TECT option, while Majorana neutrinos arise in a natural but extended branch of the defect spectrum.

%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{RG Proof of Infrared Isotropy in the BCC TECT Background}

\subsection{Goal}

The microscopic BCC condensate preserves only cubic symmetry rather than full spatial \(O(3)\) rotational invariance.
Nevertheless, the low-energy sector of TECT is assumed to exhibit emergent isotropy and, after temporal matching, emergent Lorentz invariance.

The precise question is therefore:

\begin{quote}
Why does the microscopic cubic anisotropy of the BCC condensate not survive as a relevant deformation of the infrared continuum theory?
\end{quote}

The answer is that the cubic anisotropy enters the long-wavelength effective action as an operator which is irrelevant at the isotropic infrared fixed point.

\subsection{Low-energy order parameter and isotropic fixed point}

Let \(\varphi_a(x)\), \(a=1,\dots,N\), denote the low-energy real order-parameter multiplet describing the relevant transverse/orientational sector near the critical shell condensation.
For the purpose of the RG argument, the minimal Euclidean effective action may be written as
\[
S_{\mathrm{iso}}
=
\int d^d x\,
\left[
\frac{Z}{2}\,\partial_i\varphi_a\,\partial_i\varphi_a
+
\frac{r}{2}\,\varphi_a\varphi_a
+
\frac{u}{4!}\,(\varphi_a\varphi_a)^2
\right].
\]
This action is \(O(N)\)-invariant and defines the isotropic Wilson--Fisher fixed point in \(d<4\), or the Gaussian fixed point in \(d=4\).

The microscopic BCC structure does not invalidate this fixed point; rather, it appears as a perturbation around it.

\subsection{Structure of the cubic anisotropy}

For a cubic environment, the leading quartic anisotropy consistent with \(O_h\) but not with full \(O(N)\) is
\[
S_{\mathrm{ani}}
=
\int d^d x\,
\frac{v}{4!}\sum_{a=1}^N \varphi_a^4.
\]
Thus the full action is
\[
S
=
S_{\mathrm{iso}}+S_{\mathrm{ani}}.
\]

Equivalently, one may decompose the quartic interactions into
\[
\frac{u}{4!}(\varphi^2)^2+\frac{v}{4!}\sum_a \varphi_a^4.
\]
The coupling \(u\) preserves \(O(N)\), while \(v\) measures the deviation toward cubic anisotropy.

Infrared isotropy is established if
\[
v(\ell)\to 0
\qquad\text{as}\qquad
\ell\to +\infty.
\]

\subsection{Linearized RG near the isotropic fixed point}

Let \((u_*,v_*)=(u_*,0)\) denote the isotropic fixed point.
Linearizing the RG flow around this point gives
\[
\frac{d}{d\ell}
\begin{pmatrix}
\delta u\\
v
\end{pmatrix}
=
\begin{pmatrix}
\lambda_u & 0\\
0 & y_{\mathrm{ani}}
\end{pmatrix}
\begin{pmatrix}
\delta u\\
v
\end{pmatrix}
+\cdots.
\]
The isotropic fixed point is stable against cubic anisotropy precisely when
\[
\boxed{
y_{\mathrm{ani}}<0.
}
\]

Hence the problem reduces to the RG eigenvalue of the operator
\[
\mathcal O_{\mathrm{ani}}=\sum_a \varphi_a^4-\frac{3}{N+2}(\varphi^2)^2,
\]
which is the traceless quartic spin-4 perturbation breaking \(O(N)\to O_h\).

\subsection{Tree-level scaling and upper critical dimension}

At the Gaussian fixed point in \(d=4\), the engineering dimension of \(\varphi\) is
\[
[\varphi]=\frac{d-2}{2}=1,
\]
so the quartic operators are marginal:
\[
[\varphi^4]=4.
\]
Thus \(u\) and \(v\) are both classically marginal at \(d=4\).

Below \(d=4\), the Wilson--Fisher fixed point controls the RG flow, and the distinction between \(u\) and \(v\) is decided by anomalous dimensions.
Therefore the relevance or irrelevance of cubic anisotropy is a one-loop and higher effect.

\subsection{One-loop beta functions}

For the cubic model, the one-loop beta functions in \(d=4-\epsilon\) are
\[
\beta_u
=
-\epsilon u
+\frac{N+8}{6}u^2
+\frac{N+2}{3}uv
+\frac12 v^2,
\]
\[
\beta_v
=
-\epsilon v
+2uv
+\frac{N+6}{6}v^2.
\]

The isotropic fixed point is at
\[
v_*=0,
\qquad
u_*=\frac{6\epsilon}{N+8}.
\]
Linearizing \(\beta_v\) around this point yields
\[
\beta_v
=
y_{\mathrm{ani}}\,v+\mathcal O(v^2),
\qquad
y_{\mathrm{ani}}
=
-\epsilon+2u_*.
\]
Substituting \(u_*\) gives
\[
y_{\mathrm{ani}}
=
-\epsilon+\frac{12\epsilon}{N+8}
=
\epsilon\left(\frac{4-N}{N+8}\right).
\]

Therefore:

\[
\boxed{
y_{\mathrm{ani}}<0
\iff N>4,
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
y_{\mathrm{ani}}=0
\iff N=4,
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
y_{\mathrm{ani}}>0
\iff N<4.
}
\]

Thus the leading quartic cubic anisotropy is irrelevant whenever the effective infrared field multiplet has
\[
N>4.
\]

\subsection{Interpretation for TECT}

In TECT, the infrared sector relevant for gauge/orientational emergence is not a single scalar with \(N=1\), nor generically a small-component order parameter.
The effective long-wavelength shell/defect sector contains several coupled transverse and internal components.
Hence the natural regime is that the effective \(N_{\mathrm{eff}}\) controlling the isotropic fixed point satisfies
\[
N_{\mathrm{eff}}>4.
\]
Under this condition, the cubic anisotropy coupling flows to zero:
\[
v(\ell)\sim v_0\,e^{y_{\mathrm{ani}}\ell},
\qquad
y_{\mathrm{ani}}<0,
\]
so
\[
v(\ell)\to 0.
\]

Therefore the BCC anisotropy is washed out at long wavelengths.

\subsection{General operator argument beyond one loop}

The above one-loop result can be reformulated more invariantly.
Let \(\mathcal O_{\mathrm{ani}}\) be the leading cubic-symmetry-breaking scalar operator which is singlet under the cubic group but not under the full isotropic group.
At the infrared isotropic fixed point, its scaling dimension is
\[
\Delta_{\mathrm{ani}}.
\]
Then the RG eigenvalue is
\[
y_{\mathrm{ani}}=d-\Delta_{\mathrm{ani}}.
\]
Hence anisotropy is irrelevant if
\[
\boxed{
\Delta_{\mathrm{ani}}>d.
}
\]

This criterion is exact.
The one-loop computation merely provides an explicit approximation to \(\Delta_{\mathrm{ani}}\).

Thus the true structural statement is:

\[
\text{IR isotropy}
\quad\Longleftrightarrow\quad
\Delta_{\mathrm{ani}}>d
\quad\text{at the isotropic fixed point.}
\]

\subsection{Gradient anisotropy and velocity anisotropy}

In the TECT setting one must also consider derivative anisotropies, such as
\[
\delta S_{\nabla}
=
\int d^d x\,
\left[
\kappa_1\sum_i (\partial_i\varphi_i)^2
+
\kappa_2\sum_{i\neq j} (\partial_i\varphi_j)^2
\right].
\]
At leading order, these are quadratic in fields and two-derivative in momenta.
In the isotropic fixed-point theory, they may be decomposed into:

\begin{enumerate}
\item an isotropic kinetic part, absorbed into the common wavefunction factor \(Z\),
\item a traceless spin-2 anisotropy part.
\end{enumerate}

The traceless derivative anisotropy renormalizes as
\[
\frac{d\kappa_{\mathrm{ani}}}{d\ell}=y_{\nabla,\mathrm{ani}}\kappa_{\mathrm{ani}}+\cdots.
\]
If
\[
y_{\nabla,\mathrm{ani}}<0,
\]
then the propagation velocities become isotropic in the infrared.

Operationally, this means that the common emergent light cone is stable if the velocity-anisotropy operator is irrelevant.
This is the derivative-sector analog of the quartic anisotropy analysis.

\subsection{Emergent Lorentz invariance}

Once spatial isotropy is restored in the infrared, one may include the time derivative sector:
\[
S_{\mathrm{dyn}}
=
\int dt\,d^d x
\left[
\frac{Z_t}{2}(\partial_t\varphi)^2
-
\frac{Z_s c_*^2}{2}(\nabla\varphi)^2
+\cdots
\right].
\]
Lorentz invariance emerges if the ratio
\[
\frac{Z_t}{Z_s}
\]
flows to a constant so that time and space derivatives share a common effective cone velocity \(c_*\).

The residual Lorentz-violating corrections appear as higher-dimension operators, for example
\[
\delta S_{\mathrm{LV}}
=
\int dt\,d^d x\,
\left[
\frac{\alpha_1}{\Lambda^2}(\nabla^2\varphi)^2
+
\frac{\alpha_2}{\Lambda^2}\partial_t^4\varphi^2
+\cdots
\right].
\]
Such operators are irrelevant in the infrared, so Lorentz symmetry becomes asymptotically exact at low energy.

Therefore the full mechanism is:

\[
\text{BCC cubic UV}
\Longrightarrow
\text{anisotropic perturbations of isotropic fixed point}
\Longrightarrow
\text{anisotropy couplings irrelevant}
\Longrightarrow
\text{IR isotropy}
\Longrightarrow
\text{emergent Lorentz invariance.}
\]

\subsection{The role of the BCC resonance selection}

The exact BCC resonance selection with invariant
\[
\Lambda_{\mathrm{BCC}}=540
\]
is a UV selection principle.
It fixes the microscopic condensate background and the existence of the BCC phase, but it does not force the IR theory to remain cubically anisotropic.
Instead, it determines the initial conditions
\[
u(\ell=0),\qquad v(\ell=0),\qquad \kappa_{\mathrm{ani}}(\ell=0),
\]
for the RG flow.

If the isotropic fixed point is stable, then these UV-selected anisotropies flow away:
\[
v_0\neq 0,\quad \kappa_{\mathrm{ani},0}\neq 0
\qquad\Longrightarrow\qquad
v(\ell),\kappa_{\mathrm{ani}}(\ell)\to 0.
\]

Thus BCC selection and IR isotropy are not contradictory.
They refer to different scales.

\subsection{Functional RG formulation}

A more nonperturbative formulation uses the effective average action \(\Gamma_k\).
Write the truncation
\[
\Gamma_k[\varphi]
=
\int d^d x
\left[
\frac{Z_k}{2}(\partial_i\varphi_a)^2
+
\frac{Y_k}{2}\,\mathcal O_{\nabla,\mathrm{ani}}
+
U_k(\rho,\tau)
\right],
\]
where
\[
\rho=\frac12\varphi_a\varphi_a,
\qquad
\tau=\sum_a \varphi_a^4-\frac{3}{N+2}(\varphi^2)^2.
\]
Then
\[
U_k(\rho,\tau)=U_k^{\mathrm{iso}}(\rho)+g_{\tau,k}\,\tau+\cdots.
\]
The Wetterich equation implies flow equations of the form
\[
\partial_t g_{\tau,k}
=
y_{\mathrm{ani}}\,g_{\tau,k}
+\mathcal O(g_{\tau,k}^2),
\qquad
t=\ln(k/\Lambda).
\]
Thus the FRG stability criterion is again
\[
y_{\mathrm{ani}}<0.
\]

Hence the perturbative and functional RG pictures agree:
infrared isotropy is equivalent to the negativity of the RG eigenvalue of the leading cubic anisotropy operator.

\subsection{TECT isotropy theorem}

We may summarize the result as follows.

\paragraph{Theorem.}
Let the low-energy TECT transverse/orientational sector be governed by an isotropic infrared fixed point with effective component number \(N_{\mathrm{eff}}\), and let the microscopic BCC structure enter only through cubic-symmetry-breaking perturbations
\[
\mathcal O_{\mathrm{ani}},\qquad \mathcal O_{\nabla,\mathrm{ani}}.
\]
If the corresponding RG eigenvalues satisfy
\[
y_{\mathrm{ani}}<0,
\qquad
y_{\nabla,\mathrm{ani}}<0,
\]
then all cubic anisotropy couplings flow to zero in the infrared:
\[
g_{\mathrm{ani}}(k)\to 0,
\qquad
\kappa_{\mathrm{ani}}(k)\to 0
\qquad
(k\to 0).
\]
Consequently, the infrared effective theory is spatially isotropic.

Moreover, if the residual Lorentz-violating higher-derivative operators are also irrelevant, then the infrared theory exhibits emergent Lorentz invariance.

\paragraph{Corollary.}
At one loop for the quartic cubic model in \(d=4-\epsilon\),
\[
y_{\mathrm{ani}}
=
\epsilon\left(\frac{4-N_{\mathrm{eff}}}{N_{\mathrm{eff}}+8}\right).
\]
Hence the isotropic fixed point is stable whenever
\[
N_{\mathrm{eff}}>4.
\]

\subsection{What is proved and what remains open}

What is established:

\begin{enumerate}
\item microscopic BCC anisotropy can be formulated as perturbations of an isotropic infrared fixed point,
\item infrared isotropy is equivalent to the irrelevance of the leading cubic anisotropy operators,
\item at one loop, quartic cubic anisotropy is irrelevant for \(N_{\mathrm{eff}}>4\),
\item derivative velocity anisotropies are controlled by the same RG logic.
\end{enumerate}

What remains open:

\begin{enumerate}
\item deriving the precise effective component number \(N_{\mathrm{eff}}\) for the full TECT gauge/transverse sector,
\item computing the anisotropy eigenvalues nonperturbatively in the specific BCC shell model,
\item embedding the full defect/gauge system into a concrete FRG truncation and solving the flow numerically.
\end{enumerate}

\section{Final summary}

The microscopic BCC condensate breaks full rotational invariance down to cubic symmetry.
In the infrared, this enters the continuum theory through anisotropy operators such as
\[
\mathcal O_{\mathrm{ani}}
=
\sum_a \varphi_a^4-\frac{3}{N+2}(\varphi^2)^2.
\]
Their RG flow is
\[
\frac{d g_{\mathrm{ani}}}{d\ell}
=
y_{\mathrm{ani}}\,g_{\mathrm{ani}}+\cdots.
\]
Therefore infrared isotropy holds if and only if
\[
\boxed{
y_{\mathrm{ani}}<0.
}
\]

At one loop in \(d=4-\epsilon\),
\[
y_{\mathrm{ani}}
=
\epsilon\left(\frac{4-N_{\mathrm{eff}}}{N_{\mathrm{eff}}+8}\right).
\]
Hence the isotropic fixed point is stable whenever
\[
\boxed{
N_{\mathrm{eff}}>4.
}
\]

The same logic applies to derivative/velocity anisotropies:
if their RG eigenvalues are negative, then the propagation cone becomes isotropic in the infrared.
Residual Lorentz-violating higher-derivative operators are also irrelevant, so the low-energy theory acquires an emergent common light cone.

Thus the full mechanism is
\[
\text{BCC UV anisotropy}
\Longrightarrow
\text{anisotropic perturbations of isotropic fixed point}
\Longrightarrow
\text{irrelevant anisotropy operators}
\Longrightarrow
\text{IR isotropy}
\Longrightarrow
\text{emergent Lorentz invariance.}
\]

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Concrete Effective Component Counting and FRG Truncation for Infrared Isotropy in TECT}

\subsection{Goal}

We now specialize the abstract infrared-isotropy argument to the actual low-energy field content of TECT.

The purpose is fourfold:

\begin{enumerate}
\item identify the physically relevant infrared degrees of freedom in the BCC TECT background,
\item define the effective component number \(N_{\mathrm{eff}}\),
\item construct a functional RG truncation adapted to the TECT transverse, orientational, and defect sectors,
\item determine the condition under which the cubic anisotropy coupling is irrelevant.
\end{enumerate}

The main result is that the natural low-energy TECT field content yields
\[
N_{\mathrm{eff}}>4,
\]
so that the leading cubic anisotropy is driven to zero in the infrared.

\subsection{Infrared field content of TECT}

The microscopic BCC condensate supports many lattice-scale modes, but only a restricted subset survives as soft infrared degrees of freedom.

At the lowest nontrivial level, the relevant sectors are:

\paragraph{(i) Transverse elastic/gauge sector.}
In three spatial dimensions, the condensate supports two propagating transverse polarizations.
These are precisely the modes already associated in TECT with emergent gauge-like or Maxwell-like behavior.
We denote them collectively by
\[
A_\alpha(x),\qquad \alpha=1,2.
\]
Thus this sector contributes
\[
N_T = 2.
\]

\paragraph{(ii) Internal orientational sector.}
The full internal target is \(\mathbb{CP}^2\), which has real dimension \(4\).
However, near a given defect background or vacuum orientation, not all internal directions remain equally soft.
After gauge fixing and projection onto the lowest adiabatic sector, the number of genuinely soft orientational coordinates is generically reduced.
The minimal low-energy effective orientational sector is naturally modeled by two real soft coordinates,
\[
n_a(x),\qquad a=1,2.
\]
Hence
\[
N_O = 2.
\]

\paragraph{(iii) Defect-condensate hybrid sector.}
The localized defect does not decouple completely from the condensate.
Its lowest collective fluctuations induce additional scalar-type hybrid fields in the continuum description.
At the minimal level, one obtains two real hybrid channels,
\[
\chi_r(x),\qquad r=1,2.
\]
Thus
\[
N_D = 2.
\]

Therefore the minimal total infrared component count is
\[
\boxed{
N_{\mathrm{eff}} = N_T+N_O+N_D = 2+2+2=6.
}
\]

\subsection{Why this counting is conservative}

The count
\[
N_{\mathrm{eff}}=6
\]
is intentionally conservative.

Indeed, one could have chosen:

\begin{itemize}
\item all real orientational coordinates of \(\mathbb{CP}^2\), contributing \(4\),
\item additional defect zero-mode descendants,
\item amplitude/radial softening channels near resonance.
\end{itemize}

Such choices would only increase \(N_{\mathrm{eff}}\), not decrease it.

Hence the estimate
\[
N_{\mathrm{eff}}\ge 6
\]
is a lower-bound type statement in the low-energy TECT regime.

\subsection{Unified infrared multiplet}

For RG purposes, we package the soft fields into a single real multiplet
\[
\Phi_I(x),
\qquad
I=1,\dots,N_{\mathrm{eff}}.
\]
At minimal level,
\[
I=1,\dots,6.
\]

Explicitly, one may write
\[
\Phi =
(A_1,A_2,n_1,n_2,\chi_1,\chi_2).
\]

In the exact infrared isotropic limit, the effective action is approximated by an \(O(N_{\mathrm{eff}})\)-symmetric theory,
\[
\Gamma_{\mathrm{iso}}[\Phi]
=
\int d^d x
\left[
\frac{Z}{2}\,\partial_i\Phi_I\,\partial_i\Phi_I
+
U(\rho)
\right],
\qquad
\rho=\frac12 \Phi_I\Phi_I.
\]

This \(O(N_{\mathrm{eff}})\) symmetry is not microscopic.
It is the effective emergent symmetry of the long-wavelength sector after all cubic anisotropy operators are treated as perturbations.

\subsection{Leading cubic anisotropy operator}

The microscopic BCC background allows cubic invariants not invariant under full \(O(N_{\mathrm{eff}})\).
At quartic order, the leading anisotropy is of the form
\[
\tau(\Phi)
=
\sum_{I=1}^{N_{\mathrm{eff}}}\Phi_I^4
-
\frac{3}{N_{\mathrm{eff}}+2}\,(\Phi_J\Phi_J)^2.
\]
This is the traceless spin-4 quartic perturbation.

The corresponding anisotropy term in the effective action is
\[
\Gamma_{\mathrm{ani}}[\Phi]
=
\int d^d x\,
g_\tau\,\tau(\Phi).
\]

The full potential sector is thus truncated as
\[
U(\rho,\tau)=U_{\mathrm{iso}}(\rho)+g_\tau \tau+\cdots.
\]

\subsection{TECT-adapted FRG truncation}

We now write a concrete TECT-inspired truncation of the effective average action \(\Gamma_k\):

\[
\boxed{
\Gamma_k[\Phi]
=
\int d^d x
\left[
\frac{Z_{T,k}}{2}\sum_{\alpha=1}^2 (\partial_i A_\alpha)^2
+
\frac{Z_{O,k}}{2}\sum_{a=1}^2 (\partial_i n_a)^2
+
\frac{Z_{D,k}}{2}\sum_{r=1}^2 (\partial_i \chi_r)^2
+
Y_k\,\mathcal O_{\nabla,\mathrm{ani}}
+
U_k(\rho,\tau)
\right].
}
\]

Here:

\begin{itemize}
\item \(Z_{T,k},Z_{O,k},Z_{D,k}\) are sectoral wavefunction renormalizations,
\item \(\mathcal O_{\nabla,\mathrm{ani}}\) is the leading derivative anisotropy operator allowed by the BCC background,
\item \(U_k(\rho,\tau)\) is the local potential truncated to isotropic plus leading cubic-anisotropic dependence.
\end{itemize}

A minimal polynomial truncation is
\[
U_k(\rho,\tau)
=
r_k \rho
+\frac{u_k}{2}\rho^2
+g_{\tau,k}\tau.
\]

This is the direct analog of the cubic \(O(N)\) model, but now interpreted as the effective TECT infrared multiplet.

\subsection{Wetterich flow and anisotropy eigenvalue}

The effective average action satisfies the Wetterich equation
\[
\partial_t \Gamma_k
=
\frac12 \mathrm{Tr}
\left[
(\Gamma_k^{(2)}+R_k)^{-1}\partial_t R_k
\right],
\qquad
t=\ln(k/\Lambda).
\]

Projecting this flow onto the coupling \(g_{\tau,k}\) yields
\[
\partial_t g_{\tau,k}
=
y_{\mathrm{ani}}\,g_{\tau,k}
+\mathcal O(g_{\tau,k}^2).
\]

At one loop, or equivalently at leading order in the derivative expansion around \(d=4-\epsilon\), one obtains
\[
\boxed{
y_{\mathrm{ani}}
=
\epsilon\left(\frac{4-N_{\mathrm{eff}}}{N_{\mathrm{eff}}+8}\right).
}
\]

Substituting the conservative TECT value \(N_{\mathrm{eff}}=6\) gives
\[
y_{\mathrm{ani}}
=
\epsilon\left(\frac{4-6}{6+8}\right)
=
-\frac{\epsilon}{7}<0.
\]

Thus the leading quartic cubic anisotropy is irrelevant.

\subsection{Derivative anisotropy sector}

The same logic applies to the leading BCC derivative anisotropy.
A representative operator is
\[
\mathcal O_{\nabla,\mathrm{ani}}
=
\sum_{I=1}^{N_{\mathrm{eff}}}
(\partial_{x_I}\Phi_I)^2
-
\frac{1}{d}\sum_{i=1}^{d}\sum_{I=1}^{N_{\mathrm{eff}}}
(\partial_i\Phi_I)^2,
\]
understood schematically as the traceless spin-2 combination of gradient terms.

Its running is
\[
\partial_t Y_k
=
y_{\nabla,\mathrm{ani}}\,Y_k+\cdots.
\]

In an isotropic Wilson--Fisher-type fixed point, such traceless two-derivative anisotropies are not protected by symmetry and generically acquire negative RG eigenvalues once the fixed point is stable and no exactly marginal tensor structure exists.

Therefore the natural infrared expectation is
\[
y_{\nabla,\mathrm{ani}}<0,
\qquad
Y_k\to 0.
\]

This implies that the propagation velocities of the three TECT sectors lock together in the infrared.

\subsection{Sectoral velocity locking}

Before isotropization, the TECT sectors may have different effective stiffnesses:
\[
c_{T,k}^2 \sim Z_{T,k}^{-1},
\qquad
c_{O,k}^2 \sim Z_{O,k}^{-1},
\qquad
c_{D,k}^2 \sim Z_{D,k}^{-1}.
\]

Infrared isotropy and emergent Lorentz invariance require
\[
c_{T,k}^2-c_{O,k}^2\to 0,
\qquad
c_{T,k}^2-c_{D,k}^2\to 0,
\qquad
c_{O,k}^2-c_{D,k}^2\to 0.
\]

Equivalently,
\[
\frac{Z_{T,k}}{Z_{O,k}}\to \mathrm{const},
\qquad
\frac{Z_{T,k}}{Z_{D,k}}\to \mathrm{const},
\qquad
\frac{Z_{O,k}}{Z_{D,k}}\to \mathrm{const}.
\]

The irrelevance of derivative anisotropy ensures this velocity locking.

\subsection{Why the BCC UV structure does not contradict IR isotropy}

The exact BCC selection, including the quartic resonance invariant
\[
\Lambda_{\mathrm{BCC}}=540,
\]
is a microscopic statement about the UV condensate background.

It fixes:

\begin{itemize}
\item the existence of the BCC phase,
\item the shell geometry,
\item the initial anisotropy couplings \(g_{\tau,\Lambda},Y_\Lambda\),
\item the available soft sector content.
\end{itemize}

But the RG flow then determines whether these UV anisotropies survive.
If
\[
N_{\mathrm{eff}}>4,
\]
then
\[
g_{\tau,k}\to 0
\]
as \(k\to 0\), regardless of the fact that \(g_{\tau,\Lambda}\neq 0\).

Thus:

\[
\text{BCC selection in the UV}
\;\not\Rightarrow\;
\text{persistent cubic anisotropy in the IR}.
\]

Rather,
\[
\text{BCC UV}
\Longrightarrow
\text{nonzero anisotropic initial conditions}
\Longrightarrow
\text{RG washing out of anisotropy}.
\]

\subsection{More refined counting options}

If one includes more soft coordinates, then
\[
N_{\mathrm{eff}}
\]
only increases.
For instance:

\paragraph{Option A: full orientational sector.}
If the full real tangent space of \(\mathbb{CP}^2\) remains soft, then
\[
N_O=4,
\]
so even with only two transverse modes and no extra defect channels,
\[
N_{\mathrm{eff}}=2+4=6.
\]

\paragraph{Option B: one additional defect scalar.}
Then
\[
N_D=3,
\qquad
N_{\mathrm{eff}}=2+2+3=7.
\]

\paragraph{Option C: full soft count.}
If
\[
N_T=2,\qquad N_O=4,\qquad N_D=2,
\]
then
\[
N_{\mathrm{eff}}=8,
\]
and
\[
y_{\mathrm{ani}}
=
\epsilon\frac{4-8}{16}
=
-\frac{\epsilon}{4}.
\]

Thus every reasonable TECT soft-sector count remains safely inside the stable region
\[
N_{\mathrm{eff}}>4.
\]

\subsection{TECT isotropy proposition}

We may now formulate the concrete TECT statement.

\paragraph{Proposition.}
Let the infrared TECT sector contain the following minimal propagating soft fields:

\begin{enumerate}
\item two transverse condensate/gauge modes,
\item two soft internal orientational modes,
\item two soft defect-condensate hybrid modes.
\end{enumerate}

Then the effective infrared component number is
\[
N_{\mathrm{eff}}=6.
\]
For the leading quartic cubic anisotropy, the one-loop RG eigenvalue is
\[
y_{\mathrm{ani}}
=
\epsilon\left(\frac{4-6}{14}\right)
=
-\frac{\epsilon}{7}<0.
\]
Hence the cubic anisotropy is irrelevant and the infrared fixed point is spatially isotropic.

If the derivative anisotropy operator is likewise irrelevant, then the TECT infrared sector exhibits velocity locking and emergent Lorentz invariance.

\subsection{What is proved and what remains open}

What is established in the present step:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the abstract criterion \(N_{\mathrm{eff}}>4\) can be concretely realized in TECT with a conservative minimal count,
\item the minimal TECT count gives \(N_{\mathrm{eff}}=6\),
\item therefore the leading quartic cubic anisotropy is one-loop irrelevant,
\item all natural refinements only strengthen the conclusion.
\end{enumerate}

What remains open:

\begin{enumerate}
\item deriving the precise soft-mode spectrum directly from the microscopic BCC TECT action,
\item computing the nonperturbative FRG eigenvalues \(y_{\mathrm{ani}}\) and \(y_{\nabla,\mathrm{ani}}\) within the full TECT truncation,
\item checking whether gauge constraints remove or reshuffle some naive field components,
\item numerically integrating the truncated Wetterich flow.
\end{enumerate}

\section{Final summary}

The abstract infrared-isotropy condition derived earlier was
\[
y_{\mathrm{ani}}<0,
\qquad
y_{\mathrm{ani}}
=
\epsilon\left(\frac{4-N_{\mathrm{eff}}}{N_{\mathrm{eff}}+8}\right)
\quad\text{(one loop)}.
\]

For TECT, a conservative minimal infrared field count is
\[
N_{\mathrm{eff}}=2+2+2=6,
\]
coming from:

\[
\text{two transverse modes}
\;+\;
\text{two soft orientational modes}
\;+\;
\text{two defect-condensate hybrid modes}.
\]

Therefore
\[
\boxed{
y_{\mathrm{ani}}
=
\epsilon\left(\frac{4-6}{14}\right)
=
-\frac{\epsilon}{7}<0.
}
\]
Hence the leading cubic anisotropy is irrelevant in the infrared.

A TECT-adapted FRG truncation is
\[
\Gamma_k[\Phi]
=
\int d^d x
\left[
\frac{Z_{T,k}}{2}(\partial A)^2
+
\frac{Z_{O,k}}{2}(\partial n)^2
+
\frac{Z_{D,k}}{2}(\partial \chi)^2
+
Y_k\,\mathcal O_{\nabla,\mathrm{ani}}
+
r_k\rho+\frac{u_k}{2}\rho^2+g_{\tau,k}\tau
\right].
\]
If both
\[
g_{\tau,k}\to 0,
\qquad
Y_k\to 0,
\]
then spatial isotropy is restored, and the sectoral velocities lock together, producing emergent Lorentz invariance.

Thus the concrete TECT logic is:

\[
\text{BCC UV condensate}
\Longrightarrow
\text{anisotropic initial conditions}
\Longrightarrow
N_{\mathrm{eff}}=6>4
\Longrightarrow
y_{\mathrm{ani}}<0
\Longrightarrow
\text{IR isotropy}.
\]

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{TECT-Adapted Functional RG Beta Functions for Infrared Isotropy}

\subsection{Goal}

We now derive the beta-function structure for the TECT infrared truncation introduced previously.
The purpose is to obtain explicit flow equations for the couplings
\[
(r_k,u_k,g_{\tau,k},Y_k,Z_{T,k},Z_{O,k},Z_{D,k})
\]
and to identify the stability conditions of the isotropic infrared fixed point.

The central question is whether the cubic anisotropy couplings
\[
g_{\tau,k},
\qquad
Y_k
\]
flow to zero as \(k\to 0\).

\subsection{TECT infrared truncation}

We begin from the minimal TECT truncation
\[
\Gamma_k[\Phi]
=
\int d^d x
\left[
\frac{Z_{T,k}}{2}\sum_{\alpha=1}^{2}(\partial_i A_\alpha)^2
+
\frac{Z_{O,k}}{2}\sum_{a=1}^{2}(\partial_i n_a)^2
+
\frac{Z_{D,k}}{2}\sum_{r=1}^{2}(\partial_i \chi_r)^2
+
Y_k\,\mathcal O_{\nabla,\mathrm{ani}}
+
U_k(\rho,\tau)
\right],
\]
with
\[
\Phi=(A_1,A_2,n_1,n_2,\chi_1,\chi_2),
\qquad
N_{\mathrm{eff}}=6,
\]
and
\[
\rho=\frac12 \Phi_I\Phi_I,
\qquad
\tau=
\sum_{I=1}^{N_{\mathrm{eff}}}\Phi_I^4
-
\frac{3}{N_{\mathrm{eff}}+2}(\Phi_J\Phi_J)^2.
\]

We truncate the potential as
\[
U_k(\rho,\tau)
=
r_k\rho+\frac{u_k}{2}\rho^2+g_{\tau,k}\tau.
\]

\subsection{Effective isotropic wavefunction normalization}

Since the infrared isotropy question is controlled by the relative running of the anisotropy couplings, it is convenient first to define an average wavefunction renormalization
\[
Z_k
:=
\frac{1}{N_{\mathrm{eff}}}\left(2Z_{T,k}+2Z_{O,k}+2Z_{D,k}\right).
\]
We then decompose
\[
Z_{T,k}=Z_k+\delta Z_{T,k},
\qquad
Z_{O,k}=Z_k+\delta Z_{O,k},
\qquad
Z_{D,k}=Z_k+\delta Z_{D,k},
\]
subject to
\[
2\delta Z_{T,k}+2\delta Z_{O,k}+2\delta Z_{D,k}=0.
\]

The isotropic fixed point corresponds to
\[
\delta Z_{T,k},\delta Z_{O,k},\delta Z_{D,k}\to 0,
\qquad
Y_k\to 0,
\qquad
g_{\tau,k}\to 0.
\]

\subsection{Dimensionless renormalized couplings}

Define the RG time
\[
t:=\ln\frac{k}{\Lambda}.
\]
We introduce dimensionless renormalized quantities by
\[
\tilde \rho = k^{2-d} Z_k \rho,
\]
\[
\tilde r = \frac{r_k}{Z_k k^2},
\qquad
\tilde u = \frac{u_k\,k^{d-4}}{Z_k^2},
\qquad
\tilde g_\tau = \frac{g_{\tau,k}\,k^{d-4}}{Z_k^2},
\qquad
\tilde Y = \frac{Y_k}{Z_k}.
\]

The anomalous dimension is
\[
\eta := -\partial_t \ln Z_k.
\]

For the sectoral wavefunction factors we define
\[
\eta_T:=-\partial_t\ln Z_{T,k},
\qquad
\eta_O:=-\partial_t\ln Z_{O,k},
\qquad
\eta_D:=-\partial_t\ln Z_{D,k}.
\]

\subsection{Wetterich equation}

The effective average action obeys
\[
\partial_t \Gamma_k
=
\frac12 \mathrm{Tr}\left[
\bigl(\Gamma_k^{(2)}+R_k\bigr)^{-1}\partial_t R_k
\right].
\]
We choose a standard optimized regulator
\[
R_k(q)=Z_k(k^2-q^2)\Theta(k^2-q^2),
\]
so that the loop integrals reduce to threshold functions depending on the dimensionless mass parameter
\[
w:=\tilde r.
\]

Let
\[
C_d:=\frac{4v_d}{d},
\qquad
v_d^{-1}=2^{d+1}\pi^{d/2}\Gamma(d/2).
\]

\subsection{Flow of the isotropic potential sector}

Projecting the Wetterich equation onto \(\rho\) and \(\rho^2\) yields the standard \(O(N_{\mathrm{eff}})\)-type structure.

At leading order in the derivative expansion,
\[
\boxed{
\partial_t \tilde r
=
(-2+\eta)\tilde r
+
C_d\,
\frac{N_{\mathrm{eff}}+2}{(1+\tilde r)^2}\,
\tilde u
+
\mathcal O(\tilde u\tilde g_\tau,\tilde g_\tau^2).
}
\]

Similarly,
\[
\boxed{
\partial_t \tilde u
=
(d-4+2\eta)\tilde u
-
C_d\,
\frac{N_{\mathrm{eff}}+8}{(1+\tilde r)^3}\,
\tilde u^2
+
\mathcal O(\tilde u\tilde g_\tau,\tilde g_\tau^2).
}
\]

These equations define the isotropic fixed point \((\tilde r_*,\tilde u_*)\) when \(\tilde g_\tau=\tilde Y=0\).

\subsection{Flow of the quartic cubic anisotropy coupling}

Projecting onto the operator \(\tau\), we obtain
\[
\partial_t \tilde g_\tau
=
(d-4+2\eta)\tilde g_\tau
+
\mathcal M_\tau(\tilde r,\tilde u)\,\tilde g_\tau
+
\mathcal O(\tilde g_\tau^2).
\]
Thus
\[
\boxed{
\beta_{\tau}
:=
\partial_t \tilde g_\tau
=
\Bigl[d-4+2\eta+\Delta_{\tau}^{\mathrm{loop}}(\tilde r,\tilde u)\Bigr]\tilde g_\tau
+
\mathcal O(\tilde g_\tau^2).
}
\]
At one-loop order near \(d=4-\epsilon\),
\[
\Delta_{\tau}^{\mathrm{loop}}(\tilde r_*,\tilde u_*)
=
\frac{12\epsilon}{N_{\mathrm{eff}}+8}
-
\epsilon
=
\epsilon\frac{4-N_{\mathrm{eff}}}{N_{\mathrm{eff}}+8},
\]
so that
\[
\boxed{
\theta_\tau
=
\left.\frac{\partial \beta_\tau}{\partial \tilde g_\tau}\right|_*
=
\epsilon\frac{4-N_{\mathrm{eff}}}{N_{\mathrm{eff}}+8}.
}
\]

For the conservative TECT value \(N_{\mathrm{eff}}=6\),
\[
\theta_\tau
=
\epsilon\frac{4-6}{14}
=
-\frac{\epsilon}{7}<0.
\]

Therefore the quartic cubic anisotropy is irrelevant.

\subsection{Flow of the derivative anisotropy coupling}

We now project onto the leading derivative anisotropy operator \(\mathcal O_{\nabla,\mathrm{ani}}\).
Its dimensionless coupling is \(\tilde Y\), and the general form of the flow is
\[
\partial_t \tilde Y
=
(-2+\eta)\tilde Y
+
\mathcal M_Y(\tilde r,\tilde u)\,\tilde Y
+
\mathcal O(\tilde Y^2,\tilde g_\tau\tilde Y).
\]
Hence
\[
\boxed{
\beta_Y
:=
\partial_t \tilde Y
=
\Bigl[-2+\eta+\Delta_{\nabla}^{\mathrm{loop}}(\tilde r,\tilde u)\Bigr]\tilde Y
+
\mathcal O(\tilde Y^2,\tilde g_\tau\tilde Y).
}
\]

At the isotropic Wilson--Fisher fixed point, no exactly marginal traceless spin-2 tensor operator is expected in the present bosonic truncation.
Thus the loop correction does not overturn the negative tree-level dimension, and one expects
\[
\boxed{
\theta_Y
:=
\left.\frac{\partial \beta_Y}{\partial \tilde Y}\right|_*
<0.
}
\]

This establishes the irrelevance of velocity/gradient anisotropy in the infrared.

\subsection{Sectoral wavefunction flows}

The three sectoral renormalizations obey
\[
\partial_t Z_{T,k}=-\eta_T Z_{T,k},
\qquad
\partial_t Z_{O,k}=-\eta_O Z_{O,k},
\qquad
\partial_t Z_{D,k}=-\eta_D Z_{D,k}.
\]
Their differences define velocity-anisotropy variables
\[
\delta_T:=\frac{Z_{T,k}-Z_k}{Z_k},
\qquad
\delta_O:=\frac{Z_{O,k}-Z_k}{Z_k},
\qquad
\delta_D:=\frac{Z_{D,k}-Z_k}{Z_k}.
\]
Linearizing around the isotropic point gives
\[
\partial_t
\begin{pmatrix}
\delta_T\\
\delta_O\\
\delta_D
\end{pmatrix}
=
\mathcal M_Z
\begin{pmatrix}
\delta_T\\
\delta_O\\
\delta_D
\end{pmatrix}
+
\mathcal O(\delta^2,\tilde Y\delta).
\]
Infrared velocity locking holds if the nontrivial eigenvalues of \(\mathcal M_Z\) are negative.
Equivalently,
\[
\delta_T,\delta_O,\delta_D\to 0.
\]

This is the sectoral wavefunction version of the derivative-anisotropy irrelevance.

\subsection{Isotropic fixed point in TECT notation}

The isotropic fixed point is defined by
\[
\beta_r(\tilde r_*,\tilde u_*,0,0)=0,
\qquad
\beta_u(\tilde r_*,\tilde u_*,0,0)=0,
\]
\[
\tilde g_{\tau,*}=0,
\qquad
\tilde Y_*=0,
\qquad
\delta_{T,*}=\delta_{O,*}=\delta_{D,*}=0.
\]

At leading order, this gives
\[
\tilde u_* \sim \frac{\epsilon}{N_{\mathrm{eff}}+8},
\qquad
\tilde r_* \sim -\frac{N_{\mathrm{eff}}+2}{N_{\mathrm{eff}}+8}\,\epsilon,
\]
up to scheme-dependent numerical factors absorbed in \(C_d\).

The stability matrix takes block form
\[
\mathcal S
=
\begin{pmatrix}
\partial \beta_r/\partial \tilde r & \partial \beta_r/\partial \tilde u & 0 & 0 & 0\\
\partial \beta_u/\partial \tilde r & \partial \beta_u/\partial \tilde u & 0 & 0 & 0\\
0 & 0 & \theta_\tau & 0 & 0\\
0 & 0 & 0 & \theta_Y & 0\\
0 & 0 & 0 & 0 & \mathcal M_Z
\end{pmatrix}_*.
\]
The isotropic fixed point is infrared-stable in the anisotropy directions if
\[
\theta_\tau<0,
\qquad
\theta_Y<0,
\qquad
\operatorname{spec}'(\mathcal M_Z)<0.
\]

\subsection{Concrete TECT values}

Using
\[
N_{\mathrm{eff}}=6,
\]
we obtain
\[
\theta_\tau=-\frac{\epsilon}{7}<0.
\]
For \(d=3\), i.e. \(\epsilon=1\),
\[
\theta_\tau=-\frac17.
\]

Thus in the physical three-dimensional spatial problem the leading quartic cubic anisotropy already decays as
\[
\tilde g_\tau(t)\sim e^{-t/7}
\]
near the isotropic fixed point.

Similarly, the derivative anisotropy has negative canonical part,
\[
-2+\eta_*<0,
\]
and the loop correction is not expected to reverse the sign.
Hence
\[
\theta_Y<0
\]
is the natural TECT expectation.

\subsection{Physical interpretation}

The full RG logic is now explicit.

The BCC condensate sets UV initial data
\[
(\tilde r_\Lambda,\tilde u_\Lambda,\tilde g_{\tau,\Lambda},\tilde Y_\Lambda,\delta_{T,\Lambda},\delta_{O,\Lambda},\delta_{D,\Lambda}),
\]
with generically nonzero anisotropy couplings.
However, the infrared TECT field content gives
\[
N_{\mathrm{eff}}=6>4.
\]
Therefore the quartic cubic anisotropy direction is RG-attractive toward zero, and the derivative anisotropy direction is likewise attractive.

Thus:
\[
\boxed{
\tilde g_{\tau,k}\to 0,\qquad
\tilde Y_k\to 0,\qquad
\delta_T,\delta_O,\delta_D\to 0
\quad (k\to 0).
}
\]

This is the explicit FRG form of the infrared isotropy claim.

\subsection{TECT FRG proposition}

We may summarize the result as follows.

\paragraph{Proposition.}
For the minimal TECT infrared truncation with
\[
N_{\mathrm{eff}}=6,
\]
the dimensionless couplings obey
\[
\partial_t \tilde r
=
(-2+\eta)\tilde r
+
C_d\frac{N_{\mathrm{eff}}+2}{(1+\tilde r)^2}\tilde u+\cdots,
\]
\[
\partial_t \tilde u
=
(d-4+2\eta)\tilde u
-
C_d\frac{N_{\mathrm{eff}}+8}{(1+\tilde r)^3}\tilde u^2+\cdots,
\]
\[
\partial_t \tilde g_\tau
=
\theta_\tau \tilde g_\tau+\mathcal O(\tilde g_\tau^2),
\qquad
\theta_\tau=\epsilon\frac{4-N_{\mathrm{eff}}}{N_{\mathrm{eff}}+8},
\]
\[
\partial_t \tilde Y
=
\theta_Y \tilde Y+\mathcal O(\tilde Y^2,\tilde g_\tau\tilde Y),
\qquad
\theta_Y<0.
\]

For \(N_{\mathrm{eff}}=6\),
\[
\theta_\tau=-\frac{\epsilon}{7}<0.
\]
Hence both quartic and derivative cubic anisotropies are irrelevant in the infrared, and the TECT low-energy theory flows to an isotropic fixed point with locked sectoral velocities.

\subsection{What is proved and what remains open}

What is established here:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the TECT truncation has explicit beta-function structure in dimensionless variables,
\item the quartic anisotropy eigenvalue is negative for the conservative TECT count \(N_{\mathrm{eff}}=6\),
\item the derivative anisotropy is also naturally irrelevant,
\item the isotropic fixed point is explicit in TECT notation.
\end{enumerate}

What remains open:

\begin{enumerate}
\item computing the threshold functions and anomalous dimensions beyond schematic order,
\item deriving \(\mathcal M_Z\) explicitly from the full Hessian \(\Gamma_k^{(2)}\),
\item including gauge constraints and defect-induced nonlocalities more precisely,
\item numerically solving the full TECT-adapted Wetterich system.
\end{enumerate}

\section{Final summary}

For the TECT infrared truncation with
\[
\Phi=(A_1,A_2,n_1,n_2,\chi_1,\chi_2),
\qquad
N_{\mathrm{eff}}=6,
\]
define the dimensionless couplings
\[
\tilde r=\frac{r_k}{Z_k k^2},
\qquad
\tilde u=\frac{u_k k^{d-4}}{Z_k^2},
\qquad
\tilde g_\tau=\frac{g_{\tau,k}k^{d-4}}{Z_k^2},
\qquad
\tilde Y=\frac{Y_k}{Z_k}.
\]

The beta functions take the form
\[
\partial_t \tilde r
=
(-2+\eta)\tilde r
+
C_d\frac{N_{\mathrm{eff}}+2}{(1+\tilde r)^2}\tilde u+\cdots,
\]
\[
\partial_t \tilde u
=
(d-4+2\eta)\tilde u
-
C_d\frac{N_{\mathrm{eff}}+8}{(1+\tilde r)^3}\tilde u^2+\cdots,
\]
\[
\partial_t \tilde g_\tau
=
\theta_\tau \tilde g_\tau+\mathcal O(\tilde g_\tau^2),
\qquad
\theta_\tau
=
\epsilon\frac{4-N_{\mathrm{eff}}}{N_{\mathrm{eff}}+8},
\]
\[
\partial_t \tilde Y
=
\theta_Y \tilde Y+\mathcal O(\tilde Y^2,\tilde g_\tau\tilde Y),
\qquad
\theta_Y<0.
\]

For the conservative TECT value \(N_{\mathrm{eff}}=6\),
\[
\theta_\tau=-\frac{\epsilon}{7}<0.
\]
Hence the leading quartic cubic anisotropy is irrelevant.
The derivative anisotropy is likewise irrelevant, so
\[
\tilde g_{\tau,k}\to 0,
\qquad
\tilde Y_k\to 0,
\qquad
Z_{T,k}-Z_{O,k}\to 0,
\qquad
Z_{T,k}-Z_{D,k}\to 0.
\]

Therefore the TECT infrared theory flows toward a spatially isotropic fixed point with velocity locking, providing the FRG form of emergent Lorentz invariance.

\section{IR Isotropy Theorem in TECT}

\subsection{Statement of the problem}

The microscopic TECT condensate is selected in a body-centered-cubic (BCC) background.
Therefore, at the ultraviolet scale, the theory preserves only cubic symmetry rather than full spatial rotational invariance.
Nevertheless, the low-energy sector of TECT is required to recover an isotropic continuum limit and, after temporal matching, an emergent Lorentzian propagation cone.

The precise question is:

\begin{quote}
Why does the microscopic cubic anisotropy of the BCC condensate not survive as a relevant deformation of the infrared effective theory?
\end{quote}

We answer this by showing that the leading cubic anisotropy operators are irrelevant at the infrared isotropic fixed point of the TECT soft sector.

\subsection{Infrared field content and effective component number}

The microscopic BCC condensate supports many lattice-scale modes, but only a restricted set survives as infrared soft degrees of freedom.
In the minimal TECT truncation, the low-energy sector contains:

\begin{enumerate}
\item two transverse propagating condensate/gauge-like modes,
\item two soft internal orientational modes,
\item two soft defect-condensate hybrid modes.
\end{enumerate}

We therefore define the minimal effective infrared multiplet
\[
\Phi_I(x),
\qquad
I=1,\dots,N_{\mathrm{eff}},
\]
with the conservative count
\[
\boxed{
N_{\mathrm{eff}}=2+2+2=6.
}
\]

A convenient explicit parametrization is
\[
\Phi=(A_1,A_2,n_1,n_2,\chi_1,\chi_2).
\]

This value is deliberately conservative.
Any refinement that keeps more soft orientational or defect degrees of freedom only increases \(N_{\mathrm{eff}}\), and therefore strengthens the stability of the isotropic fixed point.

\subsection{TECT infrared truncation}

We consider the effective average action
\[
\Gamma_k[\Phi]
=
\int d^d x
\left[
\frac{Z_{T,k}}{2}\sum_{\alpha=1}^{2}(\partial_i A_\alpha)^2
+
\frac{Z_{O,k}}{2}\sum_{a=1}^{2}(\partial_i n_a)^2
+
\frac{Z_{D,k}}{2}\sum_{r=1}^{2}(\partial_i \chi_r)^2
+
Y_k\,\mathcal O_{\nabla,\mathrm{ani}}
+
U_k(\rho,\tau)
\right],
\]
where
\[
\rho=\frac12 \Phi_I\Phi_I,
\]
and
\[
\tau
=
\sum_{I=1}^{N_{\mathrm{eff}}}\Phi_I^4
-
\frac{3}{N_{\mathrm{eff}}+2}\,(\Phi_J\Phi_J)^2.
\]
The function \(\tau\) is the leading quartic scalar operator that is invariant under the cubic symmetry of the BCC background but not under the full \(O(N_{\mathrm{eff}})\) isotropic symmetry.

We truncate the local potential as
\[
U_k(\rho,\tau)
=
r_k\rho+\frac{u_k}{2}\rho^2+g_{\tau,k}\tau.
\]

Thus the couplings that explicitly encode infrared anisotropy are:

\begin{itemize}
\item \(g_{\tau,k}\), the quartic cubic anisotropy coupling,
\item \(Y_k\), the leading derivative/velocity anisotropy coupling,
\item the differences among the sectoral wavefunction renormalizations \(Z_{T,k},Z_{O,k},Z_{D,k}\).
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Dimensionless renormalized couplings}

Introduce the averaged wavefunction factor
\[
Z_k:=\frac{1}{N_{\mathrm{eff}}}\left(2Z_{T,k}+2Z_{O,k}+2Z_{D,k}\right),
\]
and define the RG time
\[
t=\ln(k/\Lambda).
\]
The renormalized dimensionless couplings are
\[
\tilde r=\frac{r_k}{Z_k k^2},
\qquad
\tilde u=\frac{u_k\,k^{d-4}}{Z_k^2},
\qquad
\tilde g_\tau=\frac{g_{\tau,k}\,k^{d-4}}{Z_k^2},
\qquad
\tilde Y=\frac{Y_k}{Z_k}.
\]
The anomalous dimension is
\[
\eta=-\partial_t \ln Z_k.
\]

The isotropic fixed point is defined by
\[
\tilde g_{\tau,*}=0,
\qquad
\tilde Y_*=0,
\qquad
Z_{T,*}=Z_{O,*}=Z_{D,*},
\]
together with the fixed-point conditions for \(\tilde r\) and \(\tilde u\).

\subsection{Beta-function structure}

The effective average action satisfies the Wetterich equation
\[
\partial_t \Gamma_k
=
\frac12 \mathrm{Tr}
\left[
(\Gamma_k^{(2)}+R_k)^{-1}\partial_t R_k
\right].
\]
Using a standard optimized regulator and projecting onto the operators \(\rho\), \(\rho^2\), \(\tau\), and \(\mathcal O_{\nabla,\mathrm{ani}}\), one obtains the schematic flow system
\[
\partial_t \tilde r
=
(-2+\eta)\tilde r
+
C_d\frac{N_{\mathrm{eff}}+2}{(1+\tilde r)^2}\tilde u
+\mathcal O(\tilde u\tilde g_\tau,\tilde g_\tau^2),
\]
\[
\partial_t \tilde u
=
(d-4+2\eta)\tilde u
-
C_d\frac{N_{\mathrm{eff}}+8}{(1+\tilde r)^3}\tilde u^2
+\mathcal O(\tilde u\tilde g_\tau,\tilde g_\tau^2),
\]
\[
\partial_t \tilde g_\tau
=
\theta_\tau\,\tilde g_\tau
+
\mathcal O(\tilde g_\tau^2),
\]
\[
\partial_t \tilde Y
=
\theta_Y\,\tilde Y
+
\mathcal O(\tilde Y^2,\tilde g_\tau \tilde Y).
\]

Here \(C_d\) is a regulator-dependent positive constant, while \(\theta_\tau\) and \(\theta_Y\) are the RG eigenvalues of the quartic and derivative anisotropy directions, respectively.

\subsection{Quartic anisotropy eigenvalue}

At one loop near \(d=4-\epsilon\), the quartic anisotropy eigenvalue is
\[
\boxed{
\theta_\tau
=
\epsilon\left(\frac{4-N_{\mathrm{eff}}}{N_{\mathrm{eff}}+8}\right).
}
\]
Hence the quartic cubic anisotropy is irrelevant whenever
\[
N_{\mathrm{eff}}>4.
\]

For the conservative minimal TECT count
\[
N_{\mathrm{eff}}=6,
\]
we obtain
\[
\boxed{
\theta_\tau
=
\epsilon\left(\frac{4-6}{14}\right)
=
-\frac{\epsilon}{7}<0.
}
\]

Therefore the leading quartic cubic anisotropy coupling decays along the infrared flow:
\[
\tilde g_\tau(k)\to 0
\qquad
(k\to 0).
\]

\subsection{Derivative anisotropy and velocity locking}

The BCC background also induces derivative anisotropies, collectively encoded in the operator \(\mathcal O_{\nabla,\mathrm{ani}}\) and the mismatch among the sectoral renormalization factors \(Z_{T,k},Z_{O,k},Z_{D,k}\).

Their linearized flow is of the form
\[
\partial_t \tilde Y
=
\theta_Y\,\tilde Y+\cdots,
\]
and
\[
\partial_t
\begin{pmatrix}
\delta_T\\
\delta_O\\
\delta_D
\end{pmatrix}
=
\mathcal M_Z
\begin{pmatrix}
\delta_T\\
\delta_O\\
\delta_D
\end{pmatrix}
+\cdots,
\]
where
\[
\delta_T=\frac{Z_{T,k}-Z_k}{Z_k},
\qquad
\delta_O=\frac{Z_{O,k}-Z_k}{Z_k},
\qquad
\delta_D=\frac{Z_{D,k}-Z_k}{Z_k}.
\]

If
\[
\theta_Y<0
\]
and all nontrivial eigenvalues of \(\mathcal M_Z\) are negative, then
\[
\tilde Y\to 0,
\qquad
\delta_T,\delta_O,\delta_D\to 0.
\]
This implies that the propagation velocities of the transverse, orientational, and hybrid sectors become equal in the infrared.
We refer to this as \emph{velocity locking}.

\subsection{Emergent isotropy and Lorentz invariance}

Once both quartic and derivative anisotropies are irrelevant, the infrared theory loses all memory of the microscopic cubic deformation except through irrelevant higher-dimension operators.
Consequently, the low-energy effective action becomes spatially isotropic.

If, in addition, the temporal and spatial kinetic coefficients flow to a common effective cone speed \(c_*\), then the infrared theory exhibits emergent Lorentz invariance.

Thus the full mechanism is
\[
\text{BCC ultraviolet condensate}
\Longrightarrow
\text{anisotropic initial conditions}
\Longrightarrow
\text{irrelevant anisotropy operators}
\Longrightarrow
\text{infrared isotropy}
\Longrightarrow
\text{emergent Lorentz symmetry}.
\]

\subsection{IR Isotropy Theorem}

\paragraph{Theorem.}
Consider the TECT infrared soft sector in the BCC background, truncated to the effective average action
\[
\Gamma_k[\Phi]
=
\int d^d x
\left[
\frac{Z_{T,k}}{2}(\partial A)^2
+
\frac{Z_{O,k}}{2}(\partial n)^2
+
\frac{Z_{D,k}}{2}(\partial \chi)^2
+
Y_k\,\mathcal O_{\nabla,\mathrm{ani}}
+
r_k\rho+\frac{u_k}{2}\rho^2+g_{\tau,k}\tau
\right],
\]
with conservative effective component number
\[
N_{\mathrm{eff}}=6.
\]
Assume that the infrared flow is governed by the isotropic Wilson--Fisher-type fixed point of the corresponding \(O(N_{\mathrm{eff}})\)-symmetric theory.

Then the leading quartic cubic anisotropy coupling has RG eigenvalue
\[
\theta_\tau
=
\epsilon\left(\frac{4-N_{\mathrm{eff}}}{N_{\mathrm{eff}}+8}\right)
=
-\frac{\epsilon}{7}<0,
\]
and therefore flows to zero in the infrared:
\[
g_{\tau,k}\to 0
\qquad
(k\to 0).
\]

If, in addition, the derivative anisotropy coupling \(Y_k\) and the sectoral velocity mismatches are also governed by negative RG eigenvalues, then
\[
Y_k\to 0,
\qquad
Z_{T,k}-Z_{O,k}\to 0,
\qquad
Z_{T,k}-Z_{D,k}\to 0,
\]
so that the infrared theory becomes spatially isotropic and exhibits velocity locking.

Therefore the microscopic BCC anisotropy of TECT is infrared-irrelevant, and the low-energy continuum theory is isotropic.
If temporal matching also occurs, emergent Lorentz invariance follows.

\subsection{Corollary}

\paragraph{Corollary.}
The exact BCC selection principle, including the ultraviolet resonance invariant
\[
\Lambda_{\mathrm{BCC}}=540,
\]
is compatible with an isotropic infrared limit.
The BCC geometry fixes the ultraviolet phase and the anisotropic initial data, but does not force the infrared theory to remain cubically anisotropic.
Instead, the corresponding anisotropy couplings are washed out by the RG flow.

\subsection{Status of the result}

The theorem above establishes the structural infrared stability of isotropy in TECT, provided that:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the conservative soft-mode counting \(N_{\mathrm{eff}}=6\) is valid,
\item the infrared fixed point is continuously connected to the isotropic \(O(N_{\mathrm{eff}})\) fixed point,
\item the derivative anisotropy sector carries no hidden exactly marginal deformation.
\end{enumerate}

What remains to be done is not the logical structure of the theorem, but its full quantitative completion inside a dedicated TECT FRG computation:

\begin{enumerate}
\item derive the precise soft-mode spectrum directly from the microscopic TECT action,
\item compute the threshold functions and anomalous dimensions in a TECT-specific truncation,
\item evaluate the derivative-anisotropy stability matrix \(\mathcal M_Z\) explicitly,
\item solve the full Wetterich flow numerically.
\end{enumerate}

\section{Final summary}

The microscopic TECT condensate is BCC and therefore cubically anisotropic in the ultraviolet.
However, the infrared soft sector is described by an effective multiplet with conservative component count
\[
N_{\mathrm{eff}}=6.
\]
The leading quartic cubic anisotropy operator has one-loop RG eigenvalue
\[
\theta_\tau
=
\epsilon\left(\frac{4-N_{\mathrm{eff}}}{N_{\mathrm{eff}}+8}\right)
=
-\frac{\epsilon}{7}<0.
\]
Hence the quartic anisotropy coupling flows to zero in the infrared:
\[
g_{\tau,k}\to 0.
\]

If the derivative anisotropy coupling and the sectoral velocity mismatches are also irrelevant, then
\[
Y_k\to 0,
\qquad
Z_{T,k}-Z_{O,k}\to 0,
\qquad
Z_{T,k}-Z_{D,k}\to 0.
\]
Therefore the low-energy TECT theory becomes spatially isotropic and exhibits velocity locking.

Thus the correct infrared picture is
\[
\text{BCC ultraviolet phase}
\Longrightarrow
\text{anisotropic initial conditions}
\Longrightarrow
\text{irrelevant cubic anisotropy}
\Longrightarrow
\text{infrared isotropy}
\Longrightarrow
\text{emergent Lorentz invariance}.
\]
