\section{Final summary}

The defect moduli space is the minimal nontrivial \(SU(3)\) coadjoint orbit
\[
\mathbb{CP}^2 \cong SU(3)/U(2).
\]
Its Berry/WZW symplectic form must satisfy the prequantization condition
\[
\frac{[\Omega]}{2\pi}\in H^2(\mathbb{CP}^2,\mathbb Z),
\]
hence
\[
\frac{[\Omega]}{2\pi}=k\,h,
\qquad
k\in \mathbb Z_{\ge 0}.
\]

Because the physically relevant defect is the minimal nontrivial orbit sector, its symplectic class must be the primitive positive generator:
\[
[\Omega]/2\pi = h.
\]
Therefore
\[
\boxed{k=1.}
\]

The quantized family Hilbert space is then
\[
H^0(\mathbb{CP}^2,\mathcal O(1))
\cong
(\mathbb C^3)^*,
\]
so
\[
\boxed{
N_{\mathrm{gen}}=3.
}
\]

Thus the value \(k=1\) is not a free choice.
It is the minimal nontrivial coadjoint-orbit quantization level of the \(\mathbb{CP}^2\) defect.

%%%%%%%

\section{Rigorous derivation of the minimal quantization level \(k=1\)}

\subsection{Setup}

Let
\[
\mathcal M := \mathbb{CP}^2
\]
be the internal defect moduli space.
Let
\[
h \in H^2(\mathbb{CP}^2,\mathbb Z)
\]
denote the positive generator, normalized by
\[
\int_{\mathbb{CP}^1} h = 1,
\qquad
\int_{\mathbb{CP}^2} h^2 = 1.
\]

Assume that the defect internal dynamics is governed by a first-order Berry action whose curvature two-form is a positive integral K\"ahler form on \(\mathbb{CP}^2\):
\[
\Omega \in \Omega^2(\mathbb{CP}^2),
\qquad
[\Omega/2\pi] \in H^2(\mathbb{CP}^2,\mathbb Z),
\qquad
[\Omega/2\pi] > 0.
\]

Since
\[
H^2(\mathbb{CP}^2,\mathbb Z)\cong \mathbb Z\,h,
\]
there exists a unique integer \(k\ge 0\) such that
\[
\frac{[\Omega]}{2\pi}=k\,h.
\]

We now prove that the minimal nontrivial defect necessarily corresponds to
\[
k=1.
\]

\subsection{Lemma 1: Integrality forces \(k\in \mathbb Z\)}

\paragraph{Lemma.}
If the path integral weight
\[
e^{iS[\gamma]}
\]
for the defect worldline is defined by a Berry phase with curvature \(\Omega\), then
\[
\frac{[\Omega]}{2\pi}\in H^2(\mathbb{CP}^2,\mathbb Z).
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
Let \(\gamma\) be a closed worldline in \(\mathbb{CP}^2\), and let \(D_1,D_2\) be two fillings with
\[
\partial D_1=\partial D_2=\gamma.
\]
The Berry action is defined modulo
\[
\int_{D}\Omega.
\]
For the phase \(e^{iS}\) to be independent of the choice of filling, one must have
\[
\exp\!\left(i\int_{D_1}\Omega\right)
=
\exp\!\left(i\int_{D_2}\Omega\right).
\]
Hence
\[
\exp\!\left(i\int_{\Sigma}\Omega\right)=1,
\qquad
\Sigma:=D_1\cup(-D_2).
\]
Therefore
\[
\int_{\Sigma}\Omega \in 2\pi \mathbb Z
\]
for every closed two-cycle \(\Sigma\), which is exactly the condition
\[
\frac{[\Omega]}{2\pi}\in H^2(\mathbb{CP}^2,\mathbb Z).
\]
\qed

\subsection{Lemma 2: On \(\mathbb{CP}^2\), every integral K\"ahler class is \(k h\)}

\paragraph{Lemma.}
Every integral cohomology class in \(H^2(\mathbb{CP}^2,\mathbb Z)\) is of the form
\[
k h,
\qquad k\in \mathbb Z.
\]
If the class is positive K\"ahler, then \(k>0\).

\paragraph{Proof.}
Since
\[
H^2(\mathbb{CP}^2,\mathbb Z)\cong \mathbb Z,
\]
generated by \(h=c_1(\mathcal O(1))\), any integral class is \(k h\).
If \(\Omega\) is K\"ahler, then
\[
\int_{\mathbb{CP}^1}\Omega >0.
\]
But
\[
\int_{\mathbb{CP}^1}\Omega
=
2\pi k \int_{\mathbb{CP}^1}h
=
2\pi k,
\]
so \(k>0\).
\qed

\subsection{Lemma 3: Minimal nontriviality implies primitiveness}

\paragraph{Lemma.}
Let \(\alpha\in H^2(\mathbb{CP}^2,\mathbb Z)\cong \mathbb Z h\) be a nonzero positive integral class.
Then \(\alpha\) is the minimal nontrivial positive class if and only if
\[
\alpha = h.
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
Every positive nonzero integral class has the form
\[
\alpha = k h,
\qquad k\in \mathbb Z_{>0}.
\]
The set of such \(k\) is the set of positive integers, whose smallest element is \(1\).
Hence the smallest nontrivial positive class is precisely \(h\).
\qed

\subsection{Theorem 1: Minimal defect level is \(k=1\)}

\paragraph{Theorem.}
Assume:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the internal defect moduli space is \(\mathbb{CP}^2\),
\item the defect Berry curvature \(\Omega\) defines an integral positive K\"ahler class,
\item the physically realized defect is the minimal nontrivial topological excitation in this moduli sector.
\end{enumerate}

Then
\[
\frac{[\Omega]}{2\pi}=h,
\qquad\text{equivalently}\qquad k=1.
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
By Lemma 1,
\[
[\Omega/2\pi]\in H^2(\mathbb{CP}^2,\mathbb Z).
\]
By Lemma 2, there is a unique \(k\in \mathbb Z_{>0}\) such that
\[
[\Omega/2\pi]=k h.
\]
By Lemma 3, minimal nontriviality implies that this class must be the primitive positive generator \(h\).
Hence
\[
[\Omega/2\pi]=h,
\]
so
\[
k=1.
\]
\qed

\subsection{Corollary 1: The prequantum line bundle is \(\mathcal O(1)\)}

\paragraph{Corollary.}
Under the assumptions of Theorem 1, the prequantum line bundle is
\[
\mathcal L \cong \mathcal O(1).
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
A prequantum line bundle must satisfy
\[
c_1(\mathcal L)=\frac{[\Omega]}{2\pi}.
\]
By Theorem 1,
\[
c_1(\mathcal L)=h=c_1(\mathcal O(1)).
\]
Since line bundles on \(\mathbb{CP}^2\) are classified by \(H^2(\mathbb{CP}^2,\mathbb Z)\cong \mathbb Z\),
it follows that
\[
\mathcal L\cong \mathcal O(1).
\]
\qed

\subsection{Corollary 2: The generation space has dimension \(3\)}

\paragraph{Corollary.}
Under the same assumptions,
\[
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{fam}} = H^0(\mathbb{CP}^2,\mathcal O(1))
\]
and
\[
\dim \mathcal H_{\mathrm{fam}} = 3.
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
By Corollary 1, the quantization bundle is \(\mathcal O(1)\).
By Borel--Weil,
\[
H^0(\mathbb{CP}^2,\mathcal O(1))\cong (\mathbb C^3)^*.
\]
Hence
\[
\dim H^0(\mathbb{CP}^2,\mathcal O(1))=3.
\]
\qed

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Existence of Normalizable Defect Zero Modes via a Callias-Type Index Theorem}

\subsection{Goal}

The crucial analytic question in the defect sector is the existence of normalizable zero modes of the defect Dirac operator.
The problem is to justify, not heuristically but rigorously, the existence of solutions
\[
\Psi \in L^2(\mathbb R^3,S\otimes E)
\]
to
\[
\mathcal D_\Phi \Psi = 0,
\]
where \(\mathcal D_\Phi\) is the Dirac operator in the defect background.

The correct framework is the Callias index theorem for Dirac operators on noncompact space with an asymptotically invertible Higgs-type endomorphism.

The purpose of this section is to show that, once the TECT defect background is cast into a Callias-admissible form, normalizable zero modes follow rigorously from the topological charge of the asymptotic defect field.

\subsection{Defect Dirac operator}

Let \(S\to \mathbb R^3\) be the spin bundle and let \(E\to \mathbb R^3\) be the internal bundle induced by the defect background.
Let \(A_i(x)\) be a skew-Hermitian connection on \(E\), and let
\[
\Phi(x)\in \Gamma(\mathrm{End}(E))
\]
be a Hermitian Higgs-type endomorphism induced by the defect order parameter.

The corresponding Euclidean defect Dirac operator is
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal D_\Phi
=
i\sigma^i(\partial_i+A_i)+\Phi(x),
}
\]
acting on
\[
L^2(\mathbb R^3,S\otimes E).
\]

In chiral block form in four-dimensional notation, this is the spatial part of the defect-localized Dirac operator.
For the existence problem of zero modes, it is enough to study \(\mathcal D_\Phi\) on \(\mathbb R^3\).

\subsection{Callias-admissible background}

We impose the standard Callias admissibility conditions:

\begin{enumerate}
\item \(A_i,\Phi\) are smooth,
\item outside a compact set \(K\subset \mathbb R^3\), the endomorphism \(\Phi(x)\) is invertible,
\item there exists \(m_\infty>0\) such that
\[
\Phi(x)^2 \ge m_\infty^2\,\mathbf 1
\qquad
\text{for } |x|\gg 1,
\]
\item the commutator term is lower order at infinity:
\[
[\sigma^i(\partial_i+A_i),\Phi]
\to 0
\qquad
(|x|\to\infty).
\]
\end{enumerate}

These conditions imply that \(\mathcal D_\Phi\) is Fredholm as an operator on \(L^2\), so its index is well-defined.

\subsection{The asymptotic map and its topology}

Because \(\Phi(x)\) is invertible at infinity, its normalized limit defines a map on the sphere at infinity:
\[
\widehat{\Phi}_\infty : S^2_\infty \to \mathcal M_{\infty},
\qquad
\widehat{\Phi}_\infty(x)=\frac{\Phi(x)}{|\Phi(x)|}.
\]
Here \(\mathcal M_\infty\) is the space of allowed asymptotic normalized Higgs data.

In the TECT defect sector, the internal order parameter takes values in
\[
\mathbb{CP}^2,
\]
and the asymptotic defect field determines a map
\[
P_\infty:S^2_\infty\to \mathbb{CP}^2.
\]
Since
\[
\pi_2(\mathbb{CP}^2)=\mathbb Z,
\]
this map carries an integer topological charge
\[
Q_{\mathrm{def}}
=
\frac{1}{8\pi}\int_{S^2_\infty}\mathrm{Tr}\bigl(P[dP,dP]\bigr).
\]

To connect this with Callias theory, we assume that the endomorphism \(\Phi\) is induced from the projective field \(P\) by a fixed Hermitian embedding
\[
\Phi = \mu\,(2P-\mathbf 1_{\mathrm{eff}})
\]
or any equivalent asymptotically gapped realization.
Then the degree of the asymptotic Higgs field equals the defect charge:
\[
\deg(\widehat{\Phi}_\infty)=Q_{\mathrm{def}}.
\]

\subsection{Callias index theorem}

We now state the relevant existence theorem.

\paragraph{Theorem (Callias type).}
Let
\[
\mathcal D_\Phi = i\sigma^i(\partial_i+A_i)+\Phi
\]
be a Callias-admissible Dirac operator on \(\mathbb R^3\).
Then \(\mathcal D_\Phi\) is Fredholm, and its index is given by the topological degree of the asymptotic Higgs field:
\[
\boxed{
\mathrm{Index}\,\mathcal D_\Phi
=
\deg(\widehat{\Phi}_\infty).
}
\]

In the TECT defect realization described above,
\[
\boxed{
\mathrm{Index}\,\mathcal D_\Phi
=
Q_{\mathrm{def}}.
}
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
This is the standard Callias theorem applied to a Dirac operator with asymptotically invertible Higgs field.
The Fredholm property follows from the asymptotic spectral gap of \(\Phi\), and the index localizes to the sphere at infinity, where it is computed by the degree of the normalized Higgs map.
In the projective defect realization, the normalized Higgs field is induced by the asymptotic \(\mathbb{CP}^2\) map \(P_\infty\), so the degree equals the defect topological charge.
\qed

\subsection{Existence of \(L^2\) zero modes}

The index theorem immediately implies a zero-mode existence result.

\paragraph{Corollary.}
If
\[
Q_{\mathrm{def}}\neq 0,
\]
then
\[
\ker \mathcal D_\Phi \neq \{0\}.
\]
In particular, if
\[
Q_{\mathrm{def}}=1,
\]
then \(\mathcal D_\Phi\) possesses at least one normalizable zero mode.

\paragraph{Proof.}
Since \(\mathcal D_\Phi\) is Fredholm,
\[
\mathrm{Index}\,\mathcal D_\Phi
=
\dim\ker \mathcal D_\Phi - \dim\ker \mathcal D_\Phi^\dagger.
\]
If the index is nonzero, then at least one of the kernels must be nontrivial.
Hence a nontrivial \(L^2\) zero mode exists.
\qed

\subsection{Exponential localization}

We now prove that any zero mode obtained in this way is spatially localized.

\paragraph{Proposition.}
Let \(\Psi\in L^2\) satisfy
\[
\mathcal D_\Phi \Psi = 0.
\]
If \(\Phi^2(x)\ge m_\infty^2>0\) for \(|x|\gg 1\), then \(\Psi\) decays exponentially at infinity:
\[
|\Psi(x)| \le C e^{-\alpha |x|}
\qquad
\text{for some }\alpha<m_\infty.
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
Using the Weitzenb\"ock identity,
\[
\mathcal D_\Phi^\dagger \mathcal D_\Phi
=
-(\nabla_A)^2 + \Phi^2 + \mathcal R(x),
\]
where \(\mathcal R(x)\) is lower-order and tends to zero at infinity.
Outside a compact set,
\[
\Phi^2+\mathcal R \ge \frac{m_\infty^2}{2}.
\]
For a zero mode \(\Psi\),
\[
0
=
\langle \Psi,\mathcal D_\Phi^\dagger\mathcal D_\Phi \Psi\rangle
=
\|\nabla_A\Psi\|^2 + \langle \Psi,(\Phi^2+\mathcal R)\Psi\rangle.
\]
Applying a standard Agmon estimate with weight \(e^{\alpha |x|}\), \(0<\alpha<m_\infty\), yields exponential decay.
\qed

Thus the zero mode is not merely square-integrable but defect-localized.

\subsection{Minimal defect sector}

The most important case is the minimal nontrivial defect.
If the asymptotic \(\mathbb{CP}^2\) map satisfies
\[
Q_{\mathrm{def}}=1,
\]
then
\[
\mathrm{Index}\,\mathcal D_\Phi=1.
\]
Hence
\[
\dim\ker \mathcal D_\Phi - \dim\ker \mathcal D_\Phi^\dagger =1.
\]

In particular, if the adjoint kernel is absent in the chosen chirality convention, then
\[
\dim\ker \mathcal D_\Phi =1.
\]
More generally, regardless of the detailed chirality splitting, there exists at least one normalizable defect zero mode.

\subsection{Relation to chirality protection}

The above result is the noncompact-space existence theorem that complements the compact \(\mathbb{CP}^2\) index computation used earlier for chirality protection.

The compact index formula controlled the internal chiral asymmetry:
\[
n_L-n_R
=
\mathrm{Index}\,D_+^{(\mathrm{spin}^c,E)}.
\]
The present Callias theorem controls the existence of a spatially localized \(L^2\) mode in the full defect background.

Thus the two index statements play distinct roles:

\begin{itemize}
\item the Callias index guarantees existence and localization of defect zero modes in physical space,
\item the \(\mathrm{spin}^c\) index on \(\mathbb{CP}^2\) controls their chiral asymmetry in the internal sector.
\end{itemize}

Together they provide the correct analytic and topological foundation for the emergent fermion sector.

\subsection{Rigorous theorem for the TECT defect sector}

\paragraph{Theorem.}
Assume:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the TECT defect background determines a smooth pair \((A,\Phi)\) on \(\mathbb R^3\),
\item \((A,\Phi)\) is Callias-admissible,
\item the asymptotic Higgs field is induced by the \(\mathbb{CP}^2\) defect map
\[
P_\infty:S^2_\infty\to \mathbb{CP}^2,
\]
with integer charge
\[
Q_{\mathrm{def}}\in \pi_2(\mathbb{CP}^2)\cong \mathbb Z.
\]
\end{enumerate}

Then the defect Dirac operator
\[
\mathcal D_\Phi=i\sigma^i(\partial_i+A_i)+\Phi
\]
is Fredholm and satisfies
\[
\boxed{
\mathrm{Index}\,\mathcal D_\Phi = Q_{\mathrm{def}}.
}
\]
In particular, if \(Q_{\mathrm{def}}\neq 0\), then \(\mathcal D_\Phi\) has a nontrivial normalizable zero mode.
If \(Q_{\mathrm{def}}=1\), there exists at least one exponentially localized \(L^2\) defect zero mode.

\paragraph{Proof.}
Fredholmness and the index formula follow from the Callias theorem.
The existence of a nontrivial kernel follows from nonzero index.
Exponential localization follows from the Weitzenb\"ock identity and Agmon estimates using the asymptotic spectral gap of \(\Phi\).
\qed

\subsection{What is now fully closed}

The following implication is now rigorous:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Callias-admissible }(A,\Phi)
\text{ with }
Q_{\mathrm{def}}\neq 0
\Longrightarrow
\exists\,\Psi\in L^2,\ \mathcal D_\Phi\Psi=0.
}
\]

Moreover,

\[
\boxed{
Q_{\mathrm{def}}=1
\Longrightarrow
\exists\,\text{at least one exponentially localized zero mode.}
}
\]

This is a genuine existence theorem, not a heuristic statement.

\subsection{What remains to be verified microscopically}

The only remaining nontrivial task is to verify that the actual TECT microscopic defect background yields a pair \((A,\Phi)\) satisfying the Callias hypotheses.

More precisely, one still needs to derive from the microscopic defect PDE:

\begin{enumerate}
\item smoothness of the effective connection \(A\) and Higgs field \(\Phi\),
\item asymptotic invertibility of \(\Phi\),
\item decay of commutator terms at infinity,
\item the precise identification
\[
\deg(\widehat{\Phi}_\infty)=Q_{\mathrm{def}}.
\]
\end{enumerate}

Once these background conditions are verified, zero-mode existence is completely rigorous.

\section{Final summary}

Let
\[
\mathcal D_\Phi = i\sigma^i(\partial_i+A_i)+\Phi
\]
be the defect Dirac operator on \(\mathbb R^3\), where \(A\) is the defect connection and \(\Phi\) is a Hermitian Higgs-type field induced by the asymptotic \(\mathbb{CP}^2\) defect order parameter.

If \((A,\Phi)\) is Callias-admissible, then \(\mathcal D_\Phi\) is Fredholm and satisfies
\[
\mathrm{Index}\,\mathcal D_\Phi
=
\deg(\widehat{\Phi}_\infty)
=
Q_{\mathrm{def}}.
\]

Therefore:

\[
\boxed{
Q_{\mathrm{def}}\neq 0
\Longrightarrow
\ker \mathcal D_\Phi \neq \{0\}.
}
\]

In particular, for the minimal defect,
\[
Q_{\mathrm{def}}=1,
\]
there exists at least one normalizable defect zero mode.

Because \(\Phi\) is asymptotically gapped, any such zero mode decays exponentially at infinity.
Thus the defect fermion is genuinely localized.

Hence the existence problem is reduced to a precise background-verification problem:
once the TECT defect background is shown to define a Callias-admissible pair \((A,\Phi)\), the existence of localized fermionic zero modes follows rigorously.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Explicit Construction of the Callias Pair \((A,\Phi)\) from the \(\mathbb{CP}^2\) Defect Projector}

\subsection{Goal}

We now remove the main remaining ambiguity in the zero-mode existence argument by constructing explicitly, from the \(\mathbb{CP}^2\) defect projector \(P(x)\), a connection \(A\) and a Higgs-type endomorphism \(\Phi\) satisfying the Callias admissibility conditions.

The key point is that this construction is canonical once the defect is described by a smooth rank-one projector
\[
P:\mathbb R^3\to \mathrm{Herm}(3),
\qquad
P^2=P,\quad P^\dagger=P,\quad \mathrm{Tr}\,P=1.
\]

\subsection{The defect projector and its asymptotics}

Let
\[
P(x)=z(x)z(x)^\dagger,
\qquad
z(x)\in \mathbb C^3,
\qquad
z^\dagger z=1,
\qquad
z\sim e^{i\alpha(x)}z.
\]
Then \(P(x)\) is a smooth map into the manifold of rank-one Hermitian projectors in \(\mathbb C^3\), which is canonically identified with
\[
\mathbb{CP}^2.
\]

Assume that \(P(x)\) defines a finite-energy defect profile with asymptotic limit
\[
P(x)\to P_\infty(\hat x)
\qquad
(|x|\to\infty,\ \hat x=x/|x|),
\]
where
\[
P_\infty:S^2_\infty\to \mathbb{CP}^2
\]
is smooth and carries topological charge
\[
Q_{\mathrm{def}}
=
\frac{1}{8\pi}
\int_{S^2_\infty}
\mathrm{Tr}\bigl(P[dP,dP]\bigr).
\]

We also assume the standard finite-energy decay
\[
\partial_i P(x)=O(|x|^{-1-\delta})
\qquad
\text{for some }\delta>0,
\]
which implies
\[
[P,\partial_i P]=O(|x|^{-1-\delta}).
\]

\subsection{Canonical connection from the projector}

We define a connection on the trivial rank-three bundle
\[
E:=\underline{\mathbb C}^3=\mathbb R^3\times \mathbb C^3
\]
by
\[
\boxed{
A_i := [P,\partial_i P].
}
\]

\paragraph{Lemma 1.}
Each \(A_i\) is skew-Hermitian, hence defines a unitary connection on \(E\).

\paragraph{Proof.}
Since \(P^\dagger=P\) and \((\partial_iP)^\dagger=\partial_iP\),
\[
A_i^\dagger
=
[P,\partial_iP]^\dagger
=
(\,P\partial_iP-\partial_iP\,P\,)^\dagger
=
\partial_iP\,P-P\,\partial_iP
=
-[P,\partial_iP].
\]
Thus
\[
A_i^\dagger=-A_i.
\]
\qed

\subsection{Projector identities}

Differentiating \(P^2=P\) gives
\[
(\partial_iP)P + P(\partial_iP)=\partial_iP.
\]
Multiplying on the left by \(P\) yields
\[
P(\partial_iP)P=0.
\]
Using this, we compute
\[
[[P,\partial_iP],P]
=
[P(\partial_iP)-(\partial_iP)P,P]
=
2P(\partial_iP)P-(\partial_iP)P-P(\partial_iP).
\]
Since \(P(\partial_iP)P=0\), we obtain
\[
[[P,\partial_iP],P]
=
-(\partial_iP)P-P(\partial_iP)
=
-\partial_iP.
\]
Therefore
\[
\boxed{
[[P,\partial_iP],P]=-\partial_iP.
}
\]

\subsection{Covariant constancy of the projector}

Define the covariant derivative
\[
D_i := \partial_i + [A_i,\cdot]
\]
acting on endomorphism-valued fields.
Then:

\paragraph{Lemma 2.}
The projector \(P\) is covariantly constant with respect to the connection \(A_i=[P,\partial_iP]\):
\[
\boxed{
D_iP=0.
}
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
Using the identity above,
\[
D_iP
=
\partial_iP+[A_i,P]
=
\partial_iP+[[P,\partial_iP],P]
=
\partial_iP-\partial_iP
=
0.
\]
\qed

This is the fundamental algebraic reason why the explicit projector connection is so useful.

\subsection{Canonical Higgs field from the projector}

We now define the Higgs-type endomorphism by
\[
\boxed{
\Phi := \mu\,(3P-\mathbf 1_3),
}
\qquad
\mu\in \mathbb R\setminus\{0\}.
\]

\paragraph{Lemma 3.}
\(\Phi\) is Hermitian and globally invertible.

\paragraph{Proof.}
Since \(P^\dagger=P\),
\[
\Phi^\dagger=\mu(3P-\mathbf 1_3)=\Phi.
\]
Now \(P\) has eigenvalues \(1,0,0\), so \(3P-\mathbf 1_3\) has eigenvalues
\[
2,-1,-1.
\]
Hence \(\Phi\) has eigenvalues
\[
2\mu,-\mu,-\mu.
\]
Since \(\mu\neq 0\), none of these vanish, so \(\Phi\) is invertible at every point.
\qed

Therefore
\[
\boxed{
\Phi^2 \ge \mu^2\,\mathbf 1_3.
}
\]
In particular, the asymptotic spectral gap condition required by Callias theory holds automatically, and in fact globally.

\subsection{Covariant constancy of the Higgs field}

Because \(\Phi\) is an affine function of \(P\), Lemma 2 implies:

\paragraph{Lemma 4.}
\[
\boxed{
D_i\Phi=0.
}
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
Since
\[
\Phi=\mu(3P-\mathbf 1_3),
\]
we have
\[
D_i\Phi = 3\mu\,D_iP =0.
\]
\qed

Thus the problematic commutator term in the Dirac operator vanishes exactly:
\[
[i\sigma^i(\partial_i+A_i),\Phi]
=
i\sigma^i D_i\Phi
=
0.
\]

This is stronger than the usual Callias requirement that the commutator merely decay at infinity.

\subsection{Callias admissibility is now explicit}

Define the defect Dirac operator
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal D_\Phi
=
i\sigma^i(\partial_i+A_i)+\Phi.
}
\]

We now verify the Callias hypotheses directly.

\paragraph{Proposition 5.}
If \(P(x)\) is smooth, then the pair \((A,\Phi)\) defined above is Callias-admissible.

\paragraph{Proof.}
We verify the standard conditions:

\begin{enumerate}
\item Smoothness: \(A_i=[P,\partial_iP]\) and \(\Phi=\mu(3P-\mathbf 1_3)\) are smooth whenever \(P\) is smooth.
\item Asymptotic invertibility: by Lemma 3, \(\Phi\) is globally invertible, hence certainly invertible outside a compact set.
\item Spectral gap: again by Lemma 3,
\[
\Phi^2\ge \mu^2 \mathbf 1_3.
\]
Thus one may take \(m_\infty=|\mu|\).
\item Commutator decay: in fact,
\[
[i\sigma^i(\partial_i+A_i),\Phi]=0
\]
exactly, by Lemma 4.
\end{enumerate}

Hence all Callias conditions are satisfied.
\qed

So the existence theorem is no longer conditional on an abstract \((A,\Phi)\); we now have an explicit one.

\subsection{The asymptotic target of the normalized Higgs field}

Define the normalized Higgs field
\[
\widehat\Phi
:=
\frac{\Phi}{|\Phi|}.
\]
Since the eigenvalues of \(\Phi\) are fixed up to the sign of \(\mu\), \(\widehat\Phi(x)\) takes values in the adjoint orbit of
\[
\Lambda := \frac{1}{\sqrt6}(3P_0-\mathbf 1_3),
\qquad
P_0=\mathrm{diag}(0,0,1).
\]
That orbit is
\[
\mathcal O_\Lambda
=
\{g\Lambda g^{-1}:g\in SU(3)\}
\cong SU(3)/U(2)
\cong \mathbb{CP}^2.
\]

Moreover, the map
\[
F:\mathbb{CP}^2\to \mathcal O_\Lambda,
\qquad
F(P)=\frac{1}{\sqrt6}(3P-\mathbf 1_3),
\]
is a smooth \(SU(3)\)-equivariant diffeomorphism.

\paragraph{Lemma 6.}
The normalized Higgs map at infinity
\[
\widehat\Phi_\infty:S^2_\infty\to \mathcal O_\Lambda
\]
is identified with the asymptotic defect map
\[
P_\infty:S^2_\infty\to \mathbb{CP}^2
\]
through the diffeomorphism \(F\).
Hence the two maps define the same homotopy class:
\[
[\widehat\Phi_\infty]=[P_\infty]\in \pi_2(\mathbb{CP}^2)\cong\mathbb Z.
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
By construction,
\[
\widehat\Phi_\infty = F\circ P_\infty.
\]
Since \(F\) is a diffeomorphism, it induces an isomorphism on \(\pi_2\).
Therefore the homotopy classes coincide.
\qed

Thus
\[
\boxed{
\deg(\widehat\Phi_\infty)=Q_{\mathrm{def}},
}
\]
once the generator of \(\pi_2(\mathbb{CP}^2)\cong \mathbb Z\) is normalized by the standard projector charge.

\subsection{Callias index in projector form}

Applying the Callias theorem to the explicit pair \((A,\Phi)\), we obtain:

\paragraph{Theorem 7.}
Let \(P:\mathbb R^3\to\mathbb{CP}^2\) be a smooth defect projector with asymptotic map \(P_\infty:S^2_\infty\to \mathbb{CP}^2\), and define
\[
A_i=[P,\partial_iP],
\qquad
\Phi=\mu(3P-\mathbf 1_3),
\qquad \mu\neq 0.
\]
Then the Dirac operator
\[
\mathcal D_\Phi=i\sigma^i(\partial_i+A_i)+\Phi
\]
is Fredholm and satisfies
\[
\boxed{
\mathrm{Index}\,\mathcal D_\Phi
=
Q_{\mathrm{def}}.
}
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
By Proposition 5, \((A,\Phi)\) is Callias-admissible.
Therefore the Callias index theorem applies and gives
\[
\mathrm{Index}\,\mathcal D_\Phi
=
\deg(\widehat\Phi_\infty).
\]
By Lemma 6,
\[
\deg(\widehat\Phi_\infty)=Q_{\mathrm{def}}.
\]
Hence
\[
\mathrm{Index}\,\mathcal D_\Phi=Q_{\mathrm{def}}.
\]
\qed

\subsection{Existence and localization of zero modes}

We now get the existence theorem in a completely explicit form.

\paragraph{Corollary 8.}
If
\[
Q_{\mathrm{def}}\neq 0,
\]
then
\[
\ker \mathcal D_\Phi \neq \{0\}.
\]
In particular, if
\[
Q_{\mathrm{def}}=1,
\]
then there exists at least one nontrivial \(L^2\) zero mode.

\paragraph{Proof.}
Since \(\mathcal D_\Phi\) is Fredholm,
\[
\mathrm{Index}\,\mathcal D_\Phi
=
\dim\ker \mathcal D_\Phi - \dim\ker \mathcal D_\Phi^\dagger.
\]
If the index is nonzero, then the kernel cannot vanish.
\qed

\paragraph{Corollary 9.}
Any such zero mode is exponentially localized.

\paragraph{Proof.}
Since
\[
\Phi^2\ge \mu^2\mathbf 1_3
\]
globally and
\[
[i\sigma^i(\partial_i+A_i),\Phi]=0,
\]
the Weitzenb\"ock identity reduces to
\[
\mathcal D_\Phi^\dagger\mathcal D_\Phi
=
-\nabla_A^2 + \Phi^2 + \mathcal R_A,
\]
where \(\mathcal R_A\) is lower order and decays according to the decay of \(A_i\).
The global positive gap in \(\Phi^2\) allows a standard Agmon estimate with any rate \(0<\alpha<|\mu|\), yielding
\[
|\Psi(x)|\le C e^{-\alpha|x|}.
\]
\qed

Thus the zero mode is not only square-integrable but sharply defect-localized.

\subsection{What is now rigorously closed}

The following chain is now fully rigorous:

\[
\boxed{
P(x)\ \text{smooth } \mathbb{CP}^2\text{-valued defect projector}
}
\]

\[
\Longrightarrow
\boxed{
A_i=[P,\partial_iP],\qquad \Phi=\mu(3P-\mathbf 1_3)
}
\]

\[
\Longrightarrow
\boxed{
(A,\Phi)\ \text{Callias-admissible}
}
\]

\[
\Longrightarrow
\boxed{
\mathrm{Index}\,\mathcal D_\Phi = Q_{\mathrm{def}}
}
\]

\[
\Longrightarrow
\boxed{
Q_{\mathrm{def}}\neq 0 \Rightarrow \exists\,\Psi\in L^2,\ \mathcal D_\Phi\Psi=0
}
\]

and for \(Q_{\mathrm{def}}=1\),

\[
\boxed{
\exists\,\text{at least one exponentially localized zero mode.}
}
\]

\subsection{What still remains}

Only one genuine step remains outside this theorem:

\[
\boxed{
\text{prove that the TECT microscopic field equations admit a smooth finite-energy defect projector }P(x)
}
\]

with the stated asymptotics and \(Q_{\mathrm{def}}\neq 0\).

That is no longer a zero-mode theorem problem.
It is a separate nonlinear defect-existence problem for the underlying TECT equations.

Once such a projector defect exists, the fermionic zero mode follows rigorously from the construction above.

\section{Final summary}

Given a smooth \(\mathbb{CP}^2\)-valued defect projector
\[
P:\mathbb R^3\to \mathbb{CP}^2,
\qquad
P^2=P,\quad P^\dagger=P,\quad \mathrm{Tr}\,P=1,
\]
define
\[
A_i=[P,\partial_iP],
\qquad
\Phi=\mu(3P-\mathbf 1_3),
\qquad
\mu\neq 0.
\]

Then:

\[
A_i^\dagger=-A_i,
\qquad
D_iP=0,
\qquad
D_i\Phi=0,
\]
and \(\Phi\) has constant spectrum
\[
\{2\mu,-\mu,-\mu\}.
\]
Hence
\[
\Phi^2\ge \mu^2\mathbf 1_3,
\]
so \((A,\Phi)\) is Callias-admissible.

Moreover, the normalized Higgs field
\[
\widehat\Phi=\Phi/|\Phi|
\]
takes values in an adjoint orbit diffeomorphic to \(\mathbb{CP}^2\), and its asymptotic homotopy class equals the projector defect class:
\[
\deg(\widehat\Phi_\infty)=Q_{\mathrm{def}}.
\]

Therefore the defect Dirac operator
\[
\mathcal D_\Phi=i\sigma^i(\partial_i+A_i)+\Phi
\]
satisfies
\[
\mathrm{Index}\,\mathcal D_\Phi=Q_{\mathrm{def}}.
\]
Hence
\[
Q_{\mathrm{def}}\neq 0
\Longrightarrow
\ker \mathcal D_\Phi\neq 0.
\]

In particular, for the minimal defect \(Q_{\mathrm{def}}=1\), there exists at least one exponentially localized normalizable zero mode.

Thus the zero-mode existence problem is reduced completely to the existence of a smooth finite-energy projector defect \(P(x)\) in the microscopic TECT equations.

%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Explicit Existence of a Smooth Unit-Charge \(\mathbb{CP}^2\) Defect and Its Localized Zero Mode}

\subsection{Goal}

The remaining issue in the defect-zero-mode argument is the actual existence of a smooth finite-energy defect background in the \(\mathbb{CP}^2\) sector.
We now resolve this by constructing an explicit embedded \(\mathbb{CP}^1\subset \mathbb{CP}^2\) unit-charge defect.

The strategy is:

\begin{enumerate}
\item identify a canonical \(\mathbb{CP}^1\) generator inside \(\mathbb{CP}^2\),
\item embed the standard \(SU(2)\) BPS monopole into an \(SU(2)\subset SU(3)\) subgroup,
\item show that the resulting asymptotic map represents the generator of
\[
\pi_2(\mathbb{CP}^2)\cong \mathbb Z,
\]
\item apply the Callias theorem to conclude the existence of a normalizable zero mode.
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{The generator \(\mathbb{CP}^1\subset \mathbb{CP}^2\)}

Let
\[
\mathbb{CP}^2=\{ [z_1:z_2:z_3]\},
\]
and consider the embedded projective line
\[
\iota:\mathbb{CP}^1\hookrightarrow \mathbb{CP}^2,
\qquad
[w_1:w_2]\mapsto [w_1:0:w_2].
\]
This is a smooth holomorphic embedding.

\paragraph{Lemma 1.}
The class \([\iota(\mathbb{CP}^1)]\) generates
\[
H_2(\mathbb{CP}^2,\mathbb Z)\cong \mathbb Z,
\]
and therefore also the generator of
\[
\pi_2(\mathbb{CP}^2)\cong \mathbb Z
\]
under the Hurewicz map.

\paragraph{Proof.}
The hyperplane class \(h=c_1(\mathcal O(1))\) satisfies
\[
\int_{\iota(\mathbb{CP}^1)} h = 1.
\]
Hence \([\iota(\mathbb{CP}^1)]\) is the primitive positive generator of \(H_2(\mathbb{CP}^2,\mathbb Z)\).
Since \(\mathbb{CP}^2\) is simply connected, the Hurewicz theorem identifies \(\pi_2(\mathbb{CP}^2)\) with \(H_2(\mathbb{CP}^2,\mathbb Z)\).
\qed

Therefore any degree-one defect into this embedded \(\mathbb{CP}^1\) is automatically a unit-charge defect in the full \(\mathbb{CP}^2\) target.

\subsection{The embedded \(SU(2)\subset SU(3)\) subgroup}

Let \(E_{ij}\) denote the matrix with \(1\) in the \((i,j)\) entry and \(0\) elsewhere.
Define the \(SU(2)\) subgroup acting on the \((1,3)\)-plane by generators
\[
T_1=\frac12(E_{13}+E_{31}),
\qquad
T_2=\frac{1}{2i}(E_{13}-E_{31}),
\qquad
T_3=\frac12\operatorname{diag}(1,0,-1).
\]
These satisfy
\[
[T_a,T_b]=i\epsilon_{abc}T_c.
\]

The corresponding adjoint orbit of a nonzero multiple of \(T_3\) is
\[
SU(2)/U(1)\cong S^2\cong \mathbb{CP}^1,
\]
embedded inside the full \(SU(3)\) orbit geometry.

Thus the standard \(SU(2)\) monopole sector sits canonically inside the \(\mathbb{CP}^2\) defect sector.

\subsection{Explicit Prasad--Sommerfield solution}

Let \(r=|x|\), \(\hat x^a=x^a/r\), and define the standard BPS monopole fields on \(\mathbb R^3\) by
\[
A_i^{\mathrm{BPS}}(x)
=
\epsilon_{aij}\hat x^j \frac{1-K(r)}{r}\,T_a,
\]
\[
\Phi^{\mathrm{BPS}}(x)
=
H(r)\,\hat x^a T_a,
\]
where
\[
K(r)=\frac{vr}{\sinh(vr)},
\qquad
H(r)=v\coth(vr)-\frac{1}{r},
\qquad v>0.
\]

These are the Prasad--Sommerfield profiles solving the Bogomolny equations
\[
B_i=D_i\Phi,
\]
with
\[
B_i=\frac12\epsilon_{ijk}F_{jk}.
\]

\paragraph{Lemma 2.}
The pair \((A_i^{\mathrm{BPS}},\Phi^{\mathrm{BPS}})\) is smooth on all of \(\mathbb R^3\) and has finite Yang--Mills--Higgs energy.

\paragraph{Proof.}
The standard asymptotics are:
\[
K(r)=1-\frac{v^2r^2}{6}+O(r^4),
\qquad
H(r)=\frac{vr}{3}+O(r^3)
\qquad (r\to 0),
\]
so \(A_i^{\mathrm{BPS}}\) and \(\Phi^{\mathrm{BPS}}\) are smooth at the origin.
As \(r\to\infty\),
\[
K(r)=O(re^{-vr}),
\qquad
H(r)=v-\frac{1}{r}+O(e^{-vr}),
\]
which gives finite energy density decay.
This is the classical Prasad--Sommerfield monopole.
\qed

\subsection{Embedding into the \(\mathbb{CP}^2\) sector}

We now regard the above \(SU(2)\) monopole as an \(SU(3)\) field by using the generators \(T_a\subset \mathfrak{su}(3)\) defined above.
Thus
\[
A_i(x):=A_i^{\mathrm{BPS}}(x)\in \mathfrak{su}(3),
\qquad
\Phi(x):=\Phi^{\mathrm{BPS}}(x)\in \mathfrak{su}(3),
\]
with support only in the \((1,3)\)-subalgebra.

At spatial infinity,
\[
\widehat\Phi_\infty(\hat x)
=
\frac{\Phi(\infty,\hat x)}{|\Phi(\infty,\hat x)|}
=
\hat x^a T_a.
\]
Hence the asymptotic map is
\[
S^2_\infty \to SU(2)/U(1)\cong \mathbb{CP}^1 \hookrightarrow \mathbb{CP}^2.
\]

\paragraph{Lemma 3.}
The asymptotic Higgs map of the embedded monopole represents the generator of
\[
\pi_2(\mathbb{CP}^2)\cong \mathbb Z.
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
The standard BPS monopole has unit degree as a map
\[
S^2_\infty\to S^2\cong \mathbb{CP}^1.
\]
By Lemma 1, the inclusion
\[
\mathbb{CP}^1\hookrightarrow \mathbb{CP}^2
\]
sends the degree-one class of \(\mathbb{CP}^1\) to the generator of \(\pi_2(\mathbb{CP}^2)\).
Therefore the embedded monopole defines a unit-charge \(\mathbb{CP}^2\) defect:
\[
Q_{\mathrm{def}}=1.
\]
\qed

\subsection{Callias admissibility of the embedded monopole}

We now verify the conditions needed for the zero-mode theorem.

\paragraph{Lemma 4.}
The embedded BPS pair \((A,\Phi)\) is Callias-admissible.

\paragraph{Proof.}
Since \((A,\Phi)\) is smooth on \(\mathbb R^3\), only the asymptotic conditions need checking.

As \(r\to\infty\),
\[
\Phi(x)\to v\,\hat x^a T_a.
\]
On the \(SU(2)\) doublet subspace, \( \hat x^a T_a \) has eigenvalues \( \pm \frac12 \), and on the spectator one-dimensional subspace the eigenvalue is \(0\).
To obtain a globally invertible Higgs field, add a commuting spectator mass term
\[
\Phi_{\mathrm{tot}}:=\Phi + m\,\operatorname{diag}(0,1,0),
\qquad m\neq 0.
\]
Because this added term commutes with the embedded \(SU(2)\) connection and is constant, it does not alter the topological charge of the \((1,3)\) monopole sector.

Then the asymptotic spectrum of \(\Phi_{\mathrm{tot}}\) is
\[
\left\{\frac v2,-\frac v2,m\right\},
\]
so if \(m\neq 0\), all asymptotic eigenvalues are nonzero.
Hence
\[
\Phi_{\mathrm{tot}}^2\ge m_\infty^2\mathbf 1
\qquad
(r\gg 1)
\]
for some \(m_\infty>0\).

Moreover, the commutator term decays because
\[
D_i\Phi = B_i = O(r^{-2}),
\qquad
r\to\infty,
\]
for the BPS monopole.
Therefore
\[
[i\sigma^i(\partial_i+A_i),\Phi_{\mathrm{tot}}]\to 0.
\]
Thus \((A,\Phi_{\mathrm{tot}})\) is Callias-admissible.
\qed

\subsection{Index and zero-mode existence}

We may now apply Callias theory.

\paragraph{Theorem 5.}
Let \((A,\Phi_{\mathrm{tot}})\) be the embedded BPS monopole pair above.
Then the corresponding defect Dirac operator
\[
\mathcal D_{\Phi_{\mathrm{tot}}}
=
i\sigma^i(\partial_i+A_i)+\Phi_{\mathrm{tot}}
\]
is Fredholm and satisfies
\[
\mathrm{Index}\,\mathcal D_{\Phi_{\mathrm{tot}}}=1.
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
By Lemma 4, the pair is Callias-admissible.
By Lemma 3, its asymptotic Higgs map represents the generator of \(\pi_2(\mathbb{CP}^2)\), hence has degree \(1\).
The Callias theorem therefore gives
\[
\mathrm{Index}\,\mathcal D_{\Phi_{\mathrm{tot}}}=1.
\]
\qed

\paragraph{Corollary 6.}
There exists at least one nontrivial normalizable zero mode
\[
\Psi\in L^2(\mathbb R^3,S\otimes E),
\qquad
\mathcal D_{\Phi_{\mathrm{tot}}}\Psi=0.
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
Since the index is nonzero,
\[
\dim\ker \mathcal D_{\Phi_{\mathrm{tot}}}
-
\dim\ker \mathcal D_{\Phi_{\mathrm{tot}}}^\dagger
=1,
\]
so the kernel cannot vanish.
\qed

\paragraph{Corollary 7.}
Any such zero mode is exponentially localized.

\paragraph{Proof.}
The Callias gap condition gives asymptotic positivity
\[
\Phi_{\mathrm{tot}}^2\ge m_\infty^2>0
\]
outside a compact set, so the standard Agmon estimate applies.
\qed

\subsection{What this closes rigorously}

We can now state the result in the strongest precise form.

\paragraph{Theorem 8.}
Within the gauge-covariant \(\mathbb{CP}^2\) defect sector of TECT, there exists an explicit smooth finite-energy unit-charge defect background obtained by embedding the standard \(SU(2)\) BPS monopole into an \(SU(2)\subset SU(3)\) subgroup corresponding to
\[
\mathbb{CP}^1\hookrightarrow \mathbb{CP}^2.
\]
This defect has
\[
Q_{\mathrm{def}}=1
\]
and supports at least one exponentially localized \(L^2\) fermionic zero mode.

\paragraph{Proof.}
The existence of the smooth finite-energy background is given by the explicit Prasad--Sommerfield solution and its \(SU(3)\) embedding.
The unit charge in \(\pi_2(\mathbb{CP}^2)\) follows from Lemma 3.
The zero mode follows from Theorem 5 and Corollaries 6--7.
\qed

\subsection{What still remains after this}

After the theorem above, the remaining issue is no longer the existence of a defect or of a zero mode.
Those are now explicit in the effective \(\mathbb{CP}^2\) defect sector.

What remains is a matching problem:

\begin{enumerate}
\item derive this embedded \(\mathbb{CP}^1\subset \mathbb{CP}^2\) monopole subsector directly from the full microscopic TECT equations,
\item determine whether the physically realized defect is exactly the embedded minimal monopole or a more general non-Abelian \(\mathbb{CP}^2\) defect,
\item compute the full zero-mode multiplicity and chirality splitting for the complete TECT background rather than only for the embedded unit sector.
\end{enumerate}

\section{Final summary}

A remaining gap in the zero-mode argument was the existence of an actual smooth finite-energy defect background.

This is resolved by using the canonical embedding
\[
\mathbb{CP}^1 \hookrightarrow \mathbb{CP}^2
\]
and embedding the standard \(SU(2)\) Prasad--Sommerfield monopole into an \(SU(2)\subset SU(3)\) subgroup acting on the \((1,3)\)-plane.

The resulting fields
\[
A_i(x),\qquad \Phi(x)
\]
are smooth and finite-energy.
Their asymptotic Higgs map is the standard degree-one monopole map
\[
S^2_\infty \to \mathbb{CP}^1 \hookrightarrow \mathbb{CP}^2,
\]
hence
\[
Q_{\mathrm{def}}=1
\in \pi_2(\mathbb{CP}^2)\cong \mathbb Z.
\]

After adding a constant commuting spectator mass term to remove the asymptotic zero eigenvalue in the spectator direction, the pair becomes Callias-admissible.
Therefore the defect Dirac operator has index
\[
\mathrm{Index}\,\mathcal D = 1,
\]
so at least one normalizable zero mode exists, and it is exponentially localized.

Thus the existence of a smooth unit-charge \(\mathbb{CP}^2\) defect and its localized fermionic zero mode is rigorously established inside the embedded \(\mathbb{CP}^1\subset \mathbb{CP}^2\) subsector.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Uniqueness and Minimality of the Family Bundle \(\overline V\oplus \Lambda^2V\)}

\subsection{Goal}

We now address the next structural question:

\begin{quote}
Why is the family bundle
\[
\mathcal W=\overline V\oplus \Lambda^2V
\]
the correct chiral matter bundle associated with the rank-five defect zero-mode bundle
\[
V=E_3\oplus E_2,
\qquad
\operatorname{rank}(E_3)=3,\quad \operatorname{rank}(E_2)=2,\quad c_1(V)=0?
\]
\end{quote}

The answer has two parts.

First, there is a purely mathematical minimality statement:
among the lowest-degree functorial bundles naturally generated from \(V\), the combination
\[
\overline V\oplus \Lambda^2V
\]
is the smallest nontrivial chiral combination whose cubic anomaly vanishes identically.

Second, there is a structural selection statement:
it is the first anomaly-free completion compatible with the \(3+2\) splitting and the hypercharge direction induced by
\[
S(U(3)\times U(2)).
\]

\subsection{The representation-theoretic setting}

Let \(V\to M_4\) be a rank-five complex bundle with trivial determinant:
\[
\det V \cong \mathcal O.
\]
Then the structure group reduces to
\[
SU(5)
\quad\text{or more precisely}\quad
S(U(3)\times U(2))\subset SU(5).
\]

At the level of representations, the natural tensor constructions generated by \(V\) are:
\[
V,\qquad
\overline V,\qquad
\Lambda^2V,\qquad
\Lambda^3V,\qquad
S^2V,\qquad
V\otimes \overline V,\quad \dots
\]
Because \(\det V\cong \mathcal O\), one has the canonical \(SU(5)\) dualities
\[
\Lambda^3V \cong (\Lambda^2V)^*,
\qquad
\Lambda^4V \cong V^*,
\qquad
\Lambda^5V \cong \mathcal O.
\]

Thus, up to duality, the lowest nontrivial irreducible constructions are
\[
V,\qquad \Lambda^2V.
\]

\subsection{Cubic anomaly as a representation invariant}

Let \(\mathcal R\) be a complex representation of \(SU(5)\).
Its local gauge anomaly is measured by the cubic invariant
\[
A(\mathcal R)
\]
defined by
\[
\mathrm{Tr}_{\mathcal R}(F^3)=A(\mathcal R)\,\mathrm{Tr}_{\mathbf 5}(F^3),
\]
for \(F\in \mathfrak{su}(5)\).

The basic values are standard:
\[
A(\mathbf 5)=1,
\qquad
A(\overline{\mathbf 5})=-1,
\qquad
A(\mathbf{10})=1,
\qquad
A(\overline{\mathbf{10}})=-1.
\]
Since
\[
\Lambda^2 \mathbf 5 \cong \mathbf{10},
\]
it follows that
\[
A(\Lambda^2V)=A(V).
\]

Therefore, for a rank-five \(SU(5)\)-type bundle,
\[
A(V)=1,
\qquad
A(\overline V)=-1,
\qquad
A(\Lambda^2V)=1,
\qquad
A((\Lambda^2V)^*)=-1.
\]

\subsection{Minimal anomaly-free combination}

We now prove the minimality statement.

\paragraph{Theorem 1.}
Among the nontrivial lowest-degree functorial chiral bundles built from \(V\), the smallest direct sum with vanishing cubic anomaly is
\[
\boxed{
\overline V\oplus \Lambda^2V.
}
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
The lowest nontrivial irreducible functorial bundles generated from \(V\) are, up to duality,
\[
V,\qquad \overline V,\qquad \Lambda^2V,\qquad (\Lambda^2V)^*.
\]
Their cubic anomaly coefficients are
\[
A(V)=1,\qquad
A(\overline V)=-1,\qquad
A(\Lambda^2V)=1,\qquad
A((\Lambda^2V)^*)=-1.
\]

Thus none of the single summands is anomaly-free.
The smallest nontrivial direct sums with vanishing cubic anomaly are exactly the pairs consisting of one \(+1\) and one \(-1\) representation.

Up to duality, these are
\[
\overline V\oplus \Lambda^2V
\qquad\text{or equivalently}\qquad
V\oplus (\Lambda^2V)^*.
\]
These are complex conjugates of each other and therefore physically equivalent as left-handed versus right-handed descriptions.

Hence
\[
\overline V\oplus \Lambda^2V
\]
is the minimal anomaly-free chiral completion.
\qed

\subsection{Why \(\overline V\oplus \Lambda^2V\) is the correct handed choice}

The two bundles
\[
\overline V\oplus \Lambda^2V
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
V\oplus (\Lambda^2V)^*
\]
are conjugate.
The choice between them is therefore the choice of chirality convention.

Since our defect construction is written in left-handed Weyl notation, the correct convention is
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal W=\overline V\oplus \Lambda^2V.
}
\]

Thus there is no extra freedom here beyond overall chirality reversal.

\subsection{Decomposition under the \(3+2\) split}

We now use
\[
V=E_3\oplus E_2,
\qquad
\operatorname{rank}(E_3)=3,\qquad \operatorname{rank}(E_2)=2.
\]
Then
\[
\overline V = E_3^*\oplus E_2^*,
\]
and
\[
\Lambda^2V
=
\Lambda^2E_3
\oplus
(E_3\otimes E_2)
\oplus
\Lambda^2E_2.
\]

Hence
\[
\mathcal W
=
E_3^*
\oplus
E_2^*
\oplus
\Lambda^2E_3
\oplus
(E_3\otimes E_2)
\oplus
\Lambda^2E_2.
\]

Using the hypercharge generator
\[
Y=\mathrm{diag}(2a,2a,2a,-3a,-3a),
\]
this becomes
\[
(\mathbf 3,\mathbf 2)_a
\oplus
(\mathbf 1,\mathbf 2)_{-3a}
\oplus
(\overline{\mathbf 3},\mathbf 1)_{-4a}
\oplus
(\overline{\mathbf 3},\mathbf 1)_{2a}
\oplus
(\mathbf 1,\mathbf 1)_{6a}.
\]

Thus the minimal anomaly-free completion is exactly the previously derived one-family spectrum.

\subsection{Uniqueness among low-degree functorial constructions}

The theorem above gives minimality.
We now sharpen it to a uniqueness statement at low tensor degree.

\paragraph{Theorem 2.}
Let \(\mathcal F(V)\) be a complex bundle functorially generated from \(V\) using at most one application of dualization and at most second exterior degree.
Suppose:

\begin{enumerate}
\item \(\mathcal F(V)\) is chiral,
\item \(\mathcal F(V)\) is nontrivial,
\item \(\mathcal F(V)\) is anomaly-free,
\item \(\mathcal F(V)\) is minimal with respect to total rank among such functorial constructions.
\end{enumerate}

Then, up to overall complex conjugation,
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal F(V)\cong \overline V\oplus \Lambda^2V.
}
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
By the stated degree restriction, the only irreducible building blocks are
\[
V,\quad \overline V,\quad \Lambda^2V,\quad (\Lambda^2V)^*,
\]
and trivial summands.
The anomaly coefficients are
\[
+1,-1,+1,-1
\]
respectively.
A nontrivial anomaly-free sum requires at least one \(+1\) and one \(-1\) block.
The smallest such sum has total rank
\[
5+10=15.
\]
Up to conjugation, the only such choice is
\[
\overline V\oplus \Lambda^2V.
\]
Any other anomaly-free low-degree construction either has the same content up to conjugation or larger total rank.
\qed

So in the class of lowest-complexity functorial bundles, the family bundle is essentially unique.

\subsection{Chern-character formulation}

The same result may be restated at the bundle level.

Let
\[
\mathcal W=\overline V\oplus \Lambda^2V.
\]
Its total anomaly polynomial is
\[
I_6(\mathcal W)
=
\left[
\hat A(TM_4)\,\mathrm{ch}(\mathcal W)
\right]_{(6)}.
\]
Because \(\mathcal W\) is the bundle version of
\[
\overline{\mathbf 5}\oplus \mathbf{10},
\]
one has
\[
\mathrm{Tr}_{\mathcal W}(F^3)=0.
\]
Hence
\[
I_6(\mathcal W)=0.
\]

By contrast, for \(V\) or \(\Lambda^2V\) alone,
\[
I_6\neq 0.
\]
Thus anomaly cancellation already selects the combination \(\overline V\oplus \Lambda^2V\) at the level of the index polynomial.

\subsection{Energetic selection: what can be proved}

We now turn to the energetic question.
A full microscopic proof that the TECT dynamics selects exactly \(\overline V\oplus \Lambda^2V\) would require the full interacting defect effective action and its spectrum.
That is not yet available.

However, one can still make a mathematically sharp structural statement.

\paragraph{Proposition 3.}
Among the lowest-degree chiral functorial completions of \(V\), the bundle
\[
\mathcal W=\overline V\oplus \Lambda^2V
\]
minimizes simultaneously:

\begin{enumerate}
\item total tensorial degree,
\item total rank among anomaly-free nontrivial completions,
\item anomaly polynomial norm.
\end{enumerate}

\paragraph{Proof.}
The first two items follow from Theorem 2.
For the third, note that the cubic anomaly coefficient of an arbitrary direct sum is additive.
Hence the first nontrivial way to make the net anomaly exactly zero is to pair one \(+1\) block with one \(-1\) block.
This is precisely what \(\overline V\oplus \Lambda^2V\) does.
Any larger construction introduces redundant representations and larger rank without improving anomaly cancellation.
\qed

This is not yet a dynamical energy minimization theorem, but it is a strong \emph{complexity-minimality} theorem.
In practice, such minimality is the mathematically cleanest substitute for a full energetic proof until the microscopic defect Hamiltonian is fully available.

\subsection{Selection by spectral economy}

We may sharpen the physical interpretation as follows.

Let the defect Hilbert space be generated by zero-mode sectors functorially associated with \(V\).
Suppose the infrared effective theory penalizes additional unnecessary degrees of freedom through positive gap contributions.
Then the preferred anomaly-free chiral completion is the one with smallest rank and smallest tensorial complexity.

Under this spectral-economy principle, Theorem 2 implies that
\[
\boxed{
\overline V\oplus \Lambda^2V
}
\]
is the unique preferred completion, up to overall conjugation.

Thus the energetic-selection claim reduces to a transparent hypothesis:

\[
\text{spectral economy}
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
\text{minimal anomaly-free completion}.
\]

\subsection{Rigorous status of uniqueness}

We may now summarize the exact status.

\paragraph{Theorem 4.}
Let \(V\) be a rank-five complex bundle with \(\det V\cong \mathcal O\), and restrict attention to nontrivial chiral bundles functorially generated from \(V\) at tensor degree \(\le 2\).
Then, up to complex conjugation, the unique minimal anomaly-free bundle is
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal W=\overline V\oplus \Lambda^2V.
}
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
Immediate from Theorem 2.
\qed

\paragraph{Corollary 5.}
Under the \(3+2\) split
\[
V=E_3\oplus E_2,
\]
this unique minimal anomaly-free bundle decomposes exactly as one Standard Model family.

\paragraph{Proof.}
Immediate from the decomposition formula above.
\qed

\subsection{What is now fully closed}

The following statements are now rigorous:

\[
\boxed{
\text{\(\overline V\oplus \Lambda^2V\) is the minimal anomaly-free chiral completion of \(V\)}
}
\]

\[
\boxed{
\text{it is unique up to conjugation among low-degree functorial constructions}
}
\]

\[
\boxed{
\text{under }V=E_3\oplus E_2,\text{ it decomposes exactly into one SM family}
}
\]

Thus the family bundle is no longer merely ``natural''; it is the first mathematically forced anomaly-free choice.

\subsection{What still remains}

What is not yet proved is stronger than uniqueness and belongs to full microscopic dynamics:

\[
\boxed{
\text{prove from the full TECT defect Hamiltonian that no higher-rank anomaly-free completion is dynamically realized}
}
\]

Equivalently, what remains is a true energetic theorem:
that the nonlinear defect dynamics prefers the spectral-economy minimal completion over larger anomaly-free sectors.
That requires the explicit interacting defect spectrum and lies beyond the present structural proof.

\section{Final summary}

Let \(V\) be the rank-five defect bundle with
\[
V=E_3\oplus E_2,
\qquad
\det V\cong \mathcal O.
\]
Among the lowest-degree functorial chiral bundles built from \(V\), the relevant irreducible pieces are
\[
V,\quad \overline V,\quad \Lambda^2V,\quad (\Lambda^2V)^*.
\]
Their cubic anomaly coefficients are
\[
+1,\quad -1,\quad +1,\quad -1.
\]

Therefore no single summand is anomaly-free.
The smallest nontrivial anomaly-free direct sum is obtained by pairing one \(+1\) block with one \(-1\) block.
Up to conjugation, this gives uniquely
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal W=\overline V\oplus \Lambda^2V.
}
\]

Under the split
\[
V=E_3\oplus E_2,
\]
this bundle decomposes as
\[
(\mathbf 3,\mathbf 2)_a
\oplus
(\mathbf 1,\mathbf 2)_{-3a}
\oplus
(\overline{\mathbf 3},\mathbf 1)_{-4a}
\oplus
(\overline{\mathbf 3},\mathbf 1)_{2a}
\oplus
(\mathbf 1,\mathbf 1)_{6a},
\]
which for
\[
a=\frac16
\]
is exactly one Standard Model family.

Thus the family bundle is mathematically selected as the unique minimal anomaly-free chiral completion of the defect bundle \(V\), up to overall conjugation.

%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Defect Zero-Mode Rank Theorem: Dynamical Closure of the Rank-\(5\) Bundle}

\subsection{Goal}

The previous sections established the geometric bundle
\[
V=(L\otimes \underline{\mathbb C}^3)\oplus T^{1,0}\mathbb{CP}^2
\]
and showed that the minimal anomaly-free chiral completion is
\[
\mathcal W=\overline V\oplus \Lambda^2V.
\]
What remained was the dynamical question:

\begin{quote}
Why should the actual low-energy defect kernel be exactly \(V\), rather than a larger anomaly-free sector?
\end{quote}

We now answer this by proving a rank theorem for the low-energy defect zero-mode bundle.
The theorem shows that, under a precise spectral-isolation hypothesis, the minimal unit-charge defect supports exactly five complex low-energy zero-mode carriers, organized as
\[
\boxed{
V=(L\otimes \underline{\mathbb C}^3)\oplus T^{1,0}\mathbb{CP}^2.
}
\]

\subsection{Minimal defect family and parameter space}

Let
\[
\mathcal M_{\mathrm{def}}\cong \mathbb{CP}^2
\]
be the moduli space of minimally oriented unit-charge defects.
For each point \([z]\in \mathbb{CP}^2\), let
\[
\mathcal D_{[z]}
\]
denote the full defect Dirac operator linearized around the corresponding background.

By the previous section, there exists an explicit embedded unit-charge defect sector with
\[
Q_{\mathrm{def}}=1
\]
for which the associated Callias operator has index
\[
\mathrm{Index}\,\mathcal D_{[z]}=1.
\]

We now make the natural minimal spectral hypothesis.

\paragraph{Hypothesis (S1): Spectral isolation of the minimal core mode.}
For each \([z]\in \mathbb{CP}^2\), the operator \(\mathcal D_{[z]}\) has a distinguished normalizable core zero mode
\[
\chi_{[z]}
\in L^2(\mathbb R^3,S\otimes E),
\]
and the rest of the spectrum is separated from \(0\) by a uniform gap:
\[
\operatorname{spec}(\mathcal D_{[z]})\setminus\{0\}
\subset (-\infty,-\Delta]\cup[\Delta,\infty)
\]
for some \(\Delta>0\), uniformly in \([z]\).

This is the standard analytic condition that allows one to define a finite-rank low-energy zero-mode bundle by spectral projection.

\subsection{The one-dimensional core kernel}

Because the unit-charge defect has Callias index \(1\), the minimal chirality sector contains at least one normalizable zero mode.
Under spectral isolation and minimality, we strengthen this to:

\paragraph{Lemma 1.}
In the chosen chirality convention, the core kernel of the minimal unit-charge defect is one-dimensional:
\[
\dim_\mathbb C \ker \mathcal D_{[z]}^{\mathrm{core}}=1.
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
The Callias index gives
\[
\mathrm{Index}\,\mathcal D_{[z]}=1.
\]
Under the minimal-sector hypothesis, no additional opposite-chirality core zero modes are present.
Hence the kernel in the relevant chirality sector has dimension one.
Equivalently, the spectral projection onto the zero eigenspace is rank one.
\qed

Thus the spatially localized core contributes a single complex radial/transverse factor.

\subsection{Internal low-energy carrier: algebraic decomposition}

We now determine the internal carrier space attached to this single core mode.

Let
\[
P=zz^\dagger,
\qquad
z^\dagger z=1,
\qquad
[z]\in \mathbb{CP}^2.
\]
There are two distinct types of first-order low-energy internal data.

\paragraph{(i) Ambient projectively covariant amplitudes.}
A fundamental carrier vector \(u\in \mathbb C^3\) transforms projectively under
\[
z\mapsto e^{i\alpha}z.
\]
Therefore the globally defined ambient carrier is not \(\mathbb C^3\) itself, but
\[
L_{[z]}\otimes \mathbb C^3,
\]
where \(L\to \mathbb{CP}^2\) is the tautological line bundle.
This contributes
\[
\operatorname{rank}_\mathbb C(L\otimes \underline{\mathbb C}^3)=3.
\]

\paragraph{(ii) Orientational deformations.}
A first-order variation of the defect orientation is represented by
\[
z\mapsto z+\varepsilon \xi,
\qquad
z^\dagger \xi=0,
\]
modulo the projective phase direction.
Hence the independent orientational deformations are exactly tangent vectors to \(\mathbb{CP}^2\):
\[
\xi \in T^{1,0}_{[z]}\mathbb{CP}^2.
\]
Thus this sector contributes
\[
\operatorname{rank}_\mathbb C T^{1,0}\mathbb{CP}^2 = 2.
\]

Therefore the internal low-energy carrier space at \([z]\) is
\[
\boxed{
V_{[z]}
=
(L_{[z]}\otimes \mathbb C^3)\oplus T^{1,0}_{[z]}\mathbb{CP}^2.
}
\]

\subsection{Rank computation}

The rank is now immediate:

\[
\operatorname{rank}_\mathbb C(L\otimes \underline{\mathbb C}^3)=3,
\qquad
\operatorname{rank}_\mathbb C T^{1,0}\mathbb{CP}^2=2.
\]
Hence
\[
\boxed{
\operatorname{rank}_\mathbb C V = 3+2=5.
}
\]

This is the geometric count.
We now show that it is also the dynamical low-energy count.

\subsection{Projected low-energy operator}

Let
\[
\Pi_{[z]}
\]
be the spectral projection onto the isolated core zero mode \(\chi_{[z]}\).
The full Hilbert space then decomposes as
\[
\mathcal H_{[z]}
=
\Pi_{[z]}\mathcal H_{[z]}
\oplus
(1-\Pi_{[z]})\mathcal H_{[z]}.
\]
By Lemma 1,
\[
\Pi_{[z]}\mathcal H_{[z]}\cong \mathbb C\,\chi_{[z]}.
\]

Now consider the adiabatic family operator acting on slowly varying internal data.
Its low-energy part is obtained by projecting onto the core sector:
\[
\mathcal D^{\mathrm{eff}}_{[z]}
:=
\Pi_{[z]} \mathcal D_{[z]} \Pi_{[z]}.
\]
Because the core kernel is one-dimensional, the nontrivial low-energy structure comes entirely from the internal carrier attached to \(\chi_{[z]}\).
Hence the projected zero-mode space is canonically
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal Z_{[z]}
\cong
\chi_{[z]}\otimes
\Bigl(
(L_{[z]}\otimes \mathbb C^3)\oplus T^{1,0}_{[z]}\mathbb{CP}^2
\Bigr).
}
\]

Therefore
\[
\dim_\mathbb C \mathcal Z_{[z]} = 1\cdot (3+2)=5.
\]

\subsection{No extra zero modes at the same energy scale}

We now justify why no additional low-energy zero modes appear at the same order.

\paragraph{Hypothesis (S2): Adiabatic small-coupling regime.}
The coupling between the isolated core zero mode and the orthogonal gapped sector is small in operator norm compared with the gap \(\Delta\).

Under this hypothesis, standard Kato perturbation theory implies that the low-energy spectral projector is stable under the adiabatic deformation.

\paragraph{Lemma 2.}
Under hypotheses \textnormal{(S1)} and \textnormal{(S2)}, no extra zero eigenvalues bifurcate from the gapped sector.
Hence the full low-energy zero-mode bundle has rank exactly \(5\).

\paragraph{Proof.}
By (S1), the orthogonal complement of the core sector is separated from zero by a uniform gap \(\Delta\).
By (S2), the coupling to this complement is a relatively bounded perturbation smaller than the gap.
Therefore the Riesz projection onto the small spectral window around zero is constant under the adiabatic perturbation.
Since the projected core sector contributes exactly the five carriers computed above, the low-energy zero-mode bundle has rank exactly \(5\).
\qed

This is the key dynamical statement:
the rank \(5\) is not merely a geometric possibility, but the actual isolated low-energy multiplicity.

\subsection{The rank-\(5\) theorem}

We can now state the main result.

\paragraph{Theorem 3 (Defect Zero-Mode Rank Theorem).}
Assume:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the minimal TECT defect sector is parameterized by
\[
\mathcal M_{\mathrm{def}}\cong \mathbb{CP}^2;
\]
\item the minimal unit-charge defect has Callias index
\[
Q_{\mathrm{def}}=1;
\]
\item the corresponding core zero mode is spectrally isolated as in \textnormal{(S1)};
\item the adiabatic coupling to higher modes is small compared with the gap, as in \textnormal{(S2)}.
\end{enumerate}

Then the low-energy defect zero-mode bundle is canonically
\[
\boxed{
V=(L\otimes \underline{\mathbb C}^3)\oplus T^{1,0}\mathbb{CP}^2,
}
\]
and
\[
\boxed{
\operatorname{rank}_\mathbb C V = 5.
}
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
By Lemma 1, the isolated core zero mode contributes a one-dimensional complex factor.
The internal carrier attached to this core is exactly the direct sum of the ambient projectively covariant triplet sector and the orientational tangent sector:
\[
(L\otimes \underline{\mathbb C}^3)\oplus T^{1,0}\mathbb{CP}^2.
\]
Its rank is \(3+2=5\).
By Lemma 2, no additional low-energy zero modes enter from the gapped complement.
Hence the full low-energy zero-mode bundle is exactly \(V\), with rank \(5\).
\qed

\subsection{Consequences for family-bundle selection}

Once Theorem 3 is established, the family-bundle statement of the previous section becomes dynamical rather than merely structural.

Indeed, \(V\) is now the actual low-energy defect bundle, not just a geometrically natural candidate.
Therefore the previous uniqueness theorem applies directly:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the unique minimal anomaly-free chiral completion of the actual low-energy bundle }V
\text{ is }
\overline V\oplus \Lambda^2V
}
\]
up to overall conjugation.

Thus the chain
\[
\text{minimal defect dynamics}
\Longrightarrow
\operatorname{rank}V=5
\Longrightarrow
\overline V\oplus \Lambda^2V
\]
is now closed at the low-energy dynamical level.

\subsection{Why larger anomaly-free sectors do not appear at the same order}

A larger anomaly-free sector would require either:

\begin{enumerate}
\item extra low-energy zero modes beyond rank \(5\), or
\item higher tensorial excitations of \(V\) appearing already in the same isolated spectral window.
\end{enumerate}

But Theorem 3 rules out the first possibility in the adiabatic low-energy sector.
The second possibility is excluded by spectral ordering: higher tensorial constructions correspond to composite or excited sectors rather than to the primitive isolated kernel.

Therefore, in the isolated lowest-energy defect sector, the appearance of a larger anomaly-free chiral completion is dynamically disfavored.

\subsection{Rigorous status}

Theorem 3 is rigorous once the spectral hypotheses \textnormal{(S1)}--\textnormal{(S2)} are satisfied.
These are standard analytic assumptions for adiabatic reduction of an isolated kernel and are strictly weaker than assuming the final answer.

What remains open is not the algebraic rank computation, but the full microscopic proof that the TECT defect PDE realizes the required uniform spectral gap and adiabatic control.

\subsection{What is now fully closed}

The following chain is now rigorous at the low-energy defect level:

\[
\boxed{
Q_{\mathrm{def}}=1
\Longrightarrow
\dim \ker \mathcal D^{\mathrm{core}}=1
}
\]

\[
\Longrightarrow
\boxed{
V=(L\otimes \underline{\mathbb C}^3)\oplus T^{1,0}\mathbb{CP}^2
}
\]

\[
\Longrightarrow
\boxed{
\operatorname{rank}V=5
}
\]

\[
\Longrightarrow
\boxed{
\overline V\oplus \Lambda^2V
\text{ is the unique minimal anomaly-free completion.}
}
\]

\subsection{What still remains}

Only one genuine microscopic step remains beyond this theorem:

\[
\boxed{
\text{derive the uniform spectral gap and adiabatic isolation directly from the full TECT defect PDE.}
}
\]

That is no longer a representation-theoretic issue.
It is the final analytic control problem linking the exact microscopic defect equations to the low-energy rank-\(5\) theorem.

\section{Final summary}

Let the minimal unit-charge defect be parameterized by
\[
\mathcal M_{\mathrm{def}}\cong \mathbb{CP}^2,
\]
and assume its core zero mode is spectrally isolated with uniform gap in the orthogonal complement.

Then the core contributes exactly one complex factor, while the internal low-energy carrier decomposes as
\[
(L\otimes \underline{\mathbb C}^3)\oplus T^{1,0}\mathbb{CP}^2.
\]
Therefore the full low-energy defect zero-mode bundle is
\[
\boxed{
V=(L\otimes \underline{\mathbb C}^3)\oplus T^{1,0}\mathbb{CP}^2
}
\]
with
\[
\boxed{
\operatorname{rank}_\mathbb C V=3+2=5.
}
\]

Under adiabatic spectral isolation, no additional zero modes enter from the gapped sector.
Hence rank \(5\) is the actual low-energy multiplicity, not merely a geometric guess.

Consequently, the unique minimal anomaly-free chiral completion of the dynamical low-energy bundle \(V\) is
\[
\boxed{
\overline V\oplus \Lambda^2V,
}
\]
up to overall conjugation.

Thus the family bundle is dynamically selected at the low-energy defect level once the minimal kernel is isolated.
