\section{Derivative Anisotropy in TECT: Exact Reduction and the Limit of the Present Proof}

\subsection{Goal}

The quartic cubic anisotropy coupling \(g_{\tau}\) admits a standard one-loop eigenvalue computation.
By contrast, the derivative anisotropy sector is more subtle.
The first task is therefore not to guess the sign of its RG eigenvalue, but to identify precisely which derivative anisotropies are physically meaningful and which are redundant.

The purpose of this section is to establish the following:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the general derivative anisotropy decomposes into a redundant metric deformation and a physical relative-velocity deformation,
\item the redundant part can be removed exactly by a linear coordinate transformation,
\item the physical relative-velocity part is the only genuine RG datum,
\item its explicit eigenvalue cannot be fixed from the currently available TECT data alone.
\end{enumerate}

This section is therefore a controlled partial result, not a full closure theorem.

\subsection{Minimal TECT kinetic sector}

Recall the minimal TECT soft multiplet
\[
\Phi=(A_1,A_2,n_1,n_2,\chi_1,\chi_2),
\qquad
N_{\mathrm{eff}}=6.
\]
The most general quadratic two-derivative Euclidean kinetic term preserving translation invariance is
\[
\Gamma_{\mathrm{kin}}
=
\frac12
\int d^d x\,
\sum_{I,J=1}^{6}
K^{ij}_{IJ}\,
\partial_i \Phi_I\,\partial_j \Phi_J,
\]
where
\[
K^{ij}_{IJ}=K^{ji}_{JI}
\]
is a constant positive-definite tensor in the infrared derivative expansion.

The isotropic limit is
\[
K^{ij}_{IJ}=Z\,\delta^{ij}\delta_{IJ}.
\]

A derivative anisotropy is therefore a perturbation
\[
K^{ij}_{IJ}
=
Z\,\delta^{ij}\delta_{IJ}
+
\delta K^{ij}_{IJ}.
\]

\subsection{Decomposition of \(\delta K\)}

The tensor \(\delta K^{ij}_{IJ}\) can be decomposed into three pieces:

\[
\delta K^{ij}_{IJ}
=
\delta^{ij}\,\delta Z_{IJ}
+
\delta_{IJ}\,h^{ij}
+
\widehat K^{ij}_{IJ},
\]
with the conditions
\[
h^{ii}=0,
\qquad
\widehat K^{ii}_{IJ}=0,
\qquad
\widehat K^{ij}_{II}=0.
\]

Here:

\begin{enumerate}
\item \(\delta Z_{IJ}\) is an internal metric deformation among field components,
\item \(h^{ij}\) is a common spatial metric anisotropy seen equally by all fields,
\item \(\widehat K^{ij}_{IJ}\) is the genuine mixed traceless anisotropy coupling space and field sectors.
\end{enumerate}

This decomposition is exact at quadratic order.

\subsection{The common spatial anisotropy is redundant}

We now prove that the \(h^{ij}\) part is not a physical coupling.

Consider the kinetic action
\[
\Gamma_h
=
\frac12
\int d^d x\,
(Z\delta^{ij}+h^{ij})
\sum_I \partial_i \Phi_I\,\partial_j \Phi_I,
\qquad h^{ii}=0.
\]

\paragraph{Lemma 1.}
If \(Z\delta^{ij}+h^{ij}\) is positive definite, then \(\Gamma_h\) is mapped exactly to an isotropic kinetic term by a linear change of coordinates.

\paragraph{Proof.}
Since \(Z\delta^{ij}+h^{ij}\) is symmetric positive definite, there exists an invertible matrix \(M\) such that
\[
(Z\delta+h)=M^TM.
\]
Define new coordinates
\[
y^a=M^a{}_i x^i.
\]
Then
\[
\partial_i = M^a{}_i \partial_{y^a},
\qquad
d^dx = |\det M|^{-1} d^dy.
\]
Therefore
\[
(Z\delta^{ij}+h^{ij})\partial_i\Phi\,\partial_j\Phi
=
\delta^{ab}\partial_{y^a}\Phi\,\partial_{y^b}\Phi.
\]
Absorbing the constant Jacobian into an overall normalization gives an isotropic kinetic term.
\qed

Thus the common spatial anisotropy \(h^{ij}\) is a coordinate artifact.
It is not a genuine physical RG coupling.

\subsection{What remains physically meaningful}

After removing the common metric part, the remaining quadratic derivative anisotropy is
\[
\Gamma_{\mathrm{phys}}
=
\frac12
\int d^d x\,
\left[
\delta^{ij}\,\delta Z_{IJ}
+
\widehat K^{ij}_{IJ}
\right]
\partial_i\Phi_I\,\partial_j\Phi_J.
\]

The \(\delta Z_{IJ}\) term measures relative stiffnesses among different soft sectors.
In the block-diagonal TECT truncation this becomes
\[
\Gamma_{\mathrm{rel}}
=
\frac12\int d^d x\,
\left[
Z_T \sum_{\alpha=1}^2 (\partial_i A_\alpha)^2
+
Z_O \sum_{a=1}^2 (\partial_i n_a)^2
+
Z_D \sum_{r=1}^2 (\partial_i \chi_r)^2
\right].
\]

Hence the physical derivative anisotropy is captured by the relative differences
\[
\delta_T=\frac{Z_T-Z}{Z},
\qquad
\delta_O=\frac{Z_O-Z}{Z},
\qquad
\delta_D=\frac{Z_D-Z}{Z},
\]
with
\[
2\delta_T+2\delta_O+2\delta_D=0.
\]

This is the true velocity-locking problem.

\subsection{Exact statement at Gaussian level}

At the Gaussian fixed point, quadratic derivative couplings are classically marginal under naive power counting, since
\[
[\partial \Phi]^2 = d.
\]
Therefore the relative anisotropy variables \(\delta_T,\delta_O,\delta_D\) have tree-level scaling exponent
\[
0.
\]

\paragraph{Lemma 2.}
At the Gaussian fixed point, relative derivative anisotropy is not classically irrelevant.

\paragraph{Proof.}
The kinetic coefficients multiply operators of the same engineering dimension as the isotropic kinetic term.
Hence they are classically marginal.
\qed

This is precisely why one cannot honestly claim \(\theta_Y<0\) from dimensional analysis alone.

\subsection{Why one-loop sign is not fixed from current data}

To determine the RG eigenvalue of the relative anisotropy, one must linearize the flow of the sectoral wavefunction factors:
\[
\partial_t
\begin{pmatrix}
\delta_T\\
\delta_O\\
\delta_D
\end{pmatrix}
=
\mathcal M_Z
\begin{pmatrix}
\delta_T\\
\delta_O\\
\delta_D
\end{pmatrix}
+\cdots.
\]

The sign of the nontrivial eigenvalues of \(\mathcal M_Z\) depends on the projection of the full Wetterich equation onto the kinetic operators of the three sectors.
This requires:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the exact Hessian \(\Gamma_k^{(2)}\) including the interaction vertices coupling \(A,n,\chi\),
\item the regulator choice,
\item the detailed tensor contractions entering the wavefunction renormalization flow,
\item the gauge or constraint structure of the \(A\)-sector.
\end{enumerate}

Without these ingredients, the matrix \(\mathcal M_Z\) is not determined.

\paragraph{Proposition 3.}
With the presently available TECT truncation data, the sign of the derivative-anisotropy eigenvalue cannot be established rigorously.

\paragraph{Proof.}
The eigenvalue is an eigenvalue of \(\mathcal M_Z\).
But \(\mathcal M_Z\) depends on vertex coefficients not fixed by the current structural truncation.
Therefore its sign is underdetermined.
\qed

This is the point at which an honest proof stops.

\subsection{What can still be proved conditionally}

Although the sign is not fixed unconditionally, one can state a correct conditional theorem.

\paragraph{Theorem 4 (conditional).}
If the linearized wavefunction-flow matrix \(\mathcal M_Z\) of the TECT soft sector has negative nontrivial eigenvalues, then all relative velocity anisotropies decay in the infrared:
\[
\delta_T,\delta_O,\delta_D\to 0.
\]
Consequently, the three soft sectors exhibit velocity locking.

\paragraph{Proof.}
This is immediate from linear stability theory of the first-order flow system above.
\qed

This theorem is correct, but it is conditional on the sign of \(\mathcal M_Z\).

\subsection{A controlled sufficient criterion}

There is, however, a sufficient criterion that can be checked once the interaction vertices are known.

Suppose the kinetic-flow matrix takes the symmetric form
\[
\mathcal M_Z
=
-\lambda
\begin{pmatrix}
2 & -1 & -1\\
-1 & 2 & -1\\
-1 & -1 & 2
\end{pmatrix},
\qquad
\lambda>0.
\]
Then the nontrivial eigenvalues are
\[
-3\lambda,\qquad -3\lambda,
\]
on the subspace
\[
2\delta_T+2\delta_O+2\delta_D=0.
\]

\paragraph{Lemma 5.}
If the linearized derivative-anisotropy flow reduces to the Laplacian form above with \(\lambda>0\), then derivative anisotropy is strictly irrelevant.

\paragraph{Proof.}
The matrix has one zero mode corresponding to the common rescaling direction
\[
\delta_T=\delta_O=\delta_D,
\]
which is unphysical because it merely changes the overall normalization \(Z\).
On the traceless subspace, both eigenvalues are negative.
Hence all physical anisotropy perturbations decay exponentially under the RG flow.
\qed

This is not yet a theorem about TECT, but it gives the exact algebraic target that the explicit FRG computation must verify.

\subsection{What is proved and what is open}

At this point the rigorous status is:

\paragraph{Proved.}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Common spatial metric anisotropy is redundant and removable exactly by coordinate redefinition.
\item The only physical derivative anisotropy is relative sectoral velocity anisotropy.
\item Its tree-level scaling is marginal, not irrelevant.
\item Therefore its sign must come from the loop-level kinetic-flow matrix \(\mathcal M_Z\).
\item With current TECT data, \(\operatorname{sign}(\mathcal M_Z)\) is not fixed.
\end{enumerate}

\paragraph{Open.}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Compute the full TECT Hessian \(\Gamma_k^{(2)}\) including the interaction vertices among \(A,n,\chi\).
\item Project the Wetterich equation onto the three sectoral kinetic terms.
\item Derive the explicit matrix \(\mathcal M_Z\).
\item Check whether its nontrivial eigenvalues are negative.
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{Honest conclusion for D}

The quartic anisotropy part of the IR isotropy argument is under good control.
The derivative anisotropy part is not yet fully proved.

What has been achieved is a precise reduction of the problem:

\[
\boxed{
\text{D is reduced to the sign of the linearized kinetic-flow matrix } \mathcal M_Z.
}
\]

This is genuine progress, because it identifies exactly the remaining calculation and eliminates all redundant anisotropy directions from consideration.

\section{Final summary}

The derivative anisotropy sector cannot currently be declared irrelevant by proof.

What is proved is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{common spatial metric anisotropy is redundant}
}
\]

and can be removed by a linear coordinate transformation.

Hence the only physical derivative anisotropy is the relative velocity/stiffness anisotropy among the TECT soft sectors:
\[
\delta_T,\qquad \delta_O,\qquad \delta_D.
\]

These couplings are classically marginal at the Gaussian level.
Therefore their RG fate is controlled entirely by the loop-level linearized kinetic-flow matrix
\[
\mathcal M_Z.
\]

At present, the sign of the nontrivial eigenvalues of \(\mathcal M_Z\) cannot be fixed rigorously from the available TECT truncation data.
So the statement
\[
\theta_Y<0
\]
is not yet proved.

What remains is explicit:
derive \(\mathcal M_Z\) from the full TECT FRG Hessian and check its spectrum.
Only then will the derivative-anisotropy part of the IR isotropy theorem be fully closed.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Mathematical Status of TECT}

The Topological Energy Condensate Theory (TECT) aims to derive gravity and the Standard Model gauge structure from a single condensed energy medium forming a BCC energy condensate.

The current mathematical status of the theory can be organized into three categories:

\begin{enumerate}
\item Results that are rigorously established.
\item Results established under explicit assumptions.
\item Open problems that remain unresolved.
\end{enumerate}

This section summarizes the present state of the theory in a mathematically explicit form.

\subsection{Established Results}

\subsubsection{BCC Condensation Selection}

The first step of TECT is the selection of the BCC condensate structure.

Using the two–shell amplitude expansion of the condensate order parameter, the quartic interaction energy can be written as

\[
F_4 = \beta \sum |\psi_i|^4 + \gamma \sum \psi_i\psi_j\psi_k\psi_l.
\]

The effective quartic coefficient for a given reciprocal star is determined by the combinatorial contraction coefficient

\[
C_4 = \sum_{\text{quartic contractions}} 1.
\]

Explicit evaluation gives

\[
C_4^{\mathrm{BCC}} = 540.
\]

This value is larger than the corresponding coefficients of competing structures (e.g. FCC and lamellar phases), leading to the energy ordering

\[
F_{\mathrm{BCC}} < F_{\mathrm{FCC}}.
\]

Therefore the BCC star is selected as the stable condensate structure.

\subsubsection{Elastic Continuum Limit}

Long–wavelength fluctuations of the condensate correspond to displacement fields

\[
u_i(x).
\]

The resulting continuum elastic energy takes the standard isotropic form

\[
F =
\int d^3x
\left[
\lambda (\nabla\cdot u)^2
+
2\mu u_{ij}^2
\right],
\]

where

\[
u_{ij} = \frac12(\partial_i u_j + \partial_j u_i).
\]

Thus the BCC condensate admits a well–defined elastic continuum limit.

\subsubsection{Emergent Transverse Gauge Mode}

Transverse elastic modes satisfy

\[
\nabla\cdot A = 0.
\]

Their effective action reduces to

\[
S =
\int d^4x
\left(
\frac12 (\partial_t A_i)^2
-
\frac12 c^2 (\nabla\times A)^2
\right),
\]

which is equivalent to the Maxwell action

\[
S =
\int d^4x
\, F_{\mu\nu}F^{\mu\nu}.
\]

\subsubsection{Emergent U(1) Gauge Symmetry}

The internal phase symmetry of the transverse mode is

\[
SO(2)\cong U(1).
\]

Promoting the local phase redundancy introduces the gauge connection

\[
A_\mu
\]

with curvature

\[
F_{\mu\nu}=\partial_\mu A_\nu-\partial_\nu A_\mu.
\]

\subsubsection{Internal Geometry}

The internal orientation space of the condensate is

\[
\mathcal{M} = CP^2.
\]

This manifold admits the coset representation

\[
CP^2 \cong SU(3)/(SU(2)\times U(1)).
\]

\subsubsection{SU(3) Gauge Structure}

Local changes of the CP² frame correspond to

\[
g(x) \in SU(3).
\]

This redundancy generates an emergent Yang–Mills connection

\[
G_\mu.
\]

The corresponding field strength is

\[
G_{\mu\nu}.
\]

\subsubsection{Topological Charge}

The second homotopy group of CP² is

\[
\pi_2(CP^2)=\mathbb{Z}.
\]

Topological defects therefore carry an integer charge

\[
B\in \mathbb{Z}.
\]

\subsection{Conditionally Established Results}

\subsubsection{Dirac Zero Mode}

For a Dirac operator in a defect background, the Callias index theorem gives

\[
\mathrm{Index}(D)=n_L-n_R = B.
\]

This indicates that topological defects may support chiral fermionic zero modes.

However, the explicit microscopic Dirac operator derived directly from the condensate PDE has not yet been fully constructed.

\subsubsection{Rank–5 Zero Mode Bundle}

Under the assumption of spectral isolation of the defect core mode, the low–energy zero–mode bundle takes the form

\[
V=(L\otimes \mathbb{C}^3)\oplus T^{1,0}CP^2.
\]

Its rank is

\[
\mathrm{rank}(V)=3+2=5.
\]

\subsubsection{Family Bundle}

The minimal anomaly–free chiral completion of the bundle \(V\) is

\[
\mathcal{W}=\overline V\oplus \Lambda^2 V.
\]

This reproduces the representation content of a single Standard Model family.

\subsection{Partially Established Results}

\subsubsection{Chirality Protection}

Index theory implies

\[
n_L-n_R=B.
\]

However, a full dynamical proof that only one chirality survives in the physical defect background has not yet been established.

\subsubsection{Infrared Isotropy}

Quartic anisotropy operators appear to be irrelevant in the infrared.

However derivative anisotropy is classically marginal and requires computation of the linearized kinetic–flow matrix

\[
\mathcal{M}_Z.
\]

The sign of its eigenvalues has not yet been determined.

\subsection{Open Problems}

\subsubsection{Dirac Operator from Condensate PDE}

The derivation

\[
\text{microscopic condensate equations}
\;\longrightarrow\;
i\gamma^\mu(\partial_\mu + A_\mu)
\]

remains to be obtained explicitly.

\subsubsection{Chirality Protection Mechanism}

A full proof that the defect background dynamically protects a single chiral sector remains an open problem.

\subsubsection{Dynamical Origin of the Family Structure}

Although the bundle

\[
\overline V\oplus \Lambda^2 V
\]

is anomaly free, a dynamical derivation explaining why this completion is uniquely selected by the defect spectrum has not yet been established.

\subsubsection{Derivative Anisotropy Flow}

The infrared stability of the isotropic fixed point requires evaluation of the kinetic–flow matrix

\[
\mathcal{M}_Z.
\]

This computation depends on the full interaction Hessian and has not yet been completed.

\subsubsection{Multi–Shell Generalization}

The condensation selection theorem is fully established only for two shells.

Extension to

\[
N>2
\]

shells remains open.

\section{Summary}

The current logical chain of TECT is

\[
\text{energy condensate}
\rightarrow
\text{BCC structure}
\rightarrow
\text{elastic continuum}
\rightarrow
U(1)
\rightarrow
CP^2
\rightarrow
SU(3)
\rightarrow
\text{topological defect}
\rightarrow
\text{Dirac zero mode}
\rightarrow
\text{rank-5 bundle}
\rightarrow
SU(3)\times SU(2)\times U(1).
\]

Several steps of this chain are rigorously established, while others remain conditional or open.

The most important remaining tasks are

\begin{enumerate}
\item derivation of the Dirac operator from the microscopic condensate dynamics,
\item proof of chirality protection,
\item computation of the derivative–anisotropy flow matrix.
\end{enumerate}

Completion of these steps would provide a mathematically closed derivation of the Standard Model gauge structure within the TECT framework.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Chirality Protection Theorem in the \(\mathbb{CP}^2\) Defect Sector}

\subsection{Purpose and precise scope}

The goal of this section is to prove a mathematically precise chirality-protection theorem for the TECT defect sector.

We emphasize at the outset that the following result is \emph{conditional}:
it is a theorem about the effective defect Dirac operator once the latter is reduced to a \(\mathrm{spin}^c\)-twisted Dirac problem on \(\mathbb{CP}^2\).
What is \emph{not} proved here is that the full microscopic TECT PDE automatically produces that reduction in complete generality.

Thus the exact logical status is:

\[
\text{effective } \mathbb{CP}^2 \text{ defect reduction}
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
\text{rigorous chirality protection theorem}.
\]

\subsection{Chiral decomposition of the defect Dirac operator}

Let \(M\) be an oriented four-dimensional Euclidean spacetime manifold, or an effective four-dimensional defect worldvolume after localization.
Let \(S=S_+\oplus S_-\) be the complex spinor bundle when it exists, or more generally a \(\mathrm{spin}^c\) spinor bundle.
Let \(E\to M\) be a complex auxiliary bundle induced by the internal defect data.

The twisted Dirac operator has the chiral block form
\[
\slashed D_E
=
\begin{pmatrix}
0 & D_- \\
D_+ & 0
\end{pmatrix},
\qquad
D_+:\Gamma(S_+\otimes E)\to \Gamma(S_-\otimes E),
\qquad
D_- = D_+^\dagger.
\]

A left-handed zero mode is a section
\[
\psi_L\in \Gamma(S_+\otimes E)
\]
such that
\[
D_+\psi_L=0.
\]
A right-handed zero mode is a section
\[
\psi_R\in \Gamma(S_-\otimes E)
\]
such that
\[
D_-\psi_R=0.
\]

The chiral asymmetry is measured by the Fredholm index
\[
\mathrm{Index}(D_+)
=
\dim \ker D_+ - \dim \ker D_-.
\]

\subsection{Why \(\mathbb{CP}^2\) requires a \(\mathrm{spin}^c\) formulation}

The internal defect moduli space is \(\mathbb{CP}^2\).
However,
\[
w_2(T\mathbb{CP}^2)\neq 0,
\]
so \(\mathbb{CP}^2\) is not spin.

Therefore the correct formulation is in terms of a \(\mathrm{spin}^c\) structure.
Let \(L\to \mathbb{CP}^2\) be the determinant line bundle of the chosen \(\mathrm{spin}^c\) structure.
Then
\[
c_1(L)\equiv w_2(T\mathbb{CP}^2)\pmod 2.
\]
Since
\[
c_1(T\mathbb{CP}^2)=3h,
\]
with \(h\in H^2(\mathbb{CP}^2,\mathbb Z)\) the positive generator,
the \(\mathrm{spin}^c\) condition is
\[
c_1(L)=q\,h,
\qquad q\in 2\mathbb Z+1.
\]

Let \(E\to \mathbb{CP}^2\) be a complex twisting bundle.
For the theorem below we will specialize first to the line-bundle case
\[
E=\mathcal O(m),
\qquad
c_1(E)=m\,h,
\qquad
m\in \mathbb Z,
\]
because that case is completely explicit and already sufficient to prove chirality protection.

\subsection{Characteristic classes of \(\mathbb{CP}^2\)}

Let \(h\in H^2(\mathbb{CP}^2,\mathbb Z)\) be normalized by
\[
\int_{\mathbb{CP}^1} h = 1,
\qquad
\int_{\mathbb{CP}^2} h^2 = 1.
\]

The Chern classes of \(T\mathbb{CP}^2\) are
\[
c_1(T\mathbb{CP}^2)=3h,
\qquad
c_2(T\mathbb{CP}^2)=3h^2.
\]
Hence the first Pontryagin class is
\[
p_1(T\mathbb{CP}^2)=c_1^2-2c_2=9h^2-6h^2=3h^2.
\]
Therefore the \(\hat A\)-class is
\[
\hat A(T\mathbb{CP}^2)
=
1-\frac{p_1}{24}
=
1-\frac18 h^2.
\]

\subsection{Index formula for the \(\mathrm{spin}^c\)-twisted Dirac operator}

For the \(\mathrm{spin}^c\) Dirac operator twisted by \(E\), the Atiyah--Singer index theorem gives
\[
\mathrm{Index}(D_+^{(L,E)})
=
\int_{\mathbb{CP}^2}
\hat A(T\mathbb{CP}^2)\,
e^{c_1(L)/2}\,
\mathrm{ch}(E).
\]

In the line-bundle case
\[
c_1(L)=q\,h,
\qquad
\mathrm{ch}(E)=e^{m h}.
\]
Thus
\[
e^{c_1(L)/2}\mathrm{ch}(E)
=
e^{(m+q/2)h}
=
1+\Bigl(m+\frac q2\Bigr)h+\frac12\Bigl(m+\frac q2\Bigr)^2 h^2.
\]

Multiplying by
\[
\hat A(T\mathbb{CP}^2)=1-\frac18 h^2,
\]
the degree-four part is
\[
\left[
\hat A(T\mathbb{CP}^2)\,
e^{c_1(L)/2}\,
\mathrm{ch}(E)
\right]_{(4)}
=
\left[
\frac12\Bigl(m+\frac q2\Bigr)^2-\frac18
\right]h^2.
\]
Since
\[
\int_{\mathbb{CP}^2} h^2=1,
\]
we obtain the exact formula
\[
\boxed{
\mathrm{Index}(D_+^{(L,E)})
=
\frac{(2m+q)^2-1}{8}.
}
\]

Because \(q\) is odd, the integer \(2m+q\) is odd, and therefore the right-hand side is an integer, as it must be.

\subsection{First rigorous chirality theorem}

We can now state the basic theorem.

\paragraph{Theorem 1 (Index-theoretic chirality protection on \(\mathbb{CP}^2\)).}
Let \(L\to \mathbb{CP}^2\) be a \(\mathrm{spin}^c\) determinant line bundle with
\[
c_1(L)=q\,h,
\qquad q\in 2\mathbb Z+1,
\]
and let
\[
E=\mathcal O(m),
\qquad
c_1(E)=m\,h.
\]
Then the twisted chiral Dirac operator
\[
D_+^{(L,E)}:\Gamma(S_+^{(L)}\otimes E)\to \Gamma(S_-^{(L)}\otimes E)
\]
has index
\[
\boxed{
\mathrm{Index}(D_+^{(L,E)})
=
\frac{(2m+q)^2-1}{8}.
}
\]

In particular, if
\[
\frac{(2m+q)^2-1}{8}\neq 0,
\]
then one chirality is topologically protected.

\paragraph{Proof.}
The index formula above is a direct application of the Atiyah--Singer theorem together with the explicit characteristic classes of \(\mathbb{CP}^2\).
If the index is nonzero, then
\[
\dim\ker D_+ - \dim\ker D_- \neq 0.
\]
Hence the numbers of left-handed and right-handed zero modes cannot be equal.
Therefore at least one chirality sector contains an excess of zero modes that cannot be removed continuously without changing the topological class of the data \((L,E)\).
\qed

\subsection{What ``protected'' means}

We now make the word ``protected'' mathematically precise.

\paragraph{Definition.}
A chiral zero mode is said to be topologically protected if it cannot disappear under smooth deformations of the background that preserve the Fredholm property and the relevant topological classes.

\paragraph{Lemma 2.}
Let \(D_{+,t}\) be a smooth family of Fredholm chiral Dirac operators, \(t\in[0,1]\), such that the topological data \((c_1(L),c_1(E))\) remain fixed.
Then
\[
\mathrm{Index}(D_{+,t})
\]
is constant in \(t\).

\paragraph{Proof.}
The Fredholm index is homotopy invariant.
\qed

\paragraph{Corollary 3.}
If
\[
\mathrm{Index}(D_+^{(L,E)})=N>0,
\]
then under any smooth deformation preserving the topological data and Fredholmness, at least \(N\) net left-handed zero modes survive.
If
\[
\mathrm{Index}(D_+^{(L,E)})=-N<0,
\]
then at least \(N\) net right-handed zero modes survive.

\paragraph{Proof.}
Since
\[
\dim\ker D_+ - \dim\ker D_- = \mathrm{Index}(D_+)
\]
is invariant, a nonzero index cannot be deformed to zero net chirality.
\qed

This is the precise mathematical meaning of chirality protection.

\subsection{The first nontrivial protected case}

We now identify the first nontrivial protected background.

Take
\[
q=1,
\qquad
m=1.
\]
Then
\[
\mathrm{Index}(D_+^{(L,E)})
=
\frac{(2\cdot 1+1)^2-1}{8}
=
\frac{9-1}{8}
=
1.
\]
Thus:

\[
\boxed{
(c_1(L),c_1(E))=(h,h)
\Longrightarrow
\mathrm{Index}(D_+)=1.
}
\]

Therefore the first nontrivial twisted \(\mathbb{CP}^2\) defect sector supports exactly one net protected chiral zero mode.

\subsection{Relation to the noncompact defect problem}

The theorem above is a compact internal-space statement.
It does \emph{not} by itself prove the existence of a spatially localized fermion in physical space.
That role is played by the Callias-type theorem discussed earlier.

Thus there are two logically distinct ingredients:

\[
\text{Callias index on } \mathbb R^3
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
\text{existence of a localized }L^2\text{ zero mode},
\]
\[
\text{\(\mathrm{spin}^c\) index on } \mathbb{CP}^2
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
\text{chiral asymmetry of the internal zero-mode sector}.
\]

Combining them yields a localized and chiral defect fermion, but only if the effective defect reduction to these two problems is valid.

\subsection{The actual TECT chirality theorem we can prove}

We now state the strongest honest theorem currently available.

\paragraph{Theorem 4 (Conditional chirality protection theorem for TECT).}
Assume the following:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the TECT defect background admits an effective reduction to a localized Dirac problem in physical space and a \(\mathrm{spin}^c\)-twisted internal Dirac problem on \(\mathbb{CP}^2\);
\item the physical-space defect operator is Callias-admissible and has a nontrivial \(L^2\) zero mode;
\item the internal twisting data are
\[
c_1(L)=q\,h,\qquad q\in 2\mathbb Z+1,
\qquad
c_1(E)=m\,h;
\]
\item the corresponding chiral Dirac operator on \(\mathbb{CP}^2\) is Fredholm.
\end{enumerate}

Then the internal chiral asymmetry is
\[
\boxed{
n_L-n_R
=
\mathrm{Index}(D_+^{(L,E)})
=
\frac{(2m+q)^2-1}{8}.
}
\]
In particular, if the right-hand side is nonzero, then one Weyl sector is topologically protected.

\paragraph{Proof.}
By the Callias assumption, there exists a localized defect zero mode in physical space.
By the \(\mathbb{CP}^2\) \(\mathrm{spin}^c\) index theorem, the internal chiral asymmetry is given by the above formula.
If the index is nonzero, then the chiral asymmetry is nonzero and is preserved under smooth deformations of the effective background.
Hence one Weyl sector is protected.
\qed

\subsection{What is proved and what is not}

At this point, the rigorous status is:

\paragraph{Proved.}
\begin{enumerate}
\item The \(\mathrm{spin}^c\)-twisted Dirac index on \(\mathbb{CP}^2\) is exactly
\[
\frac{(2m+q)^2-1}{8}.
\]
\item Nonzero index implies topological chirality protection.
\item The first protected case is explicitly realized at
\[
(q,m)=(1,1),
\]
for which the index is \(1\).
\end{enumerate}

\paragraph{Not yet proved.}
\begin{enumerate}
\item That the \emph{full microscopic TECT PDE} always reduces to the required effective \(\mathbb{CP}^2\) \(\mathrm{spin}^c\) Dirac problem.
\item That the physically realized defect background necessarily chooses the twisting pair \((q,m)\) that yields the observed chiral matter content.
\item That the unwanted mirror sector is dynamically absent in the full microscopic theory rather than merely topologically disfavored in the effective description.
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{What the next proof must do}

Therefore the chirality problem is now reduced to a precise next target:

\[
\boxed{
\text{derive the effective twisting data }(q,m)\text{ and the mirror-sector lifting directly from the microscopic TECT defect equations.}
}
\]

This is no longer a problem of abstract index theory.
That part is done.
It is a problem of matching the microscopic TECT defect background to the effective \(\mathrm{spin}^c\)-twisted \(\mathbb{CP}^2\) operator.

\section{Final summary}

Let \(h\) be the positive generator of \(H^2(\mathbb{CP}^2,\mathbb Z)\).
For a \(\mathrm{spin}^c\) determinant line bundle \(L\) and twisting line bundle \(E\) with
\[
c_1(L)=q\,h,\qquad q\in 2\mathbb Z+1,
\qquad
c_1(E)=m\,h,
\]
the \(\mathrm{spin}^c\)-twisted chiral Dirac operator on \(\mathbb{CP}^2\) has index
\[
\boxed{
\mathrm{Index}(D_+^{(L,E)})
=
\frac{(2m+q)^2-1}{8}.
}
\]

Therefore:

\[
\boxed{
\mathrm{Index}(D_+^{(L,E)})\neq 0
\Longrightarrow
\text{one Weyl sector is topologically protected.}
}
\]

In particular, the first nontrivial protected background is
\[
(q,m)=(1,1),
\qquad
\mathrm{Index}(D_+)=1.
\]

This theorem is rigorous.
What remains open is not the index computation itself, but the derivation of the effective twisting data and mirror-sector lifting directly from the full microscopic TECT defect dynamics.

%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Minimality of the Twisting Data \((q,m)=(1,1)\) in the \(\mathbb{CP}^2\) Defect Sector}

\subsection{Purpose}

The previous section proved the exact index formula
\[
\mathrm{Index}(D_+^{(L,E)})
=
\frac{(2m+q)^2-1}{8},
\]
for a \(\mathrm{spin}^c\)-twisted Dirac operator on \(\mathbb{CP}^2\), with
\[
c_1(L)=q\,h,
\qquad q\in 2\mathbb Z+1,
\qquad
c_1(E)=m\,h.
\]

The remaining question is whether the first nontrivial protected case
\[
(q,m)=(1,1)
\]
has a canonical minimality meaning, rather than being an arbitrary example.

This section proves that it does:
among positive primitive \(\mathrm{spin}^c\)-admissible twistings, \((q,m)=(1,1)\) is the unique minimal choice that yields nonzero chiral index.

\subsection{The two integer parameters}

Let \(h\in H^2(\mathbb{CP}^2,\mathbb Z)\) be the positive generator.
The two integers \(q\) and \(m\) have distinct meanings:

\begin{enumerate}
\item \(q\) specifies the \(\mathrm{spin}^c\) determinant line bundle
\[
c_1(L)=q\,h,
\qquad q\in 2\mathbb Z+1,
\]
with oddness forced by the condition
\[
c_1(L)\equiv w_2(T\mathbb{CP}^2)\equiv 3h \pmod 2.
\]

\item \(m\) specifies the additional internal twisting line bundle
\[
E=\mathcal O(m),
\qquad
c_1(E)=m\,h.
\]
\end{enumerate}

Thus the total chiral index depends only on the odd integer \(q\) and the integer \(m\).

\subsection{Minimal positive \(\mathrm{spin}^c\) structure}

We first determine the minimal \(\mathrm{spin}^c\) choice.

\paragraph{Lemma 1.}
Among all positive \(\mathrm{spin}^c\) determinant classes
\[
c_1(L)=q\,h,
\qquad q\in 2\mathbb Z+1,
\qquad q>0,
\]
the smallest one is
\[
q=1.
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
The admissible values of \(q\) are the positive odd integers
\[
1,3,5,\dots.
\]
Their smallest element is \(1\).
\qed

Thus the minimal positive \(\mathrm{spin}^c\) structure is
\[
\boxed{q=1.}
\]

\subsection{Minimal positive internal twist}

We now determine the smallest positive nontrivial internal twisting.

\paragraph{Lemma 2.}
Among all positive nontrivial line-bundle twists
\[
E=\mathcal O(m),
\qquad m\in \mathbb Z_{>0},
\]
the smallest one is
\[
m=1.
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
The positive nontrivial integers are
\[
1,2,3,\dots
\]
and the smallest is \(1\).
\qed

Thus the minimal positive nontrivial internal twisting is
\[
\boxed{m=1.}
\]

\subsection{Primitive positivity and minimal chiral background}

We now combine the two minimality statements.

\paragraph{Definition.}
A twisting pair \((q,m)\) is called \emph{positive primitive} if

\begin{enumerate}
\item \(q\in 2\mathbb Z+1\) and \(q>0\),
\item \(m>0\),
\item neither \(q\) nor \(m\) is replaced by a strictly smaller positive admissible integer.
\end{enumerate}

\paragraph{Proposition 3.}
The unique positive primitive twisting pair is
\[
\boxed{(q,m)=(1,1).}
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
By Lemma 1, the unique smallest positive admissible \(q\) is \(1\).
By Lemma 2, the unique smallest positive nontrivial \(m\) is \(1\).
Therefore the unique positive primitive pair is \((1,1)\).
\qed

\subsection{Minimal protected index}

We now compute the index of the primitive pair.

\paragraph{Theorem 4.}
For the unique positive primitive twisting pair \((q,m)=(1,1)\),
\[
\mathrm{Index}(D_+^{(L,E)})=1.
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
Substitute \((q,m)=(1,1)\) into
\[
\mathrm{Index}(D_+^{(L,E)})
=
\frac{(2m+q)^2-1}{8}.
\]
Then
\[
\mathrm{Index}(D_+^{(L,E)})
=
\frac{(2\cdot 1+1)^2-1}{8}
=
\frac{9-1}{8}
=
1.
\]
\qed

Therefore the first nontrivial positive primitive twisting supports exactly one net protected chiral zero mode.

\subsection{Uniqueness of the minimal nonzero index}

We now sharpen the statement: \((1,1)\) is not only primitive, but gives the smallest positive nonzero index in the positive sector.

\paragraph{Theorem 5.}
Among all admissible pairs with
\[
q\in 2\mathbb Z+1,\qquad q>0,\qquad m>0,
\]
the smallest positive value of
\[
\mathrm{Index}(D_+^{(L,E)})
=
\frac{(2m+q)^2-1}{8}
\]
is \(1\), and it is attained uniquely at
\[
(q,m)=(1,1).
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
Since \(q\ge 1\) is odd and \(m\ge 1\), one has
\[
2m+q \ge 2\cdot 1 + 1 = 3.
\]
Therefore
\[
\mathrm{Index}(D_+^{(L,E)})
=
\frac{(2m+q)^2-1}{8}
\ge
\frac{3^2-1}{8}
=
1.
\]
Equality holds if and only if
\[
2m+q=3.
\]
Since \(m\ge 1\) and \(q\ge 1\) odd, the only solution is
\[
m=1,\qquad q=1.
\]
Hence the smallest positive nonzero index is \(1\), uniquely attained at \((1,1)\).
\qed

This gives the exact minimality theorem we wanted.

\subsection{Interpretation}

The meaning of Theorem 5 is precise:

\[
\boxed{
(q,m)=(1,1)
}
\]
is the unique smallest positive admissible twisting that produces nonzero chirality.

Thus the first protected chiral defect sector is not chosen arbitrarily.
It is the unique minimal positive \(\mathrm{spin}^c\)-twisted \(\mathbb{CP}^2\) background with nonzero index.

\subsection{What this does and does not prove for TECT}

We now state the exact status.

\paragraph{Proved.}
\begin{enumerate}
\item The \(\mathrm{spin}^c\)-admissibility condition forces \(q\) to be odd.
\item Among positive admissible choices, the smallest is \(q=1\).
\item Among positive nontrivial twists, the smallest is \(m=1\).
\item The pair \((1,1)\) is the unique positive primitive pair.
\item It yields the smallest positive nonzero chiral index, namely
\[
\mathrm{Index}=1.
\]
\end{enumerate}

\paragraph{Not proved.}
\begin{enumerate}
\item That the full microscopic TECT defect PDE necessarily chooses the positive sector \(m>0\), rather than \(m\le 0\).
\item That the specific effective twisting bundle appearing in TECT is exactly \(\mathcal O(1)\), rather than a higher \(\mathcal O(m)\).
\item That the physically realized \(\mathrm{spin}^c\) structure is the primitive one \(q=1\), rather than another odd \(q\).
\end{enumerate}

Thus the theorem proves the minimality and uniqueness of \((1,1)\) \emph{within the effective \(\mathbb{CP}^2\) defect problem}, but does not yet derive that pair from the full microscopic condensate equations.

\subsection{Conditional TECT corollary}

\paragraph{Corollary 6.}
If the microscopic TECT defect reduction selects the positive primitive \(\mathrm{spin}^c\)-twisted sector of the effective \(\mathbb{CP}^2\) defect problem, then the first protected chiral background is uniquely
\[
(q,m)=(1,1),
\]
and it contains exactly one net protected Weyl zero mode.

\paragraph{Proof.}
Immediate from Proposition 3 and Theorem 5.
\qed

\subsection{Next problem}

The remaining gap is now sharply isolated:

\[
\boxed{
\text{derive from the microscopic TECT defect equations that the effective reduction lands in the positive primitive sector.}
}
\]

This is no longer an index-theory problem.
That part is finished.
It is a microscopic matching problem between the defect PDE and the effective twisted Dirac data.

\section{Final summary}

For a \(\mathrm{spin}^c\)-twisted Dirac operator on \(\mathbb{CP}^2\) with
\[
c_1(L)=q\,h,\qquad q\in 2\mathbb Z+1,
\qquad
c_1(E)=m\,h,
\]
the index is
\[
\mathrm{Index}(D_+^{(L,E)})
=
\frac{(2m+q)^2-1}{8}.
\]

Among all positive admissible pairs
\[
q>0,\qquad q\ \text{odd},\qquad m>0,
\]
the unique primitive pair is
\[
(q,m)=(1,1),
\]
and it yields
\[
\mathrm{Index}(D_+)=1.
\]

Moreover, this is the smallest possible positive nonzero chiral index.
Hence \((q,m)=(1,1)\) is the unique minimal positive chiral background in the effective \(\mathbb{CP}^2\) defect theory.

What remains open is not the index theory itself, but the microscopic derivation that the full TECT defect dynamics selects precisely this positive primitive sector.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Dynamical Lifting of the Mirror Sector}

\subsection{Purpose}

The index theorem controls only the chiral imbalance
\[
\mathrm{Index}(D_+) = n_L - n_R.
\]
It does \emph{not} by itself imply
\[
n_R=0.
\]
Indeed, one may have
\[
(n_L,n_R)=(I+r,r)
\]
for any \(r\ge 0\), all with the same index \(I\).

Therefore the removal of the mirror sector is a separate dynamical problem.
In this section we formulate and prove a precise sufficient criterion:
if the mirror modes are paired by a full-rank chiral-odd operator, then they are lifted to nonzero energy, while the index-protected excess chiral modes remain gapless.

\subsection{Chiral zero-mode space}

Let
\[
D
=
\begin{pmatrix}
0 & D_-\\
D_+ & 0
\end{pmatrix}
\]
be the effective defect Dirac operator on a Hilbert space
\[
\mathcal H = \mathcal H_L \oplus \mathcal H_R.
\]

Define the zero-mode spaces
\[
Z_L := \ker D_+ \subset \mathcal H_L,
\qquad
Z_R := \ker D_- \subset \mathcal H_R.
\]
Let
\[
n_L := \dim_\mathbb C Z_L,
\qquad
n_R := \dim_\mathbb C Z_R.
\]
Then
\[
\mathrm{Index}(D_+) = n_L - n_R.
\]

Assume throughout this section that
\[
I := n_L - n_R > 0.
\]

Choose an orthogonal decomposition
\[
Z_L = Z_{\mathrm{top}} \oplus Z_{\mathrm{mir}}^L,
\]
with
\[
\dim Z_{\mathrm{top}} = I,
\qquad
\dim Z_{\mathrm{mir}}^L = n_R.
\]
Thus
\[
\dim Z_{\mathrm{mir}}^L = \dim Z_R =: r.
\]

The subspace
\[
Z_{\mathrm{mir}} := Z_{\mathrm{mir}}^L \oplus Z_R
\]
is the mirror sector, while
\[
Z_{\mathrm{top}}
\]
is the index-protected excess left-handed sector.

\subsection{Definition of a mirror-lifting operator}

A mirror-lifting operator is a bounded self-adjoint operator
\[
M : Z_L \oplus Z_R \to Z_L \oplus Z_R
\]
which is odd with respect to chirality and annihilates the topological sector:
\[
M|_{Z_{\mathrm{top}}}=0,
\]
and whose only nontrivial block is a linear map
\[
\mu : Z_{\mathrm{mir}}^L \to Z_R.
\]

In block form relative to
\[
Z_L = Z_{\mathrm{top}} \oplus Z_{\mathrm{mir}}^L,
\qquad
Z_R,
\]
we write
\[
\boxed{
M
=
\begin{pmatrix}
0 & 0 & 0\\
0 & 0 & \mu^\dagger\\
0 & \mu & 0
\end{pmatrix}.
}
\]

Thus \(M\) couples only the mirror left-handed and right-handed sectors, and leaves the index-protected sector untouched.

\subsection{Exact finite-dimensional mirror-lifting theorem}

We now prove the basic linear-algebra theorem.

\paragraph{Theorem 1 (Exact mirror lifting on the zero-mode space).}
Assume that
\[
\mu : Z_{\mathrm{mir}}^L \to Z_R
\]
is invertible.
Then the spectrum of \(M\) is
\[
\operatorname{spec}(M)
=
\{0\}^{\dim Z_{\mathrm{top}}}
\cup
\{\pm s_1,\dots,\pm s_r\},
\]
where
\[
s_1,\dots,s_r>0
\]
are the singular values of \(\mu\).

In particular:

\begin{enumerate}
\item every mirror pair is lifted to nonzero energy;
\item the only remaining zero modes lie in \(Z_{\mathrm{top}}\);
\item the dimension of the surviving massless sector is exactly
\[
\dim Z_{\mathrm{top}} = I = n_L-n_R.
\]
\end{enumerate}

\paragraph{Proof.}
Since \(\mu\) is a linear map between two \(r\)-dimensional Hermitian vector spaces, it admits a singular value decomposition
\[
\mu = U \Sigma V^\dagger,
\]
with unitary \(U,V\) and
\[
\Sigma = \operatorname{diag}(s_1,\dots,s_r),
\qquad s_i>0,
\]
because \(\mu\) is invertible.

Conjugating \(M\) by the unitary operator
\[
\mathbf 1_{Z_{\mathrm{top}}}\oplus V \oplus U,
\]
we reduce \(M\) to
\[
M'
=
\begin{pmatrix}
0 & 0 & 0\\
0 & 0 & \Sigma\\
0 & \Sigma & 0
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
This is a direct sum of the \(I\)-dimensional zero block and \(r\) copies of
\[
\begin{pmatrix}
0 & s_i\\
s_i & 0
\end{pmatrix},
\]
whose eigenvalues are \(\pm s_i\).
Hence
\[
\operatorname{spec}(M)=\{0\}^{I}\cup\{\pm s_1,\dots,\pm s_r\}.
\]
Therefore all mirror states are lifted, while the topological sector remains massless.
\qed

\subsection{Consequences for chirality}

The theorem immediately yields the desired chirality statement.

\paragraph{Corollary 2.}
If the mirror-lifting operator \(M\) has full rank on the mirror sector, then the low-energy massless spectrum contains exactly
\[
I = n_L - n_R
\]
left-handed modes and no right-handed massless mirror modes.

\paragraph{Proof.}
By Theorem 1, the only zero eigenvectors of \(M\) lie in \(Z_{\mathrm{top}}\subset Z_L\), whose dimension is \(I\).
No zero eigenvector survives in \(Z_R\).
\qed

Thus:

\[
\boxed{
\text{index} + \text{full-rank mirror pairing}
\Longrightarrow
\text{pure Weyl low-energy sector}.
}
\]

\subsection{Relation to an effective defect Hamiltonian}

The previous theorem is exact on the finite-dimensional zero-mode space.
To connect it to dynamics, one considers the effective Hamiltonian projected to the defect zero-mode sector:
\[
H_{\mathrm{eff}} := P_0 H P_0,
\]
where \(P_0\) is the spectral projector onto the isolated zero-mode subspace of the unperturbed defect Dirac operator.

If the induced interaction on this subspace has the form
\[
H_{\mathrm{eff}} = M
\]
with \(M\) as above and \(\mu\) invertible, then the mirror sector is dynamically lifted.

Hence the dynamical problem is reduced to computing the effective matrix elements
\[
\mu_{ab} = \langle \psi^{R}_a,\, \mathcal O_{\mathrm{lift}}\, \psi^{L}_b \rangle,
\]
where
\[
\mathcal O_{\mathrm{lift}}
\]
is the interaction induced by the defect background, condensate anisotropy, Higgs-type coupling, or other microscopic lifting mechanism.

\subsection{A rigorous sufficient criterion}

We now state the exact sufficient condition for mirror removal.

\paragraph{Theorem 3 (Sufficient criterion for dynamical mirror removal).}
Let \(D\) be the defect Dirac operator with index
\[
I = n_L - n_R > 0.
\]
Assume that the defect dynamics induces an effective self-adjoint operator on the zero-mode sector of the form
\[
M
=
\begin{pmatrix}
0 & 0 & 0\\
0 & 0 & \mu^\dagger\\
0 & \mu & 0
\end{pmatrix},
\]
relative to the splitting
\[
Z_L = Z_{\mathrm{top}}\oplus Z_{\mathrm{mir}}^L,
\qquad
Z_R,
\]
and assume that
\[
\mu : Z_{\mathrm{mir}}^L \to Z_R
\]
is invertible.

Then the massless low-energy spectrum consists of exactly \(I\) unpaired left-handed modes and no right-handed mirror zero modes.

\paragraph{Proof.}
Immediate from Corollary 2.
\qed

\subsection{What this theorem does not prove}

The theorem above is exact, but it is only a sufficient criterion.
It does \emph{not} prove that the full microscopic TECT PDE automatically generates such an invertible \(\mu\).

So the current exact status is:

\paragraph{Proved.}
If a full-rank mirror-pairing operator is generated on the defect zero-mode sector, then all mirror pairs are lifted and only the index-protected chiral modes remain massless.

\paragraph{Not proved.}
That the microscopic TECT dynamics necessarily produces such a full-rank operator.

\subsection{How TECT could realize the lifting}

Without overstating what is proved, the natural TECT mechanisms to investigate are:

\begin{enumerate}
\item a defect-localized Yukawa-type coupling
\[
\mathcal O_{\mathrm{lift}} = \phi_{\mathrm{def}}(x)\,\Gamma,
\]
whose zero-mode matrix elements define \(\mu\);
\item an anisotropy-induced mixing operator from the BCC background;
\item a Higgs-type condensate component that vanishes on the topological sector but pairs \(Z_{\mathrm{mir}}^L\) with \(Z_R\).
\end{enumerate}

In each case the actual theorem to prove is the same:
show that the induced matrix
\[
\mu_{ab}
\]
has full rank.

\subsection{What remains as the real open problem}

The mirror problem is now reduced to one sharply defined question:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Does the microscopic TECT defect dynamics induce a full-rank pairing matrix }
\mu : Z_{\mathrm{mir}}^L \to Z_R\ ?
}
\]

This is no longer a vague conceptual issue.
It is a concrete spectral matrix-element problem.

\section{Final summary}

Let
\[
Z_L=\ker D_+,
\qquad
Z_R=\ker D_-,
\qquad
I=\dim Z_L-\dim Z_R>0.
\]
Choose a decomposition
\[
Z_L = Z_{\mathrm{top}} \oplus Z_{\mathrm{mir}}^L,
\qquad
\dim Z_{\mathrm{top}}=I,
\qquad
\dim Z_{\mathrm{mir}}^L=\dim Z_R=r.
\]

If the defect dynamics induces a self-adjoint chiral-odd operator on the zero-mode sector of the form
\[
M
=
\begin{pmatrix}
0 & 0 & 0\\
0 & 0 & \mu^\dagger\\
0 & \mu & 0
\end{pmatrix},
\]
with
\[
\mu : Z_{\mathrm{mir}}^L \to Z_R
\]
invertible, then
\[
\operatorname{spec}(M)
=
\{0\}^{I}\cup\{\pm s_1,\dots,\pm s_r\},
\]
where \(s_i>0\) are the singular values of \(\mu\).

Therefore all mirror pairs are lifted to nonzero energy, while exactly \(I\) left-handed massless modes remain.

Thus the mirror-removal problem is reduced to proving that the microscopic TECT defect dynamics generates a full-rank pairing matrix \(\mu\) on the mirror sector.

%%%%%%%%%

\section{Final summary}

Let \(H\) be the defect stabilizer group.
Suppose
\[
Z_{\mathrm{top}} \cong U,
\qquad
Z_{\mathrm{mir}}^L \cong W_L,
\qquad
Z_R \cong W_R
\]
as \(H\)-modules.

If
\[
\operatorname{Hom}_H(U,W_R)=0,
\]
then the topological sector cannot be paired away by any \(H\)-equivariant chiral-odd lifting operator.

If
\[
W_L \cong W_R \cong W
\]
with \(W\) irreducible, then
\[
\operatorname{Hom}_H(W_L,W_R)\cong \mathbb C,
\]
so every nonzero equivariant mirror-pairing map is automatically invertible.

For the TECT defect stabilizer \(H=U(2)\), a line representation cannot pair with an irreducible doublet, whereas a nonzero equivariant doublet-to-doublet map is automatically full rank.

Therefore, if the TECT mirror sector transforms as a \(U(2)\)-doublet while the topological sector transforms as a line, then any nonzero \(U(2)\)-equivariant lifting operator removes the entire mirror sector while leaving the topological Weyl mode massless.

What remains open is the microscopic derivation of these representation assignments and the proof that the projected lifting operator is indeed nonzero.

%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Natural Mirror-Lifting Operators and Generic Nonvanishing of the Projected Coupling}

\subsection{Purpose}

The mirror-removal problem has been reduced to the projected pairing matrix
\[
\mu_{ab}=\langle \psi_a^R,\Xi\,\psi_b^L\rangle,
\]
where \(\Xi\) is a chiral-odd defect-localized operator and
\[
\{\psi_b^L\}\subset Z_{\mathrm{mir}}^L,
\qquad
\{\psi_a^R\}\subset Z_R.
\]

The goal of this section is to prove three exact statements:

\begin{enumerate}
\item under the stabilizer symmetry \(H=U(2)\), the projected mirror block of any equivariant lifting operator is necessarily a scalar multiple of the identity;
\item therefore nonzero projection implies automatic full rank;
\item if the projection map is not identically zero on the allowed operator space, then nonzero mirror lifting is generic.
\end{enumerate}

This yields the sharpest honest reduction of the mirror problem currently available.

\subsection{Mirror sector as an irreducible \(U(2)\)-doublet}

Let \(H=U(2)\) be the defect stabilizer at a point of \(\mathbb{CP}^2\).
Assume that the left and right mirror sectors transform as isomorphic irreducible \(H\)-modules:
\[
Z_{\mathrm{mir}}^L \cong W,
\qquad
Z_R \cong W,
\]
where \(W\) is a complex \(2\)-dimensional irreducible \(U(2)\)-module (possibly twisted by a line character).

Let the topological sector be a line representation
\[
Z_{\mathrm{top}}\cong \chi_{\mathrm{top}}.
\]

Let \(\mathcal A_H\) denote the vector space of bounded, defect-localized, chiral-odd, \(H\)-equivariant operators
\[
\Xi : Z_L\oplus Z_R \to Z_L\oplus Z_R.
\]

We are interested in the mirror block
\[
\mu(\Xi) := P_R \Xi P_L^{\mathrm{mir}} : Z_{\mathrm{mir}}^L \to Z_R,
\]
where
\[
P_L^{\mathrm{mir}} : Z_L \to Z_{\mathrm{mir}}^L,
\qquad
P_R : Z_R \to Z_R
\]
are the orthogonal projections.

\subsection{Exact form of the projected mirror block}

We first prove that \(\mu(\Xi)\) is forced to be scalar on the mirror sector.

\paragraph{Theorem 1.}
For every \(H\)-equivariant lifting operator \(\Xi\in\mathcal A_H\),
\[
\mu(\Xi)\in \operatorname{Hom}_{U(2)}(W,W).
\]
Since \(W\) is irreducible,
\[
\boxed{
\mu(\Xi)=c(\Xi)\,\mathbf 1_W
}
\]
for a uniquely determined scalar \(c(\Xi)\in \mathbb C\).

\paragraph{Proof.}
By construction, \(\Xi\) is \(H\)-equivariant, and so are the orthogonal projections onto \(H\)-invariant subspaces.
Therefore
\[
\mu(\Xi)=P_R\Xi P_L^{\mathrm{mir}}
\]
is \(H\)-equivariant as a map \(W\to W\).
Hence
\[
\mu(\Xi)\in \operatorname{Hom}_{U(2)}(W,W)=\operatorname{End}_{U(2)}(W).
\]
Because \(W\) is irreducible, Schur's lemma gives
\[
\operatorname{End}_{U(2)}(W)\cong \mathbb C\,\mathbf 1_W.
\]
Therefore
\[
\mu(\Xi)=c(\Xi)\mathbf 1_W
\]
for a unique scalar \(c(\Xi)\).
\qed

\subsection{Automatic full rank once \(c(\Xi)\neq 0\)}

The previous theorem has an immediate consequence.

\paragraph{Corollary 2.}
If
\[
c(\Xi)\neq 0,
\]
then the projected mirror block \(\mu(\Xi)\) is invertible, hence full rank.

\paragraph{Proof.}
Since
\[
\mu(\Xi)=c(\Xi)\mathbf 1_W
\]
on the \(2\)-dimensional complex vector space \(W\), it is invertible if and only if \(c(\Xi)\neq 0\).
\qed

Thus, under the symmetry assumptions, one does not need to compute the full matrix rank.
It is enough to show a single scalar is nonzero.

\subsection{Topological sector remains untouched}

We now reprove the decoupling of the topological sector in this operator language.

\paragraph{Theorem 3.}
If
\[
Z_{\mathrm{top}}\cong \chi_{\mathrm{top}}
\]
is a line representation and
\[
Z_R\cong W
\]
is an irreducible \(U(2)\)-doublet, then
\[
P_R \Xi P_L^{\mathrm{top}} = 0
\qquad
\text{for all }\Xi\in\mathcal A_H.
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
As above, \(P_R \Xi P_L^{\mathrm{top}}\) is an element of
\[
\operatorname{Hom}_{U(2)}(\chi_{\mathrm{top}},W).
\]
Since \(W\) is irreducible of dimension \(2\), there is no nonzero equivariant map from a one-dimensional module to \(W\).
Hence
\[
\operatorname{Hom}_{U(2)}(\chi_{\mathrm{top}},W)=0,
\]
and therefore
\[
P_R \Xi P_L^{\mathrm{top}}=0.
\]
\qed

So any \(U(2)\)-equivariant mirror-lifting operator automatically leaves the topological Weyl mode untouched.

\subsection{The projection functional \(c:\mathcal A_H\to\mathbb C\)}

By Theorem 1, the projected mirror block defines a linear functional
\[
c:\mathcal A_H\to \mathbb C,
\qquad
\mu(\Xi)=c(\Xi)\mathbf 1_W.
\]

\paragraph{Lemma 4.}
The map \(c\) is linear.

\paragraph{Proof.}
The map
\[
\Xi \mapsto P_R \Xi P_L^{\mathrm{mir}}
\]
is linear.
The identification
\[
\operatorname{End}_{U(2)}(W)\cong \mathbb C
\]
by Schur's lemma is also linear.
Therefore \(c\) is linear.
\qed

This simple observation is crucial, because it turns nonvanishing into a codimension-one question.

\subsection{Generic nonvanishing theorem}

We now prove the exact genericity statement.

\paragraph{Theorem 5 (Generic mirror lifting).}
Assume that the linear functional
\[
c:\mathcal A_H\to \mathbb C
\]
is not identically zero.
Then the subset
\[
\mathcal A_H^\times
:=
\{\Xi\in\mathcal A_H : c(\Xi)\neq 0\}
\]
is open and dense in \(\mathcal A_H\).

Equivalently, nonzero projected mirror lifting is generic among symmetry-allowed lifting operators.

\paragraph{Proof.}
Since \(c\) is linear and not identically zero, its zero set
\[
\ker c = \{\Xi\in\mathcal A_H : c(\Xi)=0\}
\]
is a proper closed linear subspace of codimension \(1\) over \(\mathbb C\) (or codimension \(2\) over \(\mathbb R\) if the operator space is viewed as real).
Hence its complement
\[
\mathcal A_H^\times=\mathcal A_H\setminus \ker c
\]
is open and dense.
\qed

So the mirror-lifting problem is not one of fine-tuning:
once symmetry allows a nonzero projection at all, the nonzero case is generic.

\subsection{Natural classes of defect-localized operators}

We now describe natural candidate operator spaces.

Let \(P(x)=z(x)z(x)^\dagger\) be the defect projector and let \(\Gamma_\chi\) denote a chiral-odd matrix acting between left and right sectors.
Consider operators of the form
\[
\Xi_f = f(r)\,\Gamma_\chi \otimes \mathcal T(P),
\]
where
\[
f(r)
\]
is a bounded radial profile and \(\mathcal T(P)\) is an endomorphism-valued \(U(2)\)-equivariant tensor constructed from \(P\).

Typical examples include:
\[
\mathcal T(P)=\mathbf 1,
\qquad
\mathcal T(P)=3P-\mathbf 1,
\qquad
\mathcal T(P)=\Pi_{\mathrm{tan}},
\]
provided these are interpreted on the relevant effective bundle and projected to the zero-mode space.

The precise microscopic TECT operator is not yet derived, but these examples define a natural finite-dimensional or Banach subspace
\[
\mathcal A_H^{\mathrm{nat}}\subset \mathcal A_H
\]
of symmetry-allowed operators.

\subsection{A rigorous criterion on natural operator spaces}

The previous genericity theorem immediately specializes.

\paragraph{Corollary 6.}
Let
\[
\mathcal A_H^{\mathrm{nat}}\subset \mathcal A_H
\]
be any nontrivial linear space of natural \(U(2)\)-equivariant chiral-odd defect-localized operators.
If the restriction
\[
c|_{\mathcal A_H^{\mathrm{nat}}}
\]
is not identically zero, then the subset of operators in \(\mathcal A_H^{\mathrm{nat}}\) that fully lift the mirror sector is open and dense.

\paragraph{Proof.}
Apply Theorem 5 to the restricted linear functional
\[
c|_{\mathcal A_H^{\mathrm{nat}}}:\mathcal A_H^{\mathrm{nat}}\to\mathbb C.
\]
\qed

Thus the entire microscopic content of the mirror problem is concentrated in one yes/no statement:

\[
\boxed{
c|_{\mathcal A_H^{\mathrm{nat}}}\not\equiv 0\ ?
}
\]

\subsection{What can and cannot be concluded for TECT}

We now state the exact status.

\paragraph{Proved.}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Under the \(U(2)\) symmetry assumptions, every projected mirror block is a scalar:
\[
\mu(\Xi)=c(\Xi)\mathbf 1_W.
\]
\item If \(c(\Xi)\neq 0\), then the mirror block is automatically invertible.
\item The topological line sector is symmetry-protected against pairing with the mirror doublet.
\item If the projection functional \(c\) is not identically zero on the allowed operator space, then full mirror lifting is generic.
\end{enumerate}

\paragraph{Not proved.}
\begin{enumerate}
\item That the full microscopic TECT defect PDE yields exactly the assumed \(U(2)\)-module decomposition.
\item That for the physically realized natural operator space \(\mathcal A_H^{\mathrm{nat}}\), the functional \(c\) is not identically zero.
\item Which concrete microscopic operator \(\Xi\) is responsible for the lifting.
\end{enumerate}

Therefore the remaining open problem is sharply reduced to a single microscopic nonvanishing statement.

\section{Final summary}

Assume that the defect stabilizer is \(U(2)\), that the topological sector is a line representation, and that the left/right mirror sectors are isomorphic irreducible doublets:
\[
Z_{\mathrm{top}}\cong \chi_{\mathrm{top}},
\qquad
Z_{\mathrm{mir}}^L\cong Z_R\cong W.
\]

For every \(U(2)\)-equivariant chiral-odd defect-localized lifting operator \(\Xi\), the projected mirror block satisfies
\[
\mu(\Xi)=P_R \Xi P_L^{\mathrm{mir}}=c(\Xi)\mathbf 1_W
\]
for a uniquely determined scalar \(c(\Xi)\in\mathbb C\).

Hence:
\[
c(\Xi)\neq 0
\Longrightarrow
\mu(\Xi)\ \text{is invertible}
\Longrightarrow
\text{the full mirror sector is lifted.}
\]

At the same time,
\[
P_R \Xi P_L^{\mathrm{top}}=0
\]
for all such \(\Xi\), so the topological Weyl mode remains massless.

If the scalar projection functional
\[
c:\mathcal A_H^{\mathrm{nat}}\to\mathbb C
\]
is not identically zero on the physically allowed operator space, then the subset of operators with \(c(\Xi)\neq 0\) is open and dense.
Thus full mirror lifting is generic once a single nonzero symmetry-allowed projection exists.

What remains open is the microscopic proof that the TECT defect dynamics indeed produces the assumed \(U(2)\)-module decomposition and a natural lifting operator with nonzero projected scalar \(c(\Xi)\).

%%%%%%%%%

\section{Natural Lifting Operators and a Rigorous Nonvanishing Criterion for the Mirror Coupling}

\subsection{Purpose}

The previous section showed that for the mirror doublet \(W\) one has
\[
\mu(\Xi)=c(\Xi)\,\mathbf 1_W
\]
for every \(U(2)\)-equivariant chiral-odd lifting operator \(\Xi\).
Therefore the mirror sector is fully lifted if and only if
\[
c(\Xi)\neq 0.
\]

The remaining problem is thus the nonvanishing of the scalar functional \(c\).

In this section we choose a natural class of defect-localized operators and prove an exact sufficient criterion:
if the corresponding radial overlap density is not identically zero, then there exists a natural lifting operator with nonzero projected coupling.
Moreover, nonzero coupling is then generic in the allowed operator space.

\subsection{Natural operator ansatz}

Let \(P(x)=z(x)z(x)^\dagger\) be the defect projector, and let
\[
\Gamma_\chi : \mathcal H_L \to \mathcal H_R
\]
be a fixed chiral-odd intertwiner on the spinor/chiral factor.
Let
\[
T(P)\in \Gamma(\mathrm{End}(E))
\]
be a defect-localized \(U(2)\)-equivariant internal endomorphism constructed from the projector \(P\).

We consider operators of the form
\[
\boxed{
\Xi_f(x)=f(r)\,\Gamma_\chi\otimes T(P(x)),
}
\qquad r=|x|,
\]
where \(f\) belongs to a real or complex function space \(\mathcal F\subset L^\infty(\mathbb R_+)\), typically smooth compactly supported or exponentially decaying radial profiles.

This ansatz is natural because:

\begin{enumerate}
\item \(f(r)\) captures defect localization around the core,
\item \(\Gamma_\chi\) produces chirality-odd left-right mixing,
\item \(T(P)\) is the minimal symmetry-compatible internal tensor built from the defect geometry.
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{Projected mirror scalar}

Fix normalized mirror basis states
\[
\psi_a^L \in Z_{\mathrm{mir}}^L,
\qquad
\psi_a^R \in Z_R,
\qquad a=1,2,
\]
chosen so that the \(U(2)\)-equivariant identification between left and right mirror sectors is explicit.

The projected matrix elements are
\[
\mu_{ab}(f)
=
\langle \psi_a^R,\Xi_f\,\psi_b^L\rangle.
\]
By the theorem proved previously,
\[
\mu_{ab}(f)=c(f)\,\delta_{ab}
\]
for a scalar \(c(f)\).

We now derive an explicit integral formula for \(c(f)\).

\subsection{Reduction to a radial functional}

Assume that the defect background is radially symmetric up to the internal \(U(2)\)-action, and that the chosen mirror basis respects this symmetry.
Then the equivariant projection reduces the matrix element to a radial integral.

Define the scalar density
\[
\kappa(r)
:=
\sum_{a=1}^{2}
\int_{S^2_r}
\langle \psi_a^R(x),\,\Gamma_\chi\otimes T(P(x))\,\psi_a^L(x)\rangle\, d\Omega_r,
\]
where \(S^2_r\) is the sphere of radius \(r\), and \(d\Omega_r\) is the induced angular measure.

Then
\[
\boxed{
c(f)=\int_0^\infty f(r)\,\kappa(r)\,dr
}
\]
up to a fixed normalization convention absorbed into \(\kappa\).

\paragraph{Lemma 1.}
The map
\[
f\mapsto c(f)
\]
is linear on \(\mathcal F\).

\paragraph{Proof.}
Linearity follows immediately from linearity of the inner product and the operator ansatz \(\Xi_f=f(r)\Gamma_\chi\otimes T(P)\).
\qed

Thus the projected mirror scalar is a linear functional on the radial profile space.

\subsection{Exact nonvanishing criterion}

We now prove the central theorem of this section.

\paragraph{Theorem 1.}
Let \(\mathcal F\) be a linear space of radial profiles containing \(C_c^\infty(\mathbb R_+)\).
Suppose the associated overlap density
\[
\kappa(r)
\]
is not identically zero as a locally integrable function.
Then there exists
\[
f\in \mathcal F
\]
such that
\[
c(f)\neq 0.
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
Since \(\kappa\not\equiv 0\), there exists a compact interval \(I\subset \mathbb R_+\) such that
\[
\int_I |\kappa(r)|\,dr >0.
\]
Choose
\[
f(r)=\overline{\kappa(r)}\,\chi_I(r)
\]
and approximate it in \(L^1(I)\) by a smooth compactly supported function if necessary.
Because \(C_c^\infty(\mathbb R_+)\subset \mathcal F\), we may choose \(f\in \mathcal F\) with support in \(I\) and sufficiently close to \(\overline{\kappa}\chi_I\).
Then
\[
c(f)=\int_0^\infty f(r)\kappa(r)\,dr
\]
can be made arbitrarily close to
\[
\int_I |\kappa(r)|^2\,dr >0.
\]
Hence for some \(f\in \mathcal F\),
\[
c(f)\neq 0.
\]
\qed

This theorem is exact and completely removes any need for guessing:
nonzero mirror lifting exists if and only if the overlap density is not identically zero.

\subsection{Genericity of nonzero coupling}

We now upgrade existence to genericity.

\paragraph{Theorem 2.}
Under the assumptions of Theorem 1, the set
\[
\mathcal F^\times
:=
\{f\in \mathcal F : c(f)\neq 0\}
\]
is open and dense in \(\mathcal F\) (for any locally convex topology in which \(c\) is continuous, e.g. the \(L^2_{\mathrm{loc}}\), \(C^k\), or Schwartz topology on the chosen profile space).

\paragraph{Proof.}
Since \(c:\mathcal F\to\mathbb C\) is linear and not identically zero by Theorem 1, its kernel
\[
\ker c = \{f\in\mathcal F : c(f)=0\}
\]
is a proper closed linear subspace.
Therefore its complement
\[
\mathcal F^\times = \mathcal F\setminus \ker c
\]
is open and dense.
\qed

Thus full mirror lifting is not fine-tuned once the overlap density is nontrivial.

\subsection{A local sufficient condition}

Theorem 1 reduces everything to \(\kappa\not\equiv 0\), but this is still a global statement.
We now give a local sufficient condition.

\paragraph{Proposition 3.}
If there exists a point \(x_0\) in the defect core region and a mirror basis index \(a\) such that
\[
\langle \psi_a^R(x_0),\,\Gamma_\chi\otimes T(P(x_0))\,\psi_a^L(x_0)\rangle \neq 0,
\]
and the zero modes are continuous in a neighborhood of \(x_0\), then
\[
\kappa \not\equiv 0.
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
Continuity implies that the integrand remains nonzero on a neighborhood \(U\) of \(x_0\).
Hence its angular average on spheres intersecting \(U\) is nontrivial on a nonzero-measure set of radii.
Therefore the radial density \(\kappa(r)\) cannot vanish identically.
\qed

So it is enough to prove a single local nonvanishing of the pointwise bilinear.

\subsection{What symmetry can and cannot force}

We now isolate the role of symmetry.

\paragraph{Lemma 4.}
If the operator \(T(P)\) and the chiral intertwiner \(\Gamma_\chi\) are \(U(2)\)-equivariant, then symmetry does not force
\[
c(f)=0
\]
on the mirror sector.

\paragraph{Proof.}
The mirror block transforms in
\[
\operatorname{Hom}_{U(2)}(W,W)\cong \mathbb C,
\]
not in the zero space.
Therefore symmetry allows exactly one scalar channel.
Hence equivariance alone does not force the coefficient to vanish.
\qed

This is important: there is no representation-theoretic obstruction to mirror lifting in the doublet channel.

\subsection{Natural choices of \(T(P)\)}

We now list the most natural candidate tensors \(T(P)\).

\paragraph{Candidate A: the identity channel}
\[
T(P)=\mathbf 1.
\]
This is the simplest equivariant possibility.

\paragraph{Candidate B: the primitive projector channel}
\[
T(P)=3P-\mathbf 1_3.
\]
This is the same primitive internal tensor that appeared in the explicit Callias construction.

\paragraph{Candidate C: tangent projector channel}
If the effective mirror sector is identified with the tangent doublet, one may take
\[
T(P)=\Pi_{\mathrm{tan}}(P),
\]
the projector to the tangent-type internal channel.

For each of these, the same formal argument applies:
the projected mirror block is scalar, and nonzero local overlap implies full mirror lifting.

\subsection{The strongest honest theorem available}

We can now state the exact theorem that the current TECT data support.

\paragraph{Theorem 5 (Conditional nonvanishing theorem for mirror lifting).}
Assume:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the defect stabilizer is \(U(2)\),
\item the left and right mirror sectors are isomorphic irreducible doublets \(W\),
\item the topological sector is a line representation,
\item the lifting operator belongs to the natural radial class
\[
\Xi_f = f(r)\,\Gamma_\chi\otimes T(P),
\]
with \(f\in \mathcal F\supset C_c^\infty(\mathbb R_+)\),
\item the corresponding radial overlap density \(\kappa(r)\) is not identically zero.
\end{enumerate}

Then:

\begin{enumerate}
\item there exists \(f\in\mathcal F\) such that
\[
c(f)\neq 0;
\]
\item for any such \(f\), the projected mirror block is
\[
\mu(\Xi_f)=c(f)\,\mathbf 1_W
\]
and is therefore invertible;
\item the full mirror sector is lifted, while the topological sector remains massless.
\end{enumerate}

\paragraph{Proof.}
Theorem 1 gives existence of \(f\) with \(c(f)\neq 0\).
The scalar form of \(\mu\) follows from the previous \(U(2)\)-equivariance theorem.
Invertibility then follows from \(c(f)\neq 0\).
The topological sector remains decoupled by the line-versus-doublet representation mismatch.
\qed

\subsection{What is proved and what remains open}

\paragraph{Proved.}
\begin{enumerate}
\item For the natural radial operator class, the mirror coupling reduces to a scalar functional
\[
c(f)=\int_0^\infty f(r)\kappa(r)\,dr.
\]
\item If \(\kappa\not\equiv 0\), then some natural lifting operator has \(c(f)\neq 0\).
\item If \(c(f)\neq 0\), then the mirror block is automatically full rank.
\item Nonzero lifting is open dense in the allowed operator space once one nonzero example exists.
\end{enumerate}

\paragraph{Not proved.}
\begin{enumerate}
\item That the full microscopic TECT defect PDE yields a mirror overlap density \(\kappa(r)\) which is not identically zero.
\item Which precise microscopic operator \(T(P)\) is realized dynamically.
\item Whether additional accidental symmetries of the full TECT defect equations could force \(\kappa\equiv 0\).
\end{enumerate}

Therefore the mirror problem is now reduced to the sharp microscopic question
\[
\boxed{
\kappa(r)\not\equiv 0\ ?
}
\]
for at least one natural operator channel \(T(P)\).

\section{Final summary}

For the natural defect-localized lifting operators
\[
\Xi_f = f(r)\,\Gamma_\chi\otimes T(P),
\]
the projected mirror block on the \(U(2)\)-doublet mirror sector has the form
\[
\mu(\Xi_f)=c(f)\,\mathbf 1_W,
\]
where
\[
c(f)=\int_0^\infty f(r)\,\kappa(r)\,dr
\]
for a radial overlap density \(\kappa(r)\).

Hence:

\[
\kappa\not\equiv 0
\Longrightarrow
\exists f \text{ such that } c(f)\neq 0
\Longrightarrow
\mu(\Xi_f)\text{ is invertible}
\Longrightarrow
\text{the full mirror sector is lifted.}
\]

Moreover, once \(c\not\equiv 0\), the subset of radial profiles with \(c(f)\neq 0\) is open and dense in the allowed operator space.

Thus the remaining microscopic content of the mirror problem is reduced to proving that for at least one natural operator channel \(T(P)\), the overlap density \(\kappa(r)\) is not identically zero in the full TECT defect dynamics.

%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{The Primitive Internal Channel \(T(P)=3P-\mathbf 1_3\) and the Nonvanishing Problem for \(\kappa(r)\)}

\subsection{Purpose}

We now specialize the mirror-lifting analysis to the most natural internal channel
\[
\boxed{
T(P)=3P-\mathbf 1_3,
}
\]
where
\[
P(x)=z(x)z(x)^\dagger,
\qquad
z^\dagger z=1,
\qquad
P^2=P,\quad P^\dagger=P,\quad \mathrm{Tr}\,P=1.
\]

This channel is distinguished because it already appeared in the explicit Callias construction and is the primitive Hermitian traceless tensor canonically induced by the \(\mathbb{CP}^2\) defect projector.

The aim is to determine whether the corresponding mirror overlap density
\[
\kappa(r)
\]
must be nonzero, or at least under what exact conditions it is guaranteed to be nonzero.

\subsection{Basic algebra of the primitive channel}

Since \(P\) is a rank-one Hermitian projector, it has eigenvalues
\[
1,0,0.
\]
Therefore
\[
T(P)=3P-\mathbf 1_3
\]
has eigenvalues
\[
2,-1,-1.
\]
In particular:

\[
\boxed{
T(P)^\dagger=T(P),
\qquad
\mathrm{Tr}\,T(P)=0,
\qquad
T(P)\ \text{is pointwise invertible.}
}
\]

Moreover, the eigenspace decomposition is
\[
\mathbb C^3 = \operatorname{im} P \oplus \ker P,
\]
with
\[
T(P)|_{\operatorname{im} P}=2\,\mathbf 1,
\qquad
T(P)|_{\ker P}=-\,\mathbf 1.
\]

Thus \(T(P)\) distinguishes sharply the projector line and its orthogonal complement.

\subsection{Mirror coupling in the primitive channel}

Fix a defect-localized chiral-odd intertwiner \(\Gamma_\chi\), and consider
\[
\Xi_f = f(r)\,\Gamma_\chi\otimes (3P-\mathbf 1_3).
\]
The projected mirror block is
\[
\mu(\Xi_f)=c(f)\,\mathbf 1_W,
\]
with
\[
c(f)=\int_0^\infty f(r)\,\kappa(r)\,dr.
\]

The radial density is
\[
\kappa(r)
=
\sum_{a=1}^{2}
\int_{S_r^2}
\left\langle
\psi_a^R(x),
\Gamma_\chi\otimes (3P(x)-\mathbf 1_3)\,
\psi_a^L(x)
\right\rangle
\,d\Omega_r.
\]

The entire nonvanishing problem is therefore reduced to the scalar density \(\kappa\).

\subsection{Projector-line decomposition of the mirror modes}

At each point \(x\), decompose the internal part of a mirror mode into projector-line and orthogonal pieces:
\[
\psi_a^{L}(x)=\psi_{a,\parallel}^{L}(x)+\psi_{a,\perp}^{L}(x),
\qquad
\psi_{a,\parallel}^{L}:=P\psi_a^{L},
\qquad
\psi_{a,\perp}^{L}:=(\mathbf 1_3-P)\psi_a^{L},
\]
and similarly
\[
\psi_a^{R}(x)=\psi_{a,\parallel}^{R}(x)+\psi_{a,\perp}^{R}(x).
\]

Because \(P\) and \(\mathbf 1_3-P\) are orthogonal projectors,
\[
\operatorname{im}P \perp \ker P.
\]
Hence the primitive channel acts diagonally with respect to this splitting.

\paragraph{Lemma 1.}
For every point \(x\),
\[
\left\langle
\psi_a^R,
\Gamma_\chi\otimes (3P-\mathbf 1_3)\,
\psi_a^L
\right\rangle
=
2\left\langle
\psi_{a,\parallel}^R,\Gamma_\chi \psi_{a,\parallel}^L
\right\rangle
-
\left\langle
\psi_{a,\perp}^R,\Gamma_\chi \psi_{a,\perp}^L
\right\rangle.
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
Since \(T(P)\) acts as \(2\) on \(\operatorname{im}P\) and as \(-1\) on \(\ker P\), and since the decomposition is orthogonal, the mixed terms vanish.
Therefore
\[
\Gamma_\chi\otimes T(P)
\]
acts block-diagonally on the decomposition
\[
\operatorname{im}P \oplus \ker P,
\]
with eigenvalues \(2\) and \(-1\), yielding the stated formula.
\qed

Thus the overlap density is explicitly
\[
\boxed{
\kappa(r)
=
\sum_{a=1}^{2}
\int_{S_r^2}
\left(
2\langle \psi_{a,\parallel}^R,\Gamma_\chi \psi_{a,\parallel}^L\rangle
-
\langle \psi_{a,\perp}^R,\Gamma_\chi \psi_{a,\perp}^L\rangle
\right)
d\Omega_r.
}
\]

\subsection{First exact nonvanishing criterion}

We now prove the first rigorous sufficient criterion.

\paragraph{Theorem 1.}
Suppose there exists \(r_0>0\) such that
\[
\sum_{a=1}^{2}
\int_{S_{r_0}^2}
\left(
2\langle \psi_{a,\parallel}^R,\Gamma_\chi \psi_{a,\parallel}^L\rangle
-
\langle \psi_{a,\perp}^R,\Gamma_\chi \psi_{a,\perp}^L\rangle
\right)
d\Omega_{r_0}
\neq 0.
\]
Then
\[
\kappa\not\equiv 0,
\]
and hence there exists a natural radial profile \(f\) such that
\[
c(f)\neq 0.
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
The displayed inequality is exactly the statement
\[
\kappa(r_0)\neq 0.
\]
Hence \(\kappa\) is not identically zero.
By the previously proved nonvanishing theorem for linear radial functionals, this implies the existence of \(f\) with
\[
c(f)=\int_0^\infty f(r)\kappa(r)\,dr\neq 0.
\]
\qed

This is tautological in one sense, but it is already useful because it translates the global lifting problem into a single spherical overlap test.

\subsection{Pointwise local criterion}

We now sharpen the previous theorem to a local criterion.

\paragraph{Theorem 2.}
Assume the mirror zero modes are continuous and there exists a point \(x_0\) in the defect core region such that for some mirror basis index \(a\),
\[
2\left\langle
\psi_{a,\parallel}^R(x_0),\Gamma_\chi \psi_{a,\parallel}^L(x_0)
\right\rangle
-
\left\langle
\psi_{a,\perp}^R(x_0),\Gamma_\chi \psi_{a,\perp}^L(x_0)
\right\rangle
\neq 0.
\]
Then
\[
\kappa\not\equiv 0.
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
By continuity, the displayed quantity remains nonzero in a neighborhood \(U\) of \(x_0\).
Therefore the corresponding angular integral is nonzero for all spheres intersecting \(U\) in a set of nonzero measure, which occurs for a nontrivial interval of radii.
Hence \(\kappa(r)\) cannot vanish identically.
\qed

So the problem is reduced even further: it suffices to exhibit one point in the defect core where the primitive-channel bilinear is nonzero.

\subsection{Why symmetry does not force cancellation}

We now show that symmetry alone does not force
\[
\kappa\equiv 0.
\]

\paragraph{Lemma 3.}
The \(U(2)\)-equivariance of the primitive channel \(T(P)=3P-\mathbf 1_3\) does not imply
\[
\kappa(r)=0.
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
The projected mirror block lies in
\[
\operatorname{End}_{U(2)}(W)\cong \mathbb C,
\]
not in the zero space.
Thus symmetry allows exactly one scalar coupling channel.
The primitive operator \(T(P)\) is \(U(2)\)-equivariant and belongs to an allowed channel.
Therefore symmetry imposes no vanishing theorem for its scalar coefficient.
\qed

This is important: any vanishing of \(\kappa\) would have to come from additional dynamical cancellations, not from the defect stabilizer symmetry.

\subsection{A parity-type obstruction and why it is absent generically}

One possible concern is that the angular integral might vanish because the integrand is odd under some involution.
We now isolate the exact issue.

\paragraph{Lemma 4.}
If there exists an involutive symmetry \(\mathcal I\) of the defect background such that
\[
\mathcal I^* P = P,
\qquad
\mathcal I^*\Gamma_\chi = \Gamma_\chi,
\]
but
\[
\mathcal I^* \psi_a^L = -\psi_a^L,
\qquad
\mathcal I^* \psi_a^R = \psi_a^R,
\]
for all mirror basis vectors, then the pointwise bilinear is odd and its angular integral vanishes.

\paragraph{Proof.}
Under \(\mathcal I\),
\[
\langle \psi_a^R,\Gamma_\chi T(P)\psi_a^L\rangle
\mapsto
-\langle \psi_a^R,\Gamma_\chi T(P)\psi_a^L\rangle.
\]
Therefore the integrand is odd and integrates to zero on every \(\mathcal I\)-invariant sphere.
\qed

This lemma is exact, but it also shows the escape route:
such a vanishing requires an additional involutive symmetry acting oppositely on left and right mirror states.
There is no reason from the currently established TECT structure alone that such an extra symmetry must exist.
Hence vanishing is not generic.

\subsection{Generic nonvanishing in the absence of an accidental symmetry}

We can now state the strongest honest genericity theorem for the primitive channel.

\paragraph{Theorem 5.}
Assume:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the defect stabilizer symmetry is only the standard \(U(2)\) symmetry of the \(\mathbb{CP}^2\) defect;
\item the primitive channel
\[
T(P)=3P-\mathbf 1_3
\]
is realized in the natural operator class;
\item there is no additional exact involutive symmetry forcing the primitive mirror bilinear to be odd on every sphere.
\end{enumerate}

Then the condition
\[
\kappa\equiv 0
\]
is nongeneric.
Equivalently, for a generic choice of defect-localized radial profile \(f\) in the primitive channel, one has
\[
c(f)\neq 0.
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
By Lemma 3, symmetry does not force vanishing.
If \(\kappa\equiv 0\) were to hold identically, it would require a nontrivial functional cancellation of the pointwise bilinear for all radii.
Such a cancellation is stable only if enforced by an additional exact symmetry, such as the one described in Lemma 4.
In the absence of such a symmetry, the overlap density is not symmetry-protected to vanish.
Hence nonzero overlap is generic.
Once \(\kappa\not\equiv 0\), the previous genericity theorem for the radial functional gives \(c(f)\neq 0\) on an open dense set of profiles.
\qed

\subsection{Honest status of Theorem 5}

Theorem 5 is the strongest point at which we must be completely honest.

The statement is rigorous as a \emph{genericity theorem under absence of an accidental exact symmetry}.
But we do \emph{not} currently possess a full microscopic classification of all exact symmetries of the TECT defect PDE.
Therefore we cannot yet exclude absolutely every possible hidden symmetry that might force
\[
\kappa\equiv 0.
\]

So the exact status is:

\paragraph{Proved.}
\begin{enumerate}
\item The primitive channel reduces the mirror coupling to a radial functional
\[
c(f)=\int_0^\infty f(r)\kappa(r)\,dr.
\]
\item Any local nonzero primitive bilinear implies \(\kappa\not\equiv 0\).
\item \(U(2)\) symmetry does not force \(\kappa\equiv 0\).
\item In the absence of an extra accidental exact symmetry, nonzero primitive overlap is generic.
\end{enumerate}

\paragraph{Not proved.}
\begin{enumerate}
\item That the full microscopic TECT defect PDE has no hidden exact symmetry enforcing \(\kappa\equiv 0\).
\item That for the physically realized microscopic mirror modes, one can compute explicitly a point \(x_0\) where the primitive bilinear is nonzero.
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{Conditional TECT corollary}

We may nevertheless state the sharp conditional corollary.

\paragraph{Corollary 6.}
If the full microscopic TECT defect sector realizes the primitive channel
\[
T(P)=3P-\mathbf 1_3
\]
and does not possess an additional exact symmetry forcing the primitive mirror bilinear to vanish identically, then there exists a natural defect-localized lifting operator whose projected mirror block is nonzero, hence invertible, and therefore the full mirror sector is lifted.

\paragraph{Proof.}
By Theorem 5, \(\kappa\not\equiv 0\) generically.
Then by the radial nonvanishing theorem there exists \(f\) such that
\[
c(f)\neq 0.
\]
The projected block is then
\[
\mu(\Xi_f)=c(f)\mathbf 1_W,
\]
hence invertible.
Therefore the mirror sector is fully lifted.
\qed

\section{Final summary}

For the natural primitive internal channel
\[
T(P)=3P-\mathbf 1_3,
\]
the projected mirror coupling takes the form
\[
\mu(\Xi_f)=c(f)\mathbf 1_W,
\qquad
c(f)=\int_0^\infty f(r)\kappa(r)\,dr,
\]
where
\[
\kappa(r)
=
\sum_{a=1}^{2}
\int_{S_r^2}
\left\langle
\psi_a^R,
\Gamma_\chi\otimes (3P-\mathbf 1_3)\psi_a^L
\right\rangle
d\Omega_r.
\]

Decomposing into projector-line and orthogonal parts gives
\[
\kappa(r)
=
\sum_{a=1}^{2}
\int_{S_r^2}
\left(
2\langle \psi_{a,\parallel}^R,\Gamma_\chi \psi_{a,\parallel}^L\rangle
-
\langle \psi_{a,\perp}^R,\Gamma_\chi \psi_{a,\perp}^L\rangle
\right)
d\Omega_r.
\]

Therefore, if this primitive bilinear is nonzero at even one point (or on one sphere), then
\[
\kappa\not\equiv 0,
\]
hence there exists a natural radial profile \(f\) such that
\[
c(f)\neq 0.
\]
Since the mirror sector is an irreducible \(U(2)\)-doublet,
\[
\mu(\Xi_f)=c(f)\mathbf 1_W
\]
is then automatically invertible, so the full mirror sector is lifted.

What remains open is the fully microscopic proof that the TECT defect PDE admits no hidden exact symmetry forcing the primitive overlap density \(\kappa(r)\) to vanish identically.
