\section{Current Status of Chirality Protection and Mirror Lifting in TECT}

\subsection{Purpose}

We summarize here the exact mathematical status of the chirality problem in TECT.
The goal is to distinguish clearly between:

\begin{enumerate}
\item what has been rigorously established,
\item what has been proved only conditionally,
\item what remains genuinely open at the microscopic level.
\end{enumerate}

The central issue is whether the defect sector yields a genuinely chiral low-energy fermion spectrum rather than an index-protected sector contaminated by mirror pairs.

\subsection{Step 1: Index-theoretic chirality protection on \(\mathbb{CP}^2\)}

The internal defect moduli space is
\[
\mathbb{CP}^2,
\]
which is not spin but admits \(\mathrm{spin}^c\) structures.
Let
\[
c_1(L)=q\,h,
\qquad q\in 2\mathbb Z+1,
\qquad
c_1(E)=m\,h,
\]
where \(L\) is the \(\mathrm{spin}^c\) determinant line bundle, \(E\) is the twisting line bundle, and \(h\) is the positive generator of \(H^2(\mathbb{CP}^2,\mathbb Z)\).

The \(\mathrm{spin}^c\)-twisted chiral Dirac operator satisfies
\[
\boxed{
\mathrm{Index}(D_+^{(L,E)})
=
\frac{(2m+q)^2-1}{8}.
}
\]

This result is rigorous and follows directly from the Atiyah--Singer index theorem.

\paragraph{Consequence.}
If
\[
\mathrm{Index}(D_+^{(L,E)})\neq 0,
\]
then the two chiral zero-mode counts cannot be equal:
\[
n_L-n_R\neq 0.
\]
Hence one Weyl sector is topologically protected in the precise sense that the net chiral asymmetry cannot be removed by smooth deformations preserving the topological data and Fredholmness.

\subsection{Step 2: Minimal positive protected background}

Among all positive admissible twistings
\[
q>0,\qquad q\in 2\mathbb Z+1,
\qquad
m>0,
\]
the unique primitive pair is
\[
\boxed{
(q,m)=(1,1).
}
\]
For this pair,
\[
\boxed{
\mathrm{Index}(D_+)=1.
}
\]

Moreover, this is the unique smallest positive nonzero index among all positive admissible pairs.

Thus the first nontrivial protected chiral background in the effective \(\mathbb{CP}^2\) defect theory is uniquely the primitive positive twisting \((q,m)=(1,1)\).

\subsection{Step 3: Localized defect zero modes in physical space}

In the effective defect sector, the physical-space Dirac operator can be cast into a Callias-admissible form
\[
\mathcal D_\Phi = i\sigma^i(\partial_i+A_i)+\Phi,
\]
with asymptotic topological charge
\[
Q_{\mathrm{def}}\in \pi_2(\mathbb{CP}^2)\cong \mathbb Z.
\]

For the explicit embedded \(\mathbb{CP}^1\subset \mathbb{CP}^2\) unit defect, one has
\[
Q_{\mathrm{def}}=1,
\qquad
\mathrm{Index}(\mathcal D_\Phi)=1.
\]
Hence there exists at least one normalizable exponentially localized fermion zero mode.

This result is rigorous inside the explicit embedded unit-charge defect subsector.

\subsection{Step 4: What the index does and does not imply}

A nonzero index fixes only the net chirality:
\[
n_L-n_R=I.
\]
It does not imply the absence of mirror pairs.

Indeed,
\[
(n_L,n_R)=(I+r,r)
\]
has the same index for any \(r\ge 0\).

Therefore the mirror problem is independent of index theory and must be treated dynamically.

\subsection{Step 5: Exact finite-dimensional mirror-lifting theorem}

Let
\[
Z_L=\ker D_+,
\qquad
Z_R=\ker D_-,
\qquad
I=\dim Z_L-\dim Z_R>0.
\]
Decompose
\[
Z_L = Z_{\mathrm{top}} \oplus Z_{\mathrm{mir}}^L,
\]
with
\[
\dim Z_{\mathrm{top}}=I,
\qquad
\dim Z_{\mathrm{mir}}^L=\dim Z_R=:r.
\]

Suppose the defect dynamics induces a chiral-odd self-adjoint operator on the zero-mode sector of the form
\[
M
=
\begin{pmatrix}
0 & 0 & 0\\
0 & 0 & \mu^\dagger\\
0 & \mu & 0
\end{pmatrix},
\]
where
\[
\mu : Z_{\mathrm{mir}}^L \to Z_R.
\]
If \(\mu\) is invertible, then:

\begin{enumerate}
\item all mirror pairs are lifted to nonzero energy,
\item only the topological sector remains massless,
\item the surviving massless spectrum has exactly \(I\) left-handed modes and no right-handed mirror zero modes.
\end{enumerate}

This theorem is exact and purely finite-dimensional.

\subsection{Step 6: Symmetry-based mirror lifting}

Let the defect stabilizer be
\[
H=U(2).
\]
Assume the representation content
\[
Z_{\mathrm{top}}\cong \chi_{\mathrm{top}},
\qquad
Z_{\mathrm{mir}}^L\cong Z_R\cong W,
\]
where \(\chi_{\mathrm{top}}\) is a line representation and \(W\) is an irreducible \(U(2)\)-doublet.

Then:

\begin{enumerate}
\item
\[
\operatorname{Hom}_{U(2)}(\chi_{\mathrm{top}},W)=0,
\]
so the topological sector cannot be paired away by any \(U(2)\)-equivariant chiral-odd operator;

\item
\[
\operatorname{End}_{U(2)}(W)\cong \mathbb C,
\]
so every projected mirror block has the form
\[
\mu(\Xi)=c(\Xi)\,\mathbf 1_W;
\]

\item if
\[
c(\Xi)\neq 0,
\]
then \(\mu(\Xi)\) is automatically invertible, and the full mirror sector is lifted.
\end{enumerate}

This theorem is exact under the stated representation assumptions.

\subsection{Step 7: Natural primitive lifting channel}

For the natural primitive internal tensor
\[
T(P)=3P-\mathbf 1_3,
\]
and a radial defect-localized profile \(f(r)\), consider
\[
\Xi_f = f(r)\,\Gamma_\chi\otimes (3P-\mathbf 1_3).
\]

Then the projected mirror block is
\[
\mu(\Xi_f)=c(f)\,\mathbf 1_W,
\]
where
\[
\boxed{
c(f)=\int_0^\infty f(r)\,\kappa(r)\,dr
}
\]
for a radial overlap density \(\kappa(r)\).

Therefore:

\[
\kappa\not\equiv 0
\Longrightarrow
\exists f \text{ such that } c(f)\neq 0
\Longrightarrow
\mu(\Xi_f)\text{ invertible}
\Longrightarrow
\text{full mirror lifting.}
\]

Moreover, once \(c\not\equiv 0\), the set of \(f\) with \(c(f)\neq 0\) is open and dense in the allowed radial profile space.

\subsection{What has been rigorously proved}

The following statements are rigorously established:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the exact \(\mathrm{spin}^c\)-twisted chirality index formula on \(\mathbb{CP}^2\),
\item the uniqueness of the primitive positive protected background \((q,m)=(1,1)\),
\item the existence of an explicit localized unit-charge defect zero mode in the embedded \(\mathbb{CP}^1\subset \mathbb{CP}^2\) sector,
\item the exact finite-dimensional mirror-lifting theorem,
\item the symmetry-based statement that line sectors cannot pair with mirror doublets under \(U(2)\),
\item the fact that a nonzero projected mirror coupling in the doublet channel is automatically full rank,
\item the reduction of the primitive-channel mirror problem to the radial overlap density \(\kappa(r)\).
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{What is only conditional}

The following statements are conditional on effective reduction assumptions:

\begin{enumerate}
\item that the full microscopic TECT defect PDE reduces to the effective \(\mathrm{spin}^c\)-twisted \(\mathbb{CP}^2\) Dirac problem;
\item that the full defect zero-mode sector has the representation assignment
\[
Z_{\mathrm{top}}\cong \chi_{\mathrm{top}},
\qquad
Z_{\mathrm{mir}}^L\cong Z_R\cong W;
\]
\item that the primitive lifting channel
\[
T(P)=3P-\mathbf 1_3
\]
belongs to the physically realized microscopic operator space.
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{What remains genuinely open}

The genuine microscopic open problems are now sharply reduced to:

\begin{enumerate}
\item derive from the full TECT defect PDE the effective twisting data \((q,m)\) rather than choosing them inside the effective \(\mathbb{CP}^2\) problem;
\item derive the \(U(2)\)-module decomposition of the full zero-mode sector;
\item prove that the microscopic natural lifting operator space contains at least one operator for which
\[
\kappa(r)\not\equiv 0;
\]
\item exclude hidden exact symmetries of the full defect PDE that might force
\[
\kappa(r)\equiv 0.
\]
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{Current strongest honest conclusion}

The strongest honest statement currently available is the following:

\paragraph{Theorem-like status statement.}
If the full microscopic TECT defect dynamics reduces to the effective \(\mathbb{CP}^2\) \(\mathrm{spin}^c\) defect problem with primitive positive twisting, \(U(2)\)-doublet mirror sector, and a natural primitive lifting channel with nontrivial overlap density, then the low-energy defect fermion spectrum is purely chiral:
\[
n_L=1,\qquad n_R=0
\]
in the primitive unit-charge sector.

At present, every implication in this chain is rigorous \emph{except} the final microscopic matching to the full TECT defect PDE.

\section{Final summary}

The current chirality chain in TECT has the following exact structure.

First, for the effective \(\mathrm{spin}^c\)-twisted Dirac operator on \(\mathbb{CP}^2\),
\[
\mathrm{Index}(D_+^{(L,E)})
=
\frac{(2m+q)^2-1}{8}.
\]
The unique primitive positive twisting is
\[
(q,m)=(1,1),
\]
for which
\[
\mathrm{Index}(D_+)=1.
\]

Second, in the effective defect sector, an explicit unit-charge embedded \(\mathbb{CP}^1\subset \mathbb{CP}^2\) defect supports at least one localized zero mode.

Third, index alone does not remove mirror pairs.
Mirror removal requires a chiral-odd effective lifting operator on the zero-mode space.
If its mirror block is invertible, then all mirror states are lifted and only the index-protected chiral sector remains.

Fourth, under the natural \(U(2)\) stabilizer symmetry, if the topological sector is a line representation and the mirror sectors are irreducible doublets, then any nonzero projected mirror coupling is automatically full rank, while the topological sector cannot be paired away.

Finally, for the primitive internal channel
\[
T(P)=3P-\mathbf 1_3,
\]
the projected mirror coupling reduces to
\[
c(f)=\int_0^\infty f(r)\,\kappa(r)\,dr.
\]
Hence
\[
\kappa\not\equiv 0
\Longrightarrow
\text{full mirror lifting}.
\]

Therefore the remaining open microscopic question is now sharply reduced to proving that the full TECT defect dynamics yields the required effective module structure and a natural primitive lifting channel with nonvanishing overlap density \(\kappa(r)\).

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Dirac Operator Emergence from the Microscopic Condensate Dynamics}

\subsection{Purpose and exact scope}

The goal of this section is to identify the precise mathematical mechanism by which a Dirac operator emerges from the microscopic condensate dynamics.

We emphasize at the outset that the present result is a \emph{conditional emergence theorem}.
It does not assume the Dirac operator from the start, but it does assume a specific structure of the linearized microscopic condensate PDE:

\begin{enumerate}
\item an isolated low-energy sector,
\item a chiral grading,
\item first-order linear momentum coupling near a band-touching point.
\end{enumerate}

Under these hypotheses, we prove that the effective low-energy operator is of Dirac type.
What remains open is the direct verification that the full microscopic TECT condensate equations satisfy these hypotheses.

\subsection{Microscopic linearized condensate equation}

Let
\[
\Psi(x,t)\in \mathcal H_{\mathrm{micro}}
\]
be the microscopic fluctuation field of the condensate, valued in a finite-dimensional internal space \(\mathcal H_{\mathrm{micro}}\cong \mathbb C^N\) after mode truncation near the relevant shell.

Assume the microscopic linearized equation takes the form
\[
i\partial_t \Psi = \mathcal L(-i\nabla)\Psi,
\]
where
\[
\mathcal L(k)
\]
is a smooth Hermitian \(N\times N\) matrix-valued symbol in momentum space.

We are interested in a distinguished momentum \(k_*\) at which a low-energy crossing occurs.

\subsection{Low-energy crossing hypotheses}

We now state the exact hypotheses under which the theorem will be proved.

\paragraph{(H1) Isolated low-energy sector.}
There exists an orthogonal decomposition
\[
\mathcal H_{\mathrm{micro}} = \mathcal H_{\ell}\oplus \mathcal H_h
\]
such that at \(k=k_*\), the operator \(\mathcal L(k_*)\) has a finite-dimensional low-energy kernel in \(\mathcal H_\ell\), while the high-energy sector is gapped:
\[
\mathcal L(k_*)=
\begin{pmatrix}
0 & 0\\
0 & M_h
\end{pmatrix},
\qquad
\operatorname{spec}(M_h)\subset (-\infty,-\Delta]\cup[\Delta,\infty)
\]
for some \(\Delta>0\).

\paragraph{(H2) Two-sector chiral splitting.}
The low-energy space \(\mathcal H_\ell\) admits a grading
\[
\mathcal H_\ell = \mathcal H_+\oplus \mathcal H_-,
\qquad
\dim \mathcal H_+ = \dim \mathcal H_- = m,
\]
with grading operator
\[
\Gamma=
\begin{pmatrix}
\mathbf 1_m & 0\\
0 & -\mathbf 1_m
\end{pmatrix},
\qquad
\Gamma^2=\mathbf 1.
\]

\paragraph{(H3) Chiral oddness of the first-order symbol.}
The first derivative of the low-energy symbol anticommutes with the grading:
\[
\Gamma\, \partial_{k_i}\mathcal L_\ell(k_*) + \partial_{k_i}\mathcal L_\ell(k_*)\,\Gamma =0,
\qquad i=1,\dots,d,
\]
where \(\mathcal L_\ell\) is the restriction of \(\mathcal L\) to the low-energy sector.

\paragraph{(H4) First-order nondegeneracy.}
The first-order low-energy symbol
\[
A(p):=\sum_{i=1}^d p_i\,\partial_{k_i}\mathcal L_\ell(k_*)
\]
is nondegenerate for \(p\neq 0\) near the origin.
Equivalently, there exists \(c>0\) such that
\[
\|A(p)\xi\|\ge c|p|\|\xi\|
\]
for all \(\xi\in \mathcal H_\ell\) and all sufficiently small \(p\neq 0\).

These hypotheses are exactly the minimal mathematical content of a linearly dispersing chiral crossing.

\subsection{Block structure of the low-energy symbol}

Because of the grading condition, the first-order symbol has an off-diagonal form.

\paragraph{Lemma 1.}
Under hypothesis \textnormal{(H3)}, the linearized low-energy symbol can be written as
\[
A(p)=
\begin{pmatrix}
0 & B(p)^\dagger\\
B(p) & 0
\end{pmatrix}
\]
for a linear map
\[
B(p)=\sum_{i=1}^d p_i B_i,
\qquad
B_i:\mathcal H_+\to \mathcal H_-.
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
Write
\[
A(p)=
\begin{pmatrix}
X(p) & Y(p)^\dagger\\
Y(p) & Z(p)
\end{pmatrix}
\]
relative to \(\mathcal H_+\oplus \mathcal H_-\).
The anticommutation relation
\[
\Gamma A(p)+A(p)\Gamma=0
\]
gives
\[
2X(p)=0,
\qquad
-2Z(p)=0.
\]
Thus \(X(p)=Z(p)=0\), and therefore
\[
A(p)=
\begin{pmatrix}
0 & Y(p)^\dagger\\
Y(p) & 0
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
Set \(B(p):=Y(p)\).
\qed

This is the fundamental algebraic origin of the Dirac form.

\subsection{Linear dispersion and ellipticity}

The nondegeneracy hypothesis implies that the off-diagonal block \(B(p)\) is itself elliptic.

\paragraph{Lemma 2.}
Under hypothesis \textnormal{(H4)}, the map \(B(p)\) is invertible for all sufficiently small \(p\neq 0\).

\paragraph{Proof.}
If \(B(p)\) were not injective for some \(p\neq 0\), there would exist \(0\neq u\in \mathcal H_+\) such that
\[
B(p)u=0.
\]
Then
\[
A(p)\binom{u}{0}=0,
\]
contradicting the nondegeneracy of \(A(p)\).
Since \(\dim \mathcal H_+=\dim \mathcal H_-=m\), injectivity implies invertibility.
\qed

Thus the crossing is linearly dispersing and not accidentally flat.

\subsection{Emergent Dirac Hamiltonian}

We can now state the first main theorem.

\paragraph{Theorem 1 (Low-energy Dirac emergence).}
Under hypotheses \textnormal{(H1)}--\textnormal{(H4)}, the projected low-energy Hamiltonian near \(k_*\) takes the form
\[
H_{\mathrm{eff}}(p)
=
\begin{pmatrix}
0 & B(p)^\dagger\\
B(p) & 0
\end{pmatrix}
+ O(|p|^2),
\qquad
p=k-k_*.
\]
In position space, after inverse Fourier transform, the corresponding effective equation is
\[
i\partial_t \psi
=
\left(
-i\sum_{i=1}^d \alpha^i \partial_i
\right)\psi
+ O(\partial^2)\psi,
\]
where the matrices \(\alpha^i\) are Hermitian and off-diagonal with respect to the chiral grading.
Hence the leading infrared operator is of Dirac type.

\paragraph{Proof.}
By Taylor expansion near \(k_*\),
\[
\mathcal L_\ell(k_*+p)=A(p)+O(|p|^2).
\]
By Lemma 1,
\[
A(p)=
\begin{pmatrix}
0 & B(p)^\dagger\\
B(p) & 0
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
Since \(B(p)\) is linear in \(p\), one may write
\[
B(p)=\sum_i p_i B_i.
\]
Define
\[
\alpha^i=
\begin{pmatrix}
0 & B_i^\dagger\\
B_i & 0
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
Then
\[
A(p)=\sum_i p_i \alpha^i.
\]
Inverse Fourier transform gives
\[
p_i \mapsto -i\partial_i,
\]
hence
\[
H_{\mathrm{eff}}=
-i\sum_i \alpha^i \partial_i + O(\partial^2).
\]
This is exactly a first-order Dirac-type operator.
\qed

\subsection{Reduction from the full microscopic operator by Schur complement}

We now justify the projection from the full \(N\)-component microscopic operator to the low-energy Dirac sector.

Write the full symbol near \(k_*\) as
\[
\mathcal L(k)=
\begin{pmatrix}
\mathcal L_{\ell\ell}(k) & \mathcal L_{\ell h}(k)\\
\mathcal L_{h\ell}(k) & \mathcal L_{hh}(k)
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
By hypothesis,
\[
\mathcal L_{hh}(k_*)
\]
is invertible with gap \(\Delta\).

For energies \(|E|\ll \Delta\), the high sector may be eliminated by the Schur complement:
\[
\mathcal L_{\mathrm{red}}(k,E)
=
\mathcal L_{\ell\ell}(k)
-
\mathcal L_{\ell h}(k)\,
(\mathcal L_{hh}(k)-E)^{-1}\,
\mathcal L_{h\ell}(k).
\]

\paragraph{Theorem 2 (Schur complement reduction).}
Under hypothesis \textnormal{(H1)}, the low-energy spectrum of the full microscopic operator near \(k_*\) is determined by the reduced symbol
\[
\mathcal L_{\mathrm{red}}(k,E).
\]
Moreover, the high-sector correction contributes only higher-order analytic terms in \(p\) and \(E\), so the leading first-order term is exactly the Dirac-type symbol of Theorem 1.

\paragraph{Proof.}
This is the standard block-matrix Schur complement theorem for a gapped high-energy sector.
Since \(\mathcal L_{hh}(k_*)\) is invertible, its inverse is analytic near \(k_*\).
Therefore the correction term is analytic in \(p\) and \(E\).
Because the leading first-order term in the low-energy sector is already fixed by \(A(p)\), the Schur correction changes only higher-order coefficients unless an additional first-order resonance is present.
Under the isolation hypothesis, no such resonance occurs.
Hence the leading infrared operator is unchanged.
\qed

So the emergence of the Dirac operator is stable under integrating out the gapped microscopic modes.

\subsection{When the operator becomes the standard Dirac operator}

Theorem 1 gives a Dirac-type operator.
To obtain the standard Clifford form, one needs one additional condition.

\paragraph{(H5) Clifford isotropy condition.}
There exists a choice of basis on \(\mathcal H_\ell\) such that
\[
\{\alpha^i,\alpha^j\}=2 g^{ij}\mathbf 1
\]
for a positive definite metric \(g^{ij}\).

\paragraph{Theorem 3 (Standard Dirac form).}
Under hypotheses \textnormal{(H1)}--\textnormal{(H5)}, the leading low-energy operator is unitarily equivalent to the standard massless Dirac operator
\[
H_{\mathrm{Dirac}}
=
-i\sum_{i=1}^d \gamma^0\gamma^i \partial_i
\]
with spatial metric \(g^{ij}\).

\paragraph{Proof.}
Theorem 1 gives
\[
H_{\mathrm{eff}}=-i\sum_i \alpha^i\partial_i + O(\partial^2).
\]
If the \(\alpha^i\) satisfy the Clifford relations, then by finite-dimensional representation theory of the complex Clifford algebra they are unitarily equivalent to a standard gamma-matrix representation.
Hence the effective operator is the standard massless Dirac operator up to unitary equivalence and higher-order corrections.
\qed

This theorem isolates the exact additional ingredient needed for full standard-Dirac recovery.

\subsection{Defect-localized version}

The same argument applies to the defect-localized sector after projecting to the isolated zero-mode bundle.

Let
\[
P_0
\]
be the spectral projector onto the defect-localized low-energy space.
Then the projected linearized operator is
\[
\mathcal L_{\mathrm{def}}(k)=P_0 \mathcal L(k) P_0.
\]

\paragraph{Theorem 4 (Defect-localized Dirac emergence).}
Assume the isolated defect zero-mode bundle is spectrally separated and the projected symbol \(\mathcal L_{\mathrm{def}}(k)\) satisfies hypotheses \textnormal{(H2)}--\textnormal{(H4)} at a crossing momentum \(k_*\).
Then the effective defect fermion obeys a Dirac-type equation at leading infrared order.

\paragraph{Proof.}
Apply Theorem 1 to the projected operator \(P_0\mathcal L P_0\).
\qed

This is the precise bridge between the abstract condensate linearization and the effective defect fermion.

\subsection{What is rigorously proved}

At this point, the following statements are rigorous:

\begin{enumerate}
\item A gapped microscopic condensate operator with an isolated low-energy chiral crossing reduces by spectral projection and Schur complement to a finite-dimensional low-energy symbol.
\item If the first-order low-energy symbol is chiral-odd and nondegenerate, then the effective operator is necessarily Dirac-type.
\item If the leading matrices satisfy the Clifford relations, then the operator is unitarily equivalent to the standard massless Dirac operator.
\item The same statement applies to the defect-localized sector once the isolated defect projector is used.
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{What is not yet proved for full TECT}

The following points remain open for the full microscopic TECT dynamics:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the explicit microscopic condensate PDE has not yet been written in a final closed form suitable for direct spectral analysis;
\item the existence of an isolated low-energy crossing \(k_*\) in the actual TECT linearization has not yet been proved;
\item the chiral grading \(\Gamma\) and first-order anticommutation relation have not yet been derived directly from the full condensate variables;
\item the Clifford isotropy condition has not yet been shown for the actual TECT low-energy matrices.
\end{enumerate}

Thus the theorem proved here is an exact \emph{reduction theorem}:
it identifies the minimal spectral conditions under which Dirac emergence follows automatically.

\subsection{Current strongest honest conclusion}

The strongest honest statement currently available is therefore:

\paragraph{Conditional Dirac emergence statement.}
If the microscopic TECT condensate linearization admits an isolated low-energy chiral crossing whose first-order symbol is nondegenerate and odd with respect to a grading, then the low-energy effective fermion operator is of Dirac type.
If in addition the leading matrices satisfy the Clifford relations, the effective operator is the standard massless Dirac operator up to higher-order corrections.

This is rigorous.
What remains open is the direct verification of these hypotheses from the full microscopic TECT condensate PDE.

\section{Final summary}

Let the microscopic condensate fluctuation equation be
\[
i\partial_t\Psi=\mathcal L(-i\nabla)\Psi.
\]
Assume that near a momentum \(k_*\) the linearized symbol \(\mathcal L(k)\) has:

\begin{enumerate}
\item an isolated low-energy sector separated from high-energy modes by a gap,
\item a chiral grading
\[
\mathcal H_\ell=\mathcal H_+\oplus \mathcal H_-,
\]
\item a first-order symbol
\[
A(p)=\sum_i p_i\,\partial_{k_i}\mathcal L_\ell(k_*)
\]
that anticommutes with the grading and is nondegenerate for \(p\neq 0\).
\end{enumerate}

Then the projected low-energy operator has the form
\[
H_{\mathrm{eff}}(p)=
\begin{pmatrix}
0 & B(p)^\dagger\\
B(p) & 0
\end{pmatrix}
+O(|p|^2),
\]
hence in position space
\[
H_{\mathrm{eff}}
=
-i\sum_i \alpha^i\partial_i + O(\partial^2),
\]
which is a Dirac-type operator.

If, in addition,
\[
\{\alpha^i,\alpha^j\}=2g^{ij}\mathbf 1,
\]
then the effective operator is unitarily equivalent to the standard massless Dirac operator.

Thus the emergence of the Dirac operator is rigorously reduced to verifying a finite set of spectral hypotheses for the full microscopic TECT condensate linearization.

%%%%%%%%%%

\section{A Minimal Shell-Pair \(k\cdot p\) Derivation of Weyl and Dirac Operators}

\subsection{Purpose}

The full microscopic TECT condensate PDE has not yet been fixed in a final closed analytic form.
Therefore one cannot honestly claim a complete first-principles derivation of the Dirac operator directly from the final TECT equations at this stage.

What can be proved rigorously, however, is the following:
if the linearized condensate dynamics has a pair of isolated opposite low-energy valleys \(\pm k_*\), and if the crossing at each valley is first-order and nondegenerate, then the projected envelope theory is necessarily Weyl at each valley and Dirac after combining the two valleys.

This section establishes that theorem explicitly.

\subsection{Microscopic linearized equation and valley hypothesis}

Let the microscopic fluctuation field satisfy
\[
i\partial_t \Psi = \mathcal L(-i\nabla)\Psi,
\]
where \(\Psi(x,t)\in \mathbb C^N\) and \(\mathcal L(k)\) is a smooth Hermitian \(N\times N\) matrix symbol.

Assume there exists a nonzero momentum \(k_*\in \mathbb R^d\) such that:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the low-energy spectrum is isolated near \(k=\pm k_*\),
\item at each valley the low-energy eigenspace is two-dimensional,
\item the rest of the spectrum is separated by a gap \(\Delta>0\).
\end{enumerate}

Let
\[
P_+(k),\qquad P_-(k)
\]
be the spectral projectors onto the two-dimensional low-energy sectors near \(+k_*\) and \(-k_*\), respectively.

Fix orthonormal bases
\[
u_{+,1},u_{+,2}\in \operatorname{Ran}P_+(k_*),
\qquad
u_{-,1},u_{-,2}\in \operatorname{Ran}P_-(-k_*).
\]

\subsection{Envelope ansatz}

Introduce slowly varying envelope amplitudes
\[
\phi_+(x,t)\in \mathbb C^2,
\qquad
\phi_-(x,t)\in \mathbb C^2,
\]
and write the multiscale ansatz
\[
\Psi(x,t)
=
e^{ik_*\cdot x}\sum_{a=1}^2 \phi_{+,a}(x,t)\,u_{+,a}
+
e^{-ik_*\cdot x}\sum_{a=1}^2 \phi_{-,a}(x,t)\,u_{-,a}.
\]

Equivalently,
\[
\Psi(x,t)
=
e^{ik_*\cdot x}\,U_+\,\phi_+(x,t)
+
e^{-ik_*\cdot x}\,U_-\,\phi_-(x,t),
\]
where
\[
U_\pm = \begin{pmatrix}u_{\pm,1}&u_{\pm,2}\end{pmatrix}.
\]

We assume the envelopes vary on scales much longer than \(|k_*|^{-1}\), so that \(k\cdot p\) expansion is valid.

\subsection{Valley-projected \(k\cdot p\) Hamiltonians}

Expand the symbol near the two valleys:
\[
\mathcal L(k_*+p)=\mathcal L(k_*)+\sum_{i=1}^d p_i\,\partial_{k_i}\mathcal L(k_*) + O(|p|^2),
\]
\[
\mathcal L(-k_*+p)=\mathcal L(-k_*)+\sum_{i=1}^d p_i\,\partial_{k_i}\mathcal L(-k_*) + O(|p|^2).
\]

By assumption, the low-energy eigenvalue at each valley is shifted to zero:
\[
U_+^\dagger \mathcal L(k_*) U_+ = 0,
\qquad
U_-^\dagger \mathcal L(-k_*) U_- = 0.
\]

Therefore the projected first-order valley Hamiltonians are
\[
h_+(p)
=
\sum_{i=1}^d p_i\,V_i^+ + O(|p|^2),
\qquad
V_i^+ := U_+^\dagger (\partial_{k_i}\mathcal L)(k_*) U_+,
\]
\[
h_-(p)
=
\sum_{i=1}^d p_i\,V_i^- + O(|p|^2),
\qquad
V_i^- := U_-^\dagger (\partial_{k_i}\mathcal L)(-k_*) U_-.
\]

Each \(V_i^\pm\) is a \(2\times 2\) Hermitian matrix.

\subsection{Pauli decomposition and Weyl form}

Every \(2\times 2\) Hermitian matrix decomposes uniquely as
\[
V_i^\pm = v_i^{0,\pm}\,\mathbf 1_2 + \sum_{a=1}^3 v_i^{a,\pm}\,\sigma^a.
\]
Hence
\[
h_\pm(p)
=
\left(\sum_i v_i^{0,\pm}p_i\right)\mathbf 1_2
+
\sum_{a=1}^3
\left(\sum_i v_i^{a,\pm}p_i\right)\sigma^a
+ O(|p|^2).
\]

The scalar tilt term
\[
\left(\sum_i v_i^{0,\pm}p_i\right)\mathbf 1_2
\]
does not affect chirality.
The genuine crossing structure is controlled by the \(3\times d\) velocity matrix
\[
\mathsf V_\pm = (v_i^{a,\pm})_{a=1,2,3}^{i=1,\dots,d}.
\]

\paragraph{Definition.}
The valley crossing is called nondegenerate if the map
\[
p\mapsto \sum_{a=1}^3 \left(\sum_i v_i^{a,\pm}p_i\right)\sigma^a
\]
has trivial kernel for \(p\neq 0\) near the origin.

In \(d=3\), this means precisely that \(\det \mathsf V_\pm \neq 0\).

\paragraph{Theorem 1 (Weyl emergence at a single valley).}
Assume \(d=3\) and the valley crossing at \(+k_*\) is nondegenerate.
Then, after subtracting the tilt term and making an invertible linear change of spatial coordinates, the leading projected Hamiltonian is unitarily equivalent to
\[
h_+^{\mathrm{Weyl}}(p)
=
\chi_+\, \sum_{a=1}^3 p_a \sigma^a
\]
with chirality
\[
\chi_+ = \operatorname{sign}\det \mathsf V_+ \in \{+1,-1\}.
\]

The same statement holds at \(-k_*\), with chirality
\[
\chi_- = \operatorname{sign}\det \mathsf V_-.
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
Since \(\mathsf V_+\) is invertible, define new momentum coordinates
\[
q_a = \sum_i v_i^{a,+} p_i.
\]
Then the traceless part of \(h_+(p)\) becomes
\[
\sum_{a=1}^3 q_a \sigma^a.
\]
If \(\det \mathsf V_+<0\), one orientation-reversing linear transformation is needed, which flips the Weyl chirality sign.
Thus the resulting operator is Weyl with chirality \(\chi_+=\operatorname{sign}\det\mathsf V_+\).
\qed

So each isolated nondegenerate two-band valley crossing is a Weyl fermion.

\subsection{Opposite valleys and Dirac doubling}

We now combine the two valleys.
Define the four-component envelope spinor
\[
\Phi(x,t)
=
\begin{pmatrix}
\phi_+(x,t)\\
\phi_-(x,t)
\end{pmatrix}
\in \mathbb C^4.
\]

At leading order the projected Hamiltonian is block diagonal:
\[
H_{\mathrm{valley}}(p)
=
\begin{pmatrix}
h_+(p) & 0\\
0 & h_-(p)
\end{pmatrix}
+ O(|p|^2).
\]

Assume now the opposite-valley symmetry:

\paragraph{(V1) Valley inversion symmetry.}
There exists a unitary or antiunitary symmetry sending \(k_*\mapsto -k_*\) such that, after a suitable basis choice,
\[
h_-(p) = - h_+(-p)
\]
to leading order.

This is the standard situation for opposite valleys related by inversion or time-reversal-type symmetry.

\paragraph{Theorem 2 (Dirac emergence from opposite Weyl valleys).}
Assume Theorem 1 holds at both valleys and condition \textnormal{(V1)} is satisfied.
Then the four-component projected Hamiltonian is unitarily equivalent, to leading order, to a massless Dirac Hamiltonian
\[
H_D(p)=\sum_{i=1}^3 \alpha^i p_i,
\]
where the \(4\times 4\) matrices \(\alpha^i\) satisfy
\[
\{\alpha^i,\alpha^j\}=2g^{ij}\mathbf 1_4
\]
for a positive definite spatial metric \(g^{ij}\).

\paragraph{Proof.}
By Theorem 1, each valley gives a Weyl Hamiltonian.
Under \textnormal{(V1)}, the two Weyl nodes have opposite chirality and are related by valley symmetry.
Therefore one may choose a basis in which
\[
H_{\mathrm{valley}}(p)
=
\begin{pmatrix}
\sum_i p_i \sigma^i & 0\\
0 & -\sum_i p_i \sigma^i
\end{pmatrix}
\]
up to an invertible linear change of \(p\).
This is exactly the chiral representation of the massless Dirac Hamiltonian
\[
H_D(p)=\sum_i \alpha^i p_i,
\qquad
\alpha^i=
\begin{pmatrix}
\sigma^i & 0\\
0 & -\sigma^i
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
The Clifford relations are immediate.
\qed

Thus a pair of opposite nondegenerate Weyl valleys yields a Dirac fermion.

\subsection{Position-space envelope equation}

Fourier inversion gives the effective low-energy PDE
\[
i\partial_t \Phi
=
\left(
-i\sum_{i=1}^3 \alpha^i \partial_i
\right)\Phi
+ O(\partial^2)\Phi.
\]

If a symmetry-allowed valley-mixing mass term is present, then one gets
\[
i\partial_t \Phi
=
\left(
-i\sum_{i=1}^3 \alpha^i \partial_i + m\beta
\right)\Phi
+ O(\partial^2)\Phi,
\]
where
\[
\beta^2=\mathbf 1_4,
\qquad
\{\beta,\alpha^i\}=0.
\]
Hence the massless Dirac operator is the universal leading infrared operator, and mass terms appear only as symmetry-allowed subleading deformations.

\subsection{Schur complement justification}

The previous derivation used only the valley subspace.
We now justify rigorously why the remaining microscopic modes do not alter the leading first-order Dirac structure.

Write the microscopic symbol in block form near the two-valley sector:
\[
\mathcal L(k)=
\begin{pmatrix}
\mathcal L_{\ell\ell}(k) & \mathcal L_{\ell h}(k)\\
\mathcal L_{h\ell}(k) & \mathcal L_{hh}(k)
\end{pmatrix},
\]
where \(\mathcal L_{\ell\ell}\) acts on the four-dimensional valley space and \(\mathcal L_{hh}\) on the high-energy complement.

Assume
\[
\operatorname{spec}\mathcal L_{hh}(k_*) \cap (-\Delta,\Delta)=\varnothing.
\]
Then the reduced low-energy symbol is the Schur complement
\[
\mathcal L_{\mathrm{red}}(k,E)
=
\mathcal L_{\ell\ell}(k)
-
\mathcal L_{\ell h}(k)\,
(\mathcal L_{hh}(k)-E)^{-1}\,
\mathcal L_{h\ell}(k).
\]

\paragraph{Theorem 3 (Stability under integrating out gapped modes).}
Under the high-energy gap assumption, the Schur complement correction is analytic in \(p=k-k_*\) and \(E\), and does not alter the leading first-order Weyl/Dirac term unless an additional first-order resonance is present.
Therefore the leading Dirac operator derived above is stable under integrating out the gapped microscopic modes.

\paragraph{Proof.}
The inverse of \(\mathcal L_{hh}(k)-E\) exists and is analytic for \(|E|,|p|\ll \Delta\).
Hence the Schur complement is analytic in \(p\) and \(E\).
Since the first-order part of \(\mathcal L_{\ell\ell}\) already gives the nondegenerate Weyl/Dirac crossing, the analytic correction can modify coefficients but cannot destroy the first-order Dirac structure without closing the high-energy gap or producing an additional resonance.
\qed

\subsection{What this proves for TECT and what it does not}

We now state the exact status.

\paragraph{Proved.}
\begin{enumerate}
\item An isolated nondegenerate two-band valley crossing yields a Weyl operator.
\item A pair of opposite Weyl valleys related by valley inversion symmetry yields a Dirac operator.
\item The Dirac structure is stable under integrating out gapped microscopic modes.
\end{enumerate}

\paragraph{Not proved.}
\begin{enumerate}
\item That the full microscopic TECT condensate linearization actually possesses such valleys \(\pm k_*\).
\item That the relevant TECT valley subspace is exactly two-dimensional at each valley.
\item That the first-order velocity matrices are nondegenerate.
\item That the opposite-valley symmetry \textnormal{(V1)} holds for the actual TECT shell/BCC linearization.
\end{enumerate}

So the present derivation is a rigorous reduction theorem, not yet a full microscopic TECT derivation.

\subsection{Current strongest honest conclusion}

The strongest honest statement now available is:

\paragraph{Conditional Dirac emergence theorem for TECT.}
If the microscopic TECT condensate linearization has an isolated pair of opposite nondegenerate low-energy two-band valleys \(\pm k_*\), then the low-energy envelope dynamics is Weyl at each valley and Dirac after valley doubling.
If the valley pair is the one selected by the defect sector, then the emergent defect fermion obeys a Dirac equation at leading infrared order.

This theorem is rigorous.
What remains open is the explicit verification of the valley hypotheses in the full microscopic TECT condensate PDE.

\section{Final summary}

Consider the microscopic condensate fluctuation equation
\[
i\partial_t\Psi=\mathcal L(-i\nabla)\Psi.
\]
Assume that near a pair of opposite momenta \(\pm k_*\), the symbol \(\mathcal L(k)\) has isolated two-dimensional low-energy valleys, each with a nondegenerate first-order crossing.

Then the \(k\cdot p\) projection onto a single valley gives a \(2\times 2\) Hermitian Hamiltonian
\[
h_\pm(p)=\sum_i p_i V_i^\pm + O(|p|^2),
\]
whose traceless part is unitarily equivalent to a Weyl Hamiltonian.

If the two valleys are related by the standard opposite-valley symmetry
\[
h_-(p)=-h_+(-p),
\]
then combining them yields a four-component massless Dirac Hamiltonian
\[
H_D(p)=\sum_i \alpha^i p_i,
\qquad
\{\alpha^i,\alpha^j\}=2g^{ij}\mathbf 1_4.
\]

Therefore the emergence of the Dirac operator is rigorously reduced to a concrete spectral verification problem:
show that the full microscopic TECT condensate linearization possesses an isolated opposite-valley pair with nondegenerate first-order crossings.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Concrete BCC Shell-Pair Linearization and Emergent Weyl/Dirac Structure}

\subsection{Purpose}

We now specialize the abstract valley-emergence theorem to the most natural minimal ansatz associated with the BCC reciprocal shell.

The goal is not yet to derive the full microscopic TECT PDE, which is still open, but rather to prove the following concrete statement:

\begin{quote}
If the low-energy TECT fluctuation sector near one opposite reciprocal pair \(\pm G_*\) is carried by the two transverse polarizations, then symmetry alone forces the projected first-order \(k\cdot p\) Hamiltonian to be of Weyl type at each valley, and of Dirac type after valley doubling.
\end{quote}

This gives the sharpest currently rigorous BCC-specific reduction of the Dirac-emergence problem.

\subsection{A reciprocal-shell pair in the BCC star}

Let
\[
S_{\mathrm{BCC}}
=
\frac{1}{\sqrt2}
\{(\pm1,\pm1,0),(\pm1,0,\pm1),(0,\pm1,\pm1)\}
\]
be the cuboctahedral 12-vector reciprocal star.

Fix one opposite pair
\[
\pm G_* \in S_{\mathrm{BCC}},
\qquad
|G_*|=1.
\]
Let
\[
\hat n := \frac{G_*}{|G_*|}
\]
be the corresponding distinguished axis.

The little group of the single wavevector \(G_*\) inside the cubic group contains the rotations preserving the axis \(\hat n\).
For the continuum \(k\cdot p\) analysis, the relevant infrared remnant is the axial group acting on the transverse plane
\[
\hat n^\perp.
\]
Thus the two transverse polarizations furnish a real two-dimensional representation, or equivalently a complex two-component low-energy envelope.

\subsection{Transverse shell-pair ansatz}

Choose an orthonormal frame
\[
e_1,e_2,e_3=\hat n
\]
with
\[
e_1,e_2\in \hat n^\perp.
\]
Let
\[
U_+=\begin{pmatrix}u_{+,1}&u_{+,2}\end{pmatrix},
\qquad
U_-=\begin{pmatrix}u_{-,1}&u_{-,2}\end{pmatrix}
\]
be orthonormal bases of the two-dimensional transverse low-energy sectors at \(+G_*\) and \(-G_*\), respectively.

We introduce slowly varying envelopes
\[
\phi_+(x,t)\in \mathbb C^2,
\qquad
\phi_-(x,t)\in \mathbb C^2,
\]
and use the shell-pair ansatz
\[
\Psi(x,t)
=
e^{iG_*\cdot x}U_+\phi_+(x,t)
+
e^{-iG_*\cdot x}U_-\phi_-(x,t).
\]

This is the minimal BCC valley ansatz: one opposite reciprocal pair, each carrying the two transverse modes.

\subsection{Momentum decomposition near a fixed valley}

Write
\[
k = G_* + p,
\]
where \(p\) is small.
Decompose \(p\) into longitudinal and transverse components:
\[
p = p_\parallel \hat n + p_1 e_1 + p_2 e_2.
\]
Thus
\[
p_\parallel = p\cdot \hat n,
\qquad
p_1 = p\cdot e_1,
\qquad
p_2 = p\cdot e_2.
\]

The valley-projected Hamiltonian is a \(2\times 2\) Hermitian matrix
\[
h_+(p)
=
U_+^\dagger \mathcal L(G_*+p) U_+
=
h_+(0)+\sum_{i=\parallel,1,2} p_i V_i^+ + O(|p|^2).
\]
By shifting the reference energy,
\[
h_+(0)=0.
\]

Every \(2\times 2\) Hermitian matrix admits the Pauli decomposition
\[
h_+(p)
=
a_0(p)\mathbf 1_2 + a_1(p)\sigma^1 + a_2(p)\sigma^2 + a_3(p)\sigma^3 + O(|p|^2),
\]
with \(a_\mu(p)\) linear in \(p\) at first order.

\subsection{Little-group covariance and the allowed first-order terms}

We now classify the linear terms using axial covariance around \(\hat n\).

Under rotations around \(\hat n\), the pair \((p_1,p_2)\) transforms as a vector in the transverse plane.
The transverse doublet basis may be chosen so that the Pauli pair \((\sigma^1,\sigma^2)\) transforms in the same way, while \(\sigma^3\) is axial-scalar with respect to this transverse rotation.

Therefore the most general first-order Hermitian Hamiltonian compatible with axial covariance is
\[
\boxed{
h_+(p)
=
\varepsilon_0(p)\mathbf 1_2
+
v_\parallel p_\parallel \sigma^3
+
v_1 p_1 \sigma^1
+
v_2 p_2 \sigma^2
+
O(|p|^2),
}
\]
where
\[
\varepsilon_0(p)=u_\parallel p_\parallel + u_1 p_1 + u_2 p_2
\]
is a possible tilt term.

\paragraph{Proposition 1.}
Up to linear redefinition of the transverse basis and spatial coordinates, the above form is the most general first-order valley Hamiltonian compatible with the transverse-doublet little-group structure.

\paragraph{Proof.}
The scalar term must multiply \(\mathbf 1_2\).
A linear term proportional to \((p_1,p_2)\) must map into the \(2\)-dimensional Hermitian traceless matrices transforming as a vector under transverse rotations, hence into the span of \((\sigma^1,\sigma^2)\).
The remaining longitudinal scalar \(p_\parallel\) may multiply the remaining traceless Hermitian matrix \(\sigma^3\).
No other independent first-order structures exist at this level.
\qed

This is the BCC-shell analog of the standard \(k\cdot p\) Weyl normal form.

\subsection{Weyl normal form}

We now remove the inessential tilt and normalize the velocities.

Define the traceless part
\[
\widetilde h_+(p)
=
h_+(p)-\varepsilon_0(p)\mathbf 1_2
=
v_\parallel p_\parallel \sigma^3
+
v_1 p_1 \sigma^1
+
v_2 p_2 \sigma^2
+
O(|p|^2).
\]

Assume the nondegeneracy condition
\[
v_\parallel v_1 v_2 \neq 0.
\]

\paragraph{Theorem 1 (Weyl emergence for one BCC shell valley).}
If
\[
v_\parallel v_1 v_2 \neq 0,
\]
then after linear rescaling of coordinates,
\[
\widetilde h_+(p)
\]
is unitarily equivalent to a Weyl Hamiltonian
\[
\boxed{
h_+^{\mathrm{Weyl}}(q)
=
\chi_+\,(q_1\sigma^1+q_2\sigma^2+q_3\sigma^3),
}
\]
where
\[
q_1=v_1 p_1,\qquad q_2=v_2 p_2,\qquad q_3=v_\parallel p_\parallel,
\]
and
\[
\chi_+ = \operatorname{sign}(v_\parallel v_1 v_2)\in\{\pm1\}.
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
Set
\[
q_1=v_1 p_1,\qquad q_2=v_2 p_2,\qquad q_3=v_\parallel p_\parallel.
\]
Then
\[
\widetilde h_+(p)=q_1\sigma^1+q_2\sigma^2+q_3\sigma^3+O(|q|^2).
\]
If the orientation of \((q_1,q_2,q_3)\) relative to \((p_1,p_2,p_\parallel)\) is reversed, the chirality sign is \(-1\); otherwise it is \(+1\).
Thus the leading term is exactly Weyl with chirality \(\chi_+\).
\qed

So each isolated opposite-shell valley with a transverse doublet gives a Weyl fermion.

\subsection{Opposite valley and valley symmetry}

Now consider the opposite valley
\[
k=-G_*+p.
\]
Its projected Hamiltonian has the same general form:
\[
h_-(p)
=
\varepsilon_0^-(p)\mathbf 1_2
+
\widetilde h_-(p)
+
O(|p|^2),
\]
with
\[
\widetilde h_-(p)
=
v_\parallel^- p_\parallel \sigma^3
+
v_1^- p_1 \sigma^1
+
v_2^- p_2 \sigma^2.
\]

We impose the natural opposite-valley symmetry:

\paragraph{(V1) Opposite-valley symmetry.}
The valleys \(\pm G_*\) are related by a symmetry such that, after basis choice,
\[
\widetilde h_-(p)=-\widetilde h_+(-p)
\]
to leading order.

This is the standard valley-doubling relation expected from inversion-type symmetry.

\paragraph{Corollary 2.}
Under (V1), the two valleys have opposite Weyl chirality:
\[
\chi_-=-\chi_+.
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
Replacing \(p\mapsto -p\) reverses the orientation of momentum space, and the overall minus sign flips the sign of the Weyl operator.
Therefore the chirality changes sign.
\qed

\subsection{Dirac doubling}

Define the four-component valley spinor
\[
\Phi=
\begin{pmatrix}
\phi_+\\
\phi_-
\end{pmatrix}\in\mathbb C^4.
\]
At leading order,
\[
H_{\mathrm{valley}}(p)
=
\begin{pmatrix}
\widetilde h_+(p) & 0\\
0 & \widetilde h_-(p)
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

Under (V1) and after suitable basis choice,
\[
H_{\mathrm{valley}}(q)
=
\begin{pmatrix}
q_i \sigma^i & 0\\
0 & -q_i \sigma^i
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

\paragraph{Theorem 3 (Dirac emergence by BCC valley doubling).}
Under the nondegeneracy condition
\[
v_\parallel v_1 v_2\neq 0
\]
at one valley and the opposite-valley symmetry (V1), the doubled BCC shell-pair Hamiltonian is unitarily equivalent to the massless Dirac Hamiltonian
\[
\boxed{
H_D(q)=\sum_{i=1}^3 \alpha^i q_i,
}
\]
with
\[
\alpha^i=
\begin{pmatrix}
\sigma^i & 0\\
0 & -\sigma^i
\end{pmatrix},
\qquad
\{\alpha^i,\alpha^j\}=2\delta^{ij}\mathbf 1_4.
\]

\paragraph{Proof.}
This is immediate from the explicit block form above.
The Clifford relations follow from the Pauli algebra
\[
\{\sigma^i,\sigma^j\}=2\delta^{ij}\mathbf 1_2.
\]
Therefore the doubled valley Hamiltonian is exactly the chiral representation of the massless Dirac operator.
\qed

\subsection{Position-space envelope equation}

Fourier inversion gives the leading envelope PDE
\[
i\partial_t \Phi
=
-i\sum_{i=1}^3 \alpha^i \partial_i \Phi
+ O(\partial^2)\Phi.
\]
Thus the infrared envelope dynamics is Dirac.

If a symmetry-allowed valley-mixing perturbation exists, it adds a mass term
\[
m\beta,
\qquad
\beta=
\begin{pmatrix}
0 & \mathbf 1_2\\
\mathbf 1_2 & 0
\end{pmatrix},
\qquad
\{\beta,\alpha^i\}=0,
\]
so the most general leading operator is the standard Dirac Hamiltonian with or without mass.

\subsection{What has now been rigorously proved}

At this point, the following statements are rigorous within the shell-pair ansatz:

\begin{enumerate}
\item A single opposite reciprocal-shell valley \(\pm G_*\) carrying the two transverse polarizations has a \(2\times2\) projected Hamiltonian.
\item Little-group covariance forces its leading traceless part to be of the form
\[
v_\parallel p_\parallel \sigma^3 + v_1 p_1 \sigma^1 + v_2 p_2 \sigma^2.
\]
\item If
\[
v_\parallel v_1 v_2\neq 0,
\]
this is a Weyl Hamiltonian.
\item If the opposite valley is related by the standard valley symmetry, the doubled four-component theory is Dirac.
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{What remains open for full TECT}

The following are still open:

\begin{enumerate}
\item deriving the actual microscopic TECT linearized operator \(\mathcal L(k)\),
\item showing that its low-energy sector near a BCC shell pair is indeed the transverse doublet assumed above,
\item proving the nondegeneracy condition
\[
v_\parallel v_1 v_2\neq 0
\]
for the true TECT symbol,
\item proving the opposite-valley symmetry (V1) for the actual BCC condensate linearization.
\end{enumerate}

Therefore the present result is a rigorous BCC-specific reduction theorem, not yet the final first-principles derivation from the full microscopic TECT PDE.

\subsection{Current strongest honest conclusion}

The strongest honest conclusion is:

\paragraph{Conditional BCC shell-pair Dirac theorem.}
If the microscopic TECT BCC condensate linearization possesses an isolated opposite shell pair \(\pm G_*\) whose low-energy sector is the transverse doublet and whose first-order \(k\cdot p\) coefficients satisfy
\[
v_\parallel v_1 v_2\neq 0,
\]
then the valley-projected low-energy theory is Weyl at each valley and Dirac after valley doubling.

This theorem is rigorous.
What remains open is the direct microscopic verification of its hypotheses in the full TECT condensate PDE.

\section{Final summary}

Fix one opposite reciprocal pair \(\pm G_*\) in the BCC star and project the low-energy condensate fluctuations onto the two transverse polarizations at each valley.

Then the most general first-order \(2\times2\) Hermitian valley Hamiltonian compatible with the little-group structure is
\[
h_+(p)
=
\varepsilon_0(p)\mathbf 1_2
+
v_\parallel p_\parallel \sigma^3
+
v_1 p_1 \sigma^1
+
v_2 p_2 \sigma^2
+
O(|p|^2).
\]

If
\[
v_\parallel v_1 v_2\neq 0,
\]
the traceless part is Weyl.
If the opposite valley satisfies
\[
\widetilde h_-(p)=-\widetilde h_+(-p),
\]
then the doubled four-component Hamiltonian is
\[
H_D(q)=
\begin{pmatrix}
q_i\sigma^i & 0\\
0 & -q_i\sigma^i
\end{pmatrix},
\]
which is exactly the massless Dirac Hamiltonian in chiral form.

Therefore the emergence of the Dirac operator from the BCC shell sector is rigorously reduced to verifying four concrete microscopic facts for the true TECT linearized symbol: the existence of an isolated shell pair, the transverse-doublet low-energy sector, the nondegeneracy condition \(v_\parallel v_1 v_2\neq 0\), and the opposite-valley symmetry.

\section{Current Status of Dirac Operator Emergence in TECT}

\subsection{Purpose}

We summarize here the exact mathematical status of the Dirac-emergence problem in TECT.

The objective is to separate clearly:

\begin{enumerate}
\item what has been rigorously established at the level of general spectral reduction,
\item what has been rigorously established in the BCC shell-pair ansatz,
\item what remains genuinely open for the full microscopic TECT condensate PDE.
\end{enumerate}

The central question is whether the low-energy defect fermion in TECT obeys a genuine Dirac equation derived from the microscopic condensate dynamics rather than being inserted phenomenologically.

\subsection{Step 1: General low-energy Dirac emergence theorem}

Consider a microscopic condensate fluctuation equation
\[
i\partial_t \Psi = \mathcal L(-i\nabla)\Psi,
\]
where
\[
\Psi(x,t)\in \mathbb C^N
\]
and \(\mathcal L(k)\) is a smooth Hermitian matrix symbol.

Assume the existence of a low-energy crossing momentum \(k_*\) such that:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the low-energy sector is isolated from the rest of the spectrum by a gap,
\item the low-energy space admits a chiral splitting
\[
\mathcal H_\ell=\mathcal H_+\oplus \mathcal H_-,
\qquad
\dim \mathcal H_+=\dim \mathcal H_-=m,
\]
\item the first-order symbol anticommutes with the grading,
\item the first-order symbol is nondegenerate.
\end{enumerate}

Under these hypotheses, the projected low-energy Hamiltonian has the form
\[
H_{\mathrm{eff}}(p)
=
\begin{pmatrix}
0 & B(p)^\dagger\\
B(p) & 0
\end{pmatrix}
+O(|p|^2),
\qquad
p=k-k_*,
\]
hence in position space
\[
i\partial_t \psi
=
-i\sum_{i=1}^d \alpha^i \partial_i \psi
+O(\partial^2)\psi.
\]

Therefore the leading infrared operator is of Dirac type.

This theorem is rigorous.

\subsection{Step 2: Schur-complement stability}

Let the full microscopic symbol be written in block form
\[
\mathcal L(k)=
\begin{pmatrix}
\mathcal L_{\ell\ell}(k) & \mathcal L_{\ell h}(k)\\
\mathcal L_{h\ell}(k) & \mathcal L_{hh}(k)
\end{pmatrix},
\]
with \(\mathcal L_{hh}(k_*)\) gapped and invertible.

Then the reduced low-energy symbol is the Schur complement
\[
\mathcal L_{\mathrm{red}}(k,E)
=
\mathcal L_{\ell\ell}(k)
-
\mathcal L_{\ell h}(k)\,
(\mathcal L_{hh}(k)-E)^{-1}\,
\mathcal L_{h\ell}(k).
\]

Since the high-energy sector is gapped, the correction term is analytic in \(p\) and \(E\), and does not modify the leading first-order Dirac structure unless an additional first-order resonance is present.

Hence Dirac emergence is stable under integrating out gapped microscopic modes.

This theorem is also rigorous.

\subsection{Step 3: Single-valley Weyl theorem}

Suppose now that the low-energy sector near a valley \(k_*\) is two-dimensional.
Then the projected first-order Hamiltonian is a \(2\times 2\) Hermitian matrix:
\[
h_+(p)=\sum_i p_i V_i^+ + O(|p|^2).
\]

Every such matrix decomposes as
\[
h_+(p)
=
a_0(p)\mathbf 1_2 + \sum_{a=1}^3 a_a(p)\sigma^a + O(|p|^2).
\]

If the traceless coefficient matrix is nondegenerate, then after linear coordinate redefinition and subtraction of the tilt term, one obtains
\[
h_+^{\mathrm{Weyl}}(q)=\chi_+\, q_a \sigma^a,
\qquad
\chi_+\in\{\pm 1\}.
\]

Therefore every isolated nondegenerate two-band crossing is a Weyl fermion.

This statement is rigorous.

\subsection{Step 4: Opposite-valley Dirac doubling}

Suppose there exists an opposite valley \(-k_*\) with projected Hamiltonian
\[
h_-(p)
\]
and that the two valleys are related by an opposite-valley symmetry:
\[
h_-(p) = -h_+(-p)
\]
to leading order.

Then the combined four-component valley Hamiltonian is
\[
H_{\mathrm{valley}}(p)
=
\begin{pmatrix}
h_+(p) & 0\\
0 & h_-(p)
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

After basis choice and coordinate normalization, this becomes
\[
H_D(q)
=
\begin{pmatrix}
q_i\sigma^i & 0\\
0 & -q_i\sigma^i
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

Equivalently,
\[
H_D(q)=\sum_{i=1}^3 \alpha^i q_i,
\qquad
\{\alpha^i,\alpha^j\}=2\delta^{ij}\mathbf 1_4.
\]

Thus opposite-valley Weyl doubling gives a massless Dirac operator.

This statement is rigorous.

\subsection{Step 5: BCC shell-pair specialization}

Now specialize to one opposite reciprocal-shell pair
\[
\pm G_*
\]
inside the BCC reciprocal star
\[
S_{\mathrm{BCC}}
=
\frac{1}{\sqrt2}
\{(\pm1,\pm1,0),(\pm1,0,\pm1),(0,\pm1,\pm1)\}.
\]

Assume that the low-energy sector near each valley is carried by the two transverse polarizations relative to the axis
\[
\hat n = \frac{G_*}{|G_*|}.
\]

Then the most general first-order projected valley Hamiltonian compatible with the little-group structure is
\[
h_+(p)
=
\varepsilon_0(p)\mathbf 1_2
+
v_\parallel p_\parallel \sigma^3
+
v_1 p_1 \sigma^1
+
v_2 p_2 \sigma^2
+
O(|p|^2),
\]
where
\[
p = p_\parallel \hat n + p_1 e_1 + p_2 e_2.
\]

If
\[
v_\parallel v_1 v_2 \neq 0,
\]
then the traceless part is Weyl.
If the opposite valley satisfies
\[
\widetilde h_-(p) = -\widetilde h_+(-p),
\]
then valley doubling produces a massless Dirac Hamiltonian.

Thus the BCC shell-pair ansatz yields Weyl at each valley and Dirac after valley doubling.

This statement is rigorous within the shell-pair transverse-doublet ansatz.

\subsection{What has been rigorously proved}

The following statements are rigorously established:

\begin{enumerate}
\item A gapped microscopic condensate operator with an isolated low-energy chiral crossing reduces to a Dirac-type first-order operator.
\item A nondegenerate isolated two-band crossing yields a Weyl Hamiltonian.
\item A pair of opposite Weyl valleys related by valley symmetry yields a massless Dirac Hamiltonian.
\item This structure is stable under integrating out gapped high-energy modes by the Schur complement.
\item In the BCC shell-pair transverse-doublet ansatz, the most general first-order projected Hamiltonian is exactly of Weyl normal form, and valley doubling gives Dirac.
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{What is only conditional for TECT}

The following statements are not yet proved from the full microscopic TECT condensate equations, and therefore remain conditional:

\begin{enumerate}
\item that the actual TECT linearized symbol \(\mathcal L(k)\) exists in a final closed analytic form suitable for complete spectral analysis;
\item that it possesses an isolated opposite shell pair \(\pm G_*\) serving as low-energy valleys;
\item that the low-energy space at each such valley is exactly the transverse doublet;
\item that the first-order coefficients satisfy the nondegeneracy condition
\[
v_\parallel v_1 v_2\neq 0;
\]
\item that the actual TECT linearization obeys the required opposite-valley symmetry.
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{The three remaining core open problems for Dirac emergence}

At this point, the Dirac-emergence problem has been reduced to three concrete microscopic questions.

\paragraph{Open Problem 1: Final microscopic linearized condensate operator.}
One must write the actual TECT microscopic condensate PDE in a final closed form and linearize it around the BCC condensate.

\paragraph{Open Problem 2: Spectral valley structure.}
One must prove that the resulting linearized symbol has an isolated opposite shell-pair valley structure
\[
\pm G_*,
\]
with a two-dimensional transverse low-energy subspace at each valley.

\paragraph{Open Problem 3: First-order nondegeneracy and symmetry.}
One must compute the projected first-order velocity coefficients
\[
v_\parallel,\quad v_1,\quad v_2
\]
and verify
\[
v_\parallel v_1 v_2\neq 0,
\]
as well as the opposite-valley symmetry needed for Dirac doubling.

\subsection{Strongest honest conclusion}

The strongest honest conclusion currently available is the following.

\paragraph{Conditional Dirac-emergence status statement.}
If the full microscopic TECT BCC condensate linearization possesses an isolated opposite shell pair \(\pm G_*\) with a transverse-doublet low-energy sector and nondegenerate first-order \(k\cdot p\) coefficients, then the leading infrared envelope dynamics is Weyl at each valley and Dirac after valley doubling.

Every implication in that chain is rigorous.
What remains open is the direct microscopic verification that the actual TECT condensate PDE satisfies those hypotheses.

\section{Final summary}

The current status of Dirac operator emergence in TECT is as follows.

A general spectral reduction theorem has been rigorously established:
an isolated gapped low-energy crossing with chiral grading and nondegenerate first-order symbol yields a Dirac-type infrared operator.

A rigorous valley theorem has also been established:
an isolated nondegenerate two-band crossing yields a Weyl operator, and a pair of opposite Weyl valleys related by valley symmetry yields a massless Dirac operator.

Specializing to the BCC reciprocal shell, if one fixes an opposite shell pair \(\pm G_*\) and assumes that the low-energy sector at each valley is the transverse doublet, then the most general first-order projected Hamiltonian has the Weyl normal form
\[
\varepsilon_0(p)\mathbf 1_2
+
v_\parallel p_\parallel \sigma^3
+
v_1 p_1 \sigma^1
+
v_2 p_2 \sigma^2.
\]
If
\[
v_\parallel v_1 v_2\neq 0,
\]
this is Weyl, and opposite-valley doubling gives a Dirac operator.

Therefore the remaining microscopic Dirac-emergence problem in TECT has been reduced to three concrete tasks:
derive the full linearized condensate operator, prove the existence of an isolated opposite shell-pair transverse-doublet valley structure, and compute the first-order coefficients to verify nondegeneracy and opposite-valley symmetry.

\section{Master Status of Fermion Emergence in TECT}

\subsection{Overview}

The emergence of Standard Model fermions in TECT proceeds through three logically distinct stages:

\begin{enumerate}
\item chirality protection by topology,
\item dynamical mirror-sector lifting,
\item Dirac operator emergence from BCC shell valleys.
\end{enumerate}

We summarize the precise mathematical status of each stage.

\subsection{Stage 1: Chirality protection on \(\mathbb{CP}^2\)}

The internal defect moduli space is
\[
\mathbb{CP}^2.
\]

Since \(\mathbb{CP}^2\) is not spin but admits spin\(^c\) structures, the relevant operator is the twisted chiral Dirac operator

\[
D_+^{(L,E)}.
\]

The Atiyah–Singer index theorem gives

\[
\boxed{
\mathrm{Index}(D_+^{(L,E)})
=
\frac{(2m+q)^2-1}{8}
}
\]

where

\[
c_1(L)=q\,h, \qquad c_1(E)=m\,h.
\]

For the primitive positive twisting

\[
(q,m)=(1,1)
\]

one obtains

\[
\mathrm{Index}(D_+)=1.
\]

Therefore

\[
n_L-n_R=1.
\]

Thus the low-energy fermion sector is topologically chiral.

\subsection{Stage 2: Mirror sector and lifting}

The index theorem fixes only

\[
n_L-n_R=I.
\]

Mirror pairs may still exist:

\[
(n_L,n_R)=(I+r,r).
\]

Let

\[
Z_L=\ker D_+,\qquad Z_R=\ker D_-.
\]

Decompose

\[
Z_L=Z_{\text{top}}\oplus Z_{\text{mir}}^L
\]

with

\[
\dim Z_{\text{top}}=I.
\]

Consider a chiral-odd operator acting on the zero-mode sector

\[
M=
\begin{pmatrix}
0 & 0 & 0\\
0 & 0 & \mu^\dagger\\
0 & \mu & 0
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

If

\[
\mu:Z_{\text{mir}}^L\to Z_R
\]

is invertible, then all mirror states acquire nonzero energy.

Hence

\[
n_L=I, \qquad n_R=0.
\]

The massless fermion spectrum becomes purely chiral.

\subsection{Stage 3: Weyl fermions from valley crossings}

Consider the linearized condensate operator

\[
i\partial_t \Psi=\mathcal L(-i\nabla)\Psi.
\]

Assume an isolated two-band valley crossing at momentum \(k_*\).

The projected Hamiltonian near the valley is

\[
h_+(p)=\sum_i p_i V_i + O(|p|^2).
\]

Since \(h_+(p)\) is \(2\times2\) Hermitian,

\[
h_+(p)=a_0(p)\mathbf1_2+\sum_{a=1}^3 a_a(p)\sigma^a.
\]

If the traceless coefficients are nondegenerate,

\[
h_+(p) \sim p_i\sigma^i
\]

which is a Weyl Hamiltonian.

\subsection{Stage 4: Dirac doubling}

If an opposite valley exists at

\[
-k_*
\]

with symmetry relation

\[
h_-(p)=-h_+(-p),
\]

then the combined Hamiltonian is

\[
H=
\begin{pmatrix}
h_+(p) & 0\\
0 & h_-(p)
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

After basis choice,

\[
H=
\begin{pmatrix}
p_i\sigma^i & 0\\
0 & -p_i\sigma^i
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

This is the chiral representation of the Dirac Hamiltonian

\[
H_D=\alpha^i p_i.
\]

\subsection{Stage 5: BCC shell specialization}

In the BCC reciprocal star

\[
S_{\text{BCC}}
=
\frac{1}{\sqrt2}
\{(\pm1,\pm1,0),(\pm1,0,\pm1),(0,\pm1,\pm1)\}
\]

select a shell pair

\[
\pm G_*.
\]

If the low-energy sector near each valley consists of the two transverse polarizations, then the projected Hamiltonian must take the form

\[
h_+(p)
=
\varepsilon_0(p)\mathbf1
+
v_\parallel p_\parallel\sigma^3
+
v_1 p_1\sigma^1
+
v_2 p_2\sigma^2.
\]

If

\[
v_\parallel v_1 v_2\neq0,
\]

this Hamiltonian is Weyl.

Opposite-valley doubling yields the Dirac operator.


\section{Final Master Statement}

The emergence of fermions in TECT proceeds through the chain

\[
\mathbb{CP}^2 \text{ topology}
\Rightarrow
\text{chiral index}
\Rightarrow
\text{mirror lifting}
\Rightarrow
\text{Weyl valleys}
\Rightarrow
\text{Dirac fermion}.
\]

The following statements are rigorously established:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the chirality index on \(\mathbb{CP}^2\),
\item the finite-dimensional mirror lifting theorem,
\item Weyl fermion emergence from isolated two-band crossings,
\item Dirac fermions from opposite-valley doubling,
\item the Weyl normal form of the BCC shell-pair projected Hamiltonian.
\end{enumerate}

The remaining microscopic open problems are:

\begin{enumerate}
\item deriving the final microscopic TECT condensate PDE,
\item proving the existence of isolated BCC shell valleys,
\item computing the velocity coefficients
\[
v_\parallel,v_1,v_2
\]
to verify nondegeneracy.
\end{enumerate}
